She walked quickly\r\nto the familiar church, and reached it just as the doors were being\r\nclosed.\r\n\r\nOf all the congregation she probably was the one who went through the\r\nservice most mechanically. Not a word reached her understanding.\r\nSitting, standing, or on her knees, she wore the same preoccupied look,\r\nwith ever and again a slight smile or a movement of the lips, as if she\r\nwere recalling some conversation of special interest.\r\n\r\nLast Sunday she had had an adventure, the first of any real moment that\r\nhad befallen her in London. She had arranged to go with Miss Eade on a\r\nsteamboat up the river. They were to meet at the Battersea Park\r\nlanding-stage at half-past two. But Miss Eade did not keep her\r\nappointment, and Monica, unwilling to lose the trip, started alone.\r\n\r\nShe disembarked at Richmond and strayed about for an hour or two, then\r\nhad a cup of tea and a bun. As it was still far too early to return,\r\nshe went down to the riverside and seated herself on one of the\r\nbenches. Many boats were going by, a majority of them containing only\r\ntwo persons\xe2\x80\x94a young man who pulled, and a girl who held the strings of\r\nthe tiller. Some of these couples Monica disregarded; but occasionally\r\nthere passed a skiff from which she could not take her eyes. To lie\r\nback like that on the cushions and converse with a companion who had\r\nnothing of the _shop_ about him!\r\n\r\nIt seemed hard that she must be alone. Poor Mr. Bullivant would gladly\r\nhave taken her on the river; but Mr. Bullivant\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nShe thought of her sisters. Their loneliness was for life, poor things.\r\nAlready they were old; and they would grow older, sadder, perpetually\r\nstruggling to supplement that dividend from the precious capital\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nmerely that they might keep alive. Oh!\xe2\x80\x94her heart ached at the misery\r\nof such a prospect. How much better if the poor girls had never been\r\nborn.\r\n\r\nHer own future was more hopeful than theirs had ever been. She knew\r\nherself good-looking. Men had followed her in the street and tried to\r\nmake her acquaintance. Some of the girls with whom she lived regarded\r\nher enviously, spitefully. But had she really the least chance of\r\nmarrying a man whom she could respect\xe2\x80\x94not to say love?\r\n\r\nOne-and-twenty a week hence. At Weston she had kept tolerable health,\r\nbut certainly her constitution was not strong, and the slavery of\r\nWalworth Road threatened her with premature decay. Her sisters\r\ncounselled wisely. Coming to London was a mistake. She would have had\r\nbetter chances at Weston, notwithstanding the extreme discretion with\r\nwhich she was obliged to conduct herself.\r\n\r\nWhile she mused thus, a profound discouragement settling on her sweet\r\nface, some one took a seat by her\xe2\x80\x94on the same bench, that is to say.\r\nGlancing aside, she saw that it was an oldish man, with grizzled\r\nwhiskers and rather a stern visage. Monica sighed.\r\n\r\nWas it possible that he had heard her? He looked this way, and with\r\ncuriosity. Ashamed of herself, she kept her eyes averted for a long\r\ntime. Presently, following the movement of a boat, her face turned\r\nunconsciously towards the silent companion; again he was looking at\r\nher, and he spoke. The gravity of his appearance and manner, the\r\ngood-natured commonplace that fell from his lips, could not alarm her;\r\na dialogue began, and went on for about half an hour.\r\n\r\nHow old might he be? After all, he was probably not fifty\xe2\x80\x94perchance\r\nnot much more than forty. His utterance fell short of perfect\r\nrefinement, but seemed that of an educated man. And certainly his\r\nclothes were such as a gentleman wears. He had thin, hairy hands,\r\nunmarked by any effect of labour; the nails could not have been better\r\ncared for. Was it a bad sign that he carried neither gloves nor\r\nwalking-stick?\r\n\r\nHis talk aimed at nothing but sober friendliness; it was perfectly\r\ninoffensive\xe2\x80\x94indeed, respectful. Now and then\xe2\x80\x94not too often\xe2\x80\x94he fixed\r\nhis eyes upon her for an instant. After the introductory phrases, he\r\nmentioned that he had had a long drive, alone; his horse was baiting in\r\npreparation for the journey back to London. He often took such drives\r\nin the summer, though generally on a weekday; the magnificent sky had\r\ntempted him out this morning. He lived at Herne Hill.\r\n\r\nAt length he ventured a question. Monica affected no reluctance to tell\r\nhim that she was in a house of business, that she had relatives in\r\nLondon, that only by chance she found herself alone to-day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be sorry if I never saw you again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese words he uttered with embarrassment, his eyes on the ground.\r\nMonica could only keep silence. Half an hour ago she would not have\r\nthought it possible for any remark of this man\xe2\x80\x99s seriously to occupy\r\nher mind, yet now she waited for the next sentence in discomposure\r\nwhich was quite free from resentment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe meet in this casual way, and talk, and then say good-bye. Why\r\nmayn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you that you interest me very much, and that I am afraid\r\nto trust only to chance for another meeting? If you were a man\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nsmiled\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI should give you my card, and ask you to my house. The card I\r\nmay at all events offer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhilst speaking, he drew out a little case, and laid a visiting-card on\r\nthe bench within Monica\xe2\x80\x99s reach. Murmuring her \xe2\x80\x9cthank you,\xe2\x80\x9d she took\r\nthe bit of pasteboard, but did not look at it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are on my side of the river,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, still with scrupulous\r\nmodesty of tone. \xe2\x80\x9cMay I not hope to see you some day, when you are\r\nwalking? All days and times are the same to me; but I am afraid it is\r\nonly on Sunday that you are at leisure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, only on a Sunday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt took a long time, and many circumlocutions, but in the end an\r\nappointment was made. Monica would see her acquaintance next Sunday\r\nevening on the river front of Battersea Park; if it rained, then the\r\nSunday after. She was ashamed and confused. Other girls were constantly\r\ndoing this kind of thing\xe2\x80\x94other girls in business; but it seemed to put\r\nher on the level of a servant. And why had she consented? The man could\r\nnever be anything to her; he was too old, too hard-featured, too grave.\r\nWell, on that very account there would be no harm in meeting him. In\r\ntruth, she had not felt the courage to refuse; in a manner he had\r\noverawed her.\r\n\r\nAnd perhaps she would not keep the engagement. Nothing compelled her.\r\nShe had not told him her name, nor the house where she was employed.\r\nThere was a week to think it over.\r\n\r\nAll days and times were the same to him\xe2\x80\x94he said. And he drove about\r\nthe country for his pleasure. A man of means. His name, according to\r\nthe card, was Edmund Widdowson.\r\n\r\nHe was upright in his walk, and strongly built. She noticed this as he\r\nmoved away from her. Fearful lest he should turn round, her eyes\r\nglanced at his figure from moment to moment. But he did not once look\r\nback.\r\n\r\n* * * * * * * * * *\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now to God the Father.\xe2\x80\x9d The bustle throughout the church wakened\r\nher from reverie so complete that she knew not a syllable of the\r\nsermon. After all she must deceive her sisters by inventing a text, and\r\nperhaps a comment.\r\n\r\nBy an arrangement with Mrs. Conisbee, dinner was down in the parlour\r\nto-day. A luxurious meal, moreover; for in her excitement Virginia had\r\nresolved to make a feast of Monica\xe2\x80\x99s birthday. There was a tiny piece\r\nof salmon, a dainty cutlet, and a cold blackcurrant tart. Virginia, at\r\nhome a constant vegetarian, took no share of the fish and meat\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nwas only enough for one person. Alice, alone upstairs, made a dinner of\r\ngruel.\r\n\r\nMonica was to be at Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road, Chelsea, by three o\xe2\x80\x99clock. The\r\nsisters hoped she would return to Lavender Hill with her news, but that\r\nwas left uncertain\xe2\x80\x94by Monica herself purposely. As an amusement, she\r\nhad decided to keep her promise to Mr. Edmund Widdowson. She was\r\ncurious to see him again, and receive a new impression of his\r\npersonality. If he behaved as inoffensively as at Richmond,\r\nacquaintance with him might be continued for the variety it brought\r\ninto her life. If anything unpleasant happened, she had only to walk\r\naway. The slight, very slight, tremor of anticipation was reasonably to\r\nbe prized by a shop-girl at Messrs. Scotcher\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nDrawing near to Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road\xe2\x80\x94the wrapped-up Keble in her hand\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nbegan to wonder whether Miss Nunn would have any serious proposal to\r\noffer. Virginia\xe2\x80\x99s report and ecstatic forecasts were, she knew, not\r\ncompletely trustworthy; though more than ten years her sister\xe2\x80\x99s junior,\r\nMonica saw the world with eyes much less disposed to magnify and colour\r\nordinary facts.\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot was still from home. Rhoda Nunn received the visitor in a\r\npleasant, old-fashioned drawing-room, where there was nothing costly,\r\nnothing luxurious; yet to Monica it appeared richly furnished. A sense\r\nof strangeness amid such surroundings had more to do with her\r\nconstrained silence for the first few minutes than the difficulty with\r\nwhich she recognized in this lady before her the Miss Nunn whom she had\r\nknown years ago.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should never have known you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, equally surprised. \xe2\x80\x9cFor\r\none thing, you look like a fever patient just recovering. What can be\r\nexpected? Your sister gave me a shocking account of how you live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe work is very hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPreposterous. Why do you stay at such a place, Monica?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am getting experience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be used in the next world?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Madden is better to-day, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice? Not much, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell me something more about the \xe2\x80\x9cexperience\xe2\x80\x9d you are\r\ngetting? For instance, what time is given you for meals?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda Nunn was not the person to manufacture light gossip when a matter\r\nof the gravest interest waited for discussion. With a face that\r\nexpressed thoughtful sympathy, she encouraged the girl to speak and\r\nconfide in her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s twenty minutes for each meal,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica explained; \xe2\x80\x9cbut at\r\ndinner and tea one is very likely to be called into the shop before\r\nfinishing. If you are long away you find the table cleared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharming arrangement! No sitting down behind the counter, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course not. We suffer a great deal from that. Some of us get\r\ndiseases. A girl has just gone to the hospital with varicose veins, and\r\ntwo or three others have the same thing in a less troublesome form.\r\nSometimes, on Saturday night, I lose all feeling in my feet; I have to\r\nstamp on the floor to be sure it\xe2\x80\x99s still under me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that Saturday night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s bad enough now; but at Christmas! There was a week or more\r\nof Saturday night\xe2\x80\x94going on to one o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning. A girl by me\r\nwas twice carried out fainting, one night after another. They gave her\r\nbrandy, and she came back again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey compelled her to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no, it was her own wish. Her \xe2\x80\x9cbook of takings\xe2\x80\x9d wasn\xe2\x80\x99t very good,\r\npoor thing, and if it didn\xe2\x80\x99t come up to a certain figure at the end of\r\nthe week she would lose her place. She lost it after all. They told her\r\nshe was too weak. After Christmas she was lucky enough to get a place\r\nas a lady\xe2\x80\x99s-maid at twenty-five pounds a year\xe2\x80\x94at Scotcher\xe2\x80\x99s she had\r\nfifteen. But we heard that she burst a blood-vessel, and now she\xe2\x80\x99s in\r\nthe hospital at Brompton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelightful story! Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you an early-closing day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey had before I went there; but only for about three months. Then\r\nthe agreement broke down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike the assistants. A pity the establishment doesn\xe2\x80\x99t follow suit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say so, Miss Nunn, if you knew how terribly hard it\r\nis for many girls to find a place, even now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it perfectly well. And I wish it were harder. I wish girls fell\r\ndown and died of hunger in the streets, instead of creeping to their\r\ngarrets and the hospitals. I should like to see their dead bodies\r\ncollected together in some open place for the crowd to stare at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica gazed at her with wide eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean, I suppose, that people would try to reform things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho knows? Perhaps they might only congratulate each other that a few\r\nof the superfluous females had been struck off. Do they give you any\r\nsummer holiday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA week, with salary continued.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally? With salary continued? That takes one\xe2\x80\x99s breath away.\xe2\x80\x94Are many\r\nof the girls ladies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone, at Scotcher\xe2\x80\x99s. They nearly all come from the country. Several\r\nare daughters of small farmers and those are dreadfully ignorant. One\r\nof them asked me the other day in what country Africa was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t find them very pleasant company?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne or two are nice quiet girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda drew a deep sigh, and moved with impatience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think you\xe2\x80\x99ve had about enough of it\xe2\x80\x94experience and\r\nall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might go into a country business: it would be easier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you don\xe2\x80\x99t care for the thought?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish now they had brought me up to something different. Alice and\r\nVirginia were afraid of having me trained for a school; you remember\r\nthat one of our sisters who went through it died of overwork. And I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nnot clever, Miss Nunn. I never did much at school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda regarded her, smiling gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have no inclination to study now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid not,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the other, looking away. \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I should\r\nlike to be better educated, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I could study seriously,\r\nto earn my living by it. The time for that has gone by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps so. But there are things you might manage. No doubt your\r\nsister told you how I get my living. There\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal of employment\r\nfor women who learn to use a typewriter. Did you ever have piano\r\nlessons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo more did I, and I was sorry for it when I went to typewriting. The\r\nfingers have to be light and supple and quick. Come with me, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nshow you one of the machines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went to a room downstairs\xe2\x80\x94a bare little room by the library. Here\r\nwere two Remingtons, and Rhoda patiently explained their use.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne must practise until one can do fifty words a minute at least. I\r\nknow one or two people who have reached almost twice that speed. It\r\ntakes a good six months\xe2\x80\x99 work to learn for any profitable use. Miss\r\nBarfoot takes pupils.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica, at first very attentive, was growing absent. Her eyes wandered\r\nabout the room. The other observed her closely, and, it seemed,\r\ndoubtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you feel any impulse to try for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have to live for six months without earning anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is by no means impossible for you, I think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot really impossible,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica replied with hesitation.\r\n\r\nSomething like dissatisfaction passed over Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s face, though she\r\ndid not allow Monica to see it. Her lips moved in a way that perhaps\r\nsignified disdain for such timidity. Tolerance was not one of the\r\nvirtues expressed in her physiognomy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go back to the drawing-room and have some tea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica could not become quite at ease. This energetic woman had little\r\nattraction for her. She saw the characteristics which made Virginia\r\nenthusiastic, but feared rather than admired them. To put herself in\r\nMiss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s hands might possibly result in a worse form of bondage than\r\nshe suffered at the shop; she would never be able to please such a\r\nperson, and failure, she imagined, would result in more or less\r\ncontemptuous dismissal.\r\n\r\nThen of a sudden, as it she had divined these thoughts, Rhoda assumed\r\nan air of gaiety of frank kindness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is your birthday? I no longer keep count of mine, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntell you without a calculation what I am exactly. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\r\nyou see. Thirty-one or fifty-one is much the same for a woman who has\r\nmade up her mind to live alone and work steadily for a definite object.\r\nBut you are still a young girl, Monica. My best wishes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica emboldened herself to ask what the object was for which her\r\nfriend worked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow shall I put it?\xe2\x80\x9d replied the other, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cTo make women\r\nhard-hearted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHard-hearted? I think I understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that you like to see them live unmarried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda laughed merrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say that almost with resentment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94indeed\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t intend it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica reddened a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing more natural if you have done. At your age, I should have\r\nresented it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d the girl hesitated\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you approve of any one marrying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not so severe! But do you know that there are half a million\r\nmore women than men in this happy country of ours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf a million!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer naive alarm again excited Rhoda to laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething like that, they say. So many _odd_ women\xe2\x80\x94no making a pair\r\nwith them. The pessimists call them useless, lost, futile lives. I,\r\nnaturally\xe2\x80\x94being one of them myself\xe2\x80\x94take another view. I look upon\r\nthem as a great reserve. When one woman vanishes in matrimony, the\r\nreserve offers a substitute for the world\xe2\x80\x99s work. True, they are not\r\nall trained yet\xe2\x80\x94far from it. I want to help in that\xe2\x80\x94to train the\r\nreserve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut married woman are not idle,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Monica earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot all of them. Some cook and rock cradles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s mood changed. She laughed the subject away, and\r\nabruptly began to talk of old days down in Somerset, of rambles about\r\nCheddar Cliffs, or at Glastonbury, or on the Quantocks. Monica,\r\nhowever, could not listen, and with difficulty commanded her face to a\r\npleasant smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you come and see Miss Barfoot?\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda asked, when it had become\r\nclear to her that the girl would gladly get away. \xe2\x80\x9cI am only her\r\nsubordinate, but I know she will wish to be of all the use to you she\r\ncan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica expressed her thanks, and promised to act as soon as possible on\r\nany invitation that was sent her. She took leave just as the servant\r\nannounced another caller.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\nTHE CASUAL ACQUAINTANCE\r\n\r\n\r\nAt that corner of Battersea Park which is near Albert Bridge there has\r\nlain for more than twenty years a curious collection of architectural\r\nfragments, chiefly dismembered columns, spread in order upon the\r\nground, and looking like portions of a razed temple. It is the\r\ncolonnade of old Burlington House, conveyed hither from Piccadilly who\r\nknows why, and likely to rest here, the sporting ground for adventurous\r\ninfants, until its origin is lost in the abyss of time.\r\n\r\nIt was at this spot that Monica had agreed to meet with her casual\r\nacquaintance, Edmund Widdowson, and there, from a distance, she saw his\r\nlank, upright, well-dressed figure moving backwards and forwards upon\r\nthe grass. Even at the last moment Monica doubted whether to approach.\r\nEmotional interest in him she had none, and the knowledge of life she\r\nhad gained in London assured her that in thus encouraging a perfect\r\nstranger she was doing a very hazardous thing. But the evening must\r\nsomehow be spent, and if she went off in another direction it would\r\nonly be to wander about with an adventurous mind; for her conversation\r\nwith Miss Nunn had had precisely the opposite effect of that which\r\nRhoda doubtless intended; she felt something of the recklessness which\r\nformerly excited her wonder when she remarked it in the other\r\nshop-girls. She could no longer be without a male companion, and as she\r\nhad given her promise to this man\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nHe had seen her, and was coming forward. To-day he carried a\r\nwalking-stick, and wore gloves; otherwise his appearance was the same\r\nas at Richmond. At the distance of a few yards he raised his hat, not\r\nvery gracefully. Monica did not offer her hand, nor did Widdowson seem\r\nto expect it. But he gave proof of an intense pleasure in the meeting;\r\nhis sallow cheeks grew warm, and in the many wrinkles about his eyes\r\nplayed a singular smile, good-natured but anxious, apprehensive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad you were able to come,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a low voice, bending\r\ntowards her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has been even finer than last Sunday,\xe2\x80\x9d was Monica\xe2\x80\x99s rather vague\r\nreply, as she glanced at some people who were passing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a wonderful day. But I only left home an hour ago. Shall we walk\r\nthis way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went along the path by the river. Widdowson exhibited none of the\r\nartifices of gallantry practised by men who are in the habit of picking\r\nup an acquaintance with shop-girls. His smile did not return; an\r\nextreme sobriety characterized his manner and speech; for the most part\r\nhe kept his eyes on the ground, and when silent he had the look of one\r\nwho inwardly debates a grave question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you been into the country?\xe2\x80\x9d was one of his first inquiries.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I spent the morning with my sisters, and in the afternoon I had to\r\nsee a lady in Chelsea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour sisters are older than yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, some years older.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it long since you went to live apart from them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have never had a home of our own since I was quite a child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s hesitation, she went on to give a brief account\r\nof her history. Widdowson listened with the closest attention, his lips\r\ntwitching now and then, his eyes half closed. But for cheek-bones that\r\nwere too prominent and nostrils rather too large, he was not\r\nill-featured. No particular force of character declared itself in his\r\ncountenance, and his mode of speech did not suggest a very active\r\nbrain. Speculating again about his age, Monica concluded that he must\r\nbe two or three and forty, in spite of the fact that his grizzled beard\r\nargued for a higher figure. He had brown hair untouched by any sign of\r\nadvanced life, his teeth were white and regular, and something\xe2\x80\x94she\r\ncould not make clear to her mind exactly what\xe2\x80\x94convinced her that he\r\nhad a right to judge himself comparatively young.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI supposed you were not a Londoner,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, when she came to a pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour speech. Not,\xe2\x80\x9d he added quickly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you have any provincial\r\naccent. And even if you had been a Londoner you would not have shown it\r\nin that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seemed to be reproving himself for a blunder, and after a short\r\nsilence asked in a tone of kindness,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you prefer the town?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn some ways\xe2\x80\x94not in all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad you have relatives here, and friends. So many young ladies\r\ncome up from the country who are quite alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, many.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheir progress to familiarity could hardly have been slower. Now and\r\nthen they spoke with a formal coldness which threatened absolute\r\nsilence. Monica\xe2\x80\x99s brain was so actively at work that she lost\r\nconsciousness of the people who were moving about them, and at times\r\nher companion was scarcely more to her than a voice.\r\n\r\nThey had walked along the whole front of the park, and were near\r\nChelsea Bridge. Widdowson gazed at the pleasure-boats lying below on\r\nthe strand, and said diffidently,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you care to go on the river?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe proposal was so unexpected that Monica looked up with a startled\r\nair. She had not thought of the man as likely to offer any kind of\r\namusement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be pleasant, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cThe tide is still running\r\nup. We might go very quietly for a mile or two, and be back as soon as\r\nyou like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I should like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe brightened up, and moved with a livelier step. In a few minutes they\r\nhad chosen their boat, had pushed off, and were gliding to the middle\r\nof the broad water. Widdowson managed the sculls without awkwardness,\r\nbut by no means like a man well trained in this form of exercise. On\r\nsitting down, he had taken off his hat, stowed it away, and put on a\r\nlittle travelling-cap, which he drew from his pocket. Monica thought\r\nthis became him. After all, he was not a companion to be ashamed of.\r\nShe looked with pleasure at his white hairy hands with their firm grip;\r\nthen at his boots\xe2\x80\x94very good boots indeed. He had gold links in his\r\nwhite shirt-cuffs, and a gold watch-guard chosen with a gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntaste.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am at your service,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with an approach to gaiety. \xe2\x80\x9cDirect\r\nme. Shall we go quickly\xe2\x80\x94some distance, or only just a little quicker\r\nthan the tide would float us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich you like. To row much would make you too hot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would like to go some distance\xe2\x80\x94I see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no. Do exactly what you like. Of course we must be back in an hour\r\nor two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew out his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s now ten minutes past six, and there is daylight till nine or\r\nafter. When do you wish to be home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much later than nine,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica answered, with the insincerity of\r\nprudence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we will just go quietly along. I wish we could have started early\r\nin the afternoon. But that may be for another day, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn her lap Monica had the little brown-paper parcel which contained her\r\npresent. She saw that Widdowson glanced at it from time to time, but\r\nshe could not bring herself to explain what it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was very much afraid that I should not see you to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as\r\nthey glided softly by Chelsea Embankment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I promised to come if it was fine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I feared something might prevent you. You are very kind to give\r\nme your company.\xe2\x80\x9d He was looking at the tips of her little boots. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t say how I thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMuch embarrassed, Monica could only gaze at one of the sculls, as it\r\nrose and fell, the water dripping from it in bright beads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast year,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cI went on the river two or three times, but\r\nalone. This year I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been in a boat till to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou prefer driving?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s only chance. I do drive a good deal, however. I wish it were\r\npossible to take you through the splendid country I saw a day or two\r\nago\xe2\x80\x94down in Surrey. Perhaps some day you will let me. I live rather a\r\nlonely life, as you see. I have a housekeeper; no relative lives with\r\nme. My only relative in London is a sister-in-law, and we very seldom\r\nmeet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t you employ yourself in any way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very idle. But that\xe2\x80\x99s partly because I have worked very hard and\r\nhopelessly all my life\xe2\x80\x94till a year and a half ago. I began to earn my\r\nown living when I was fourteen, and now I am forty-four\xe2\x80\x94to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is your birthday?\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica, with an odd look the other could\r\nnot understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I only remembered it a few hours ago. Strange that such a treat\r\nshould have been provided for me. Yes, I am very idle. A year and a\r\nhalf ago my only brother died. He had been very successful in life, and\r\nhe left me what I regard as a fortune, though it was only a small part\r\nof what he had.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe listener\xe2\x80\x99s heart throbbed. Without intending it, she pulled the\r\ntiller so that the boat began to turn towards land.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe left hand a little,\xe2\x80\x9d said Widdowson, smiling correctly. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nright. Many days I don\xe2\x80\x99t leave home. I am fond of reading, and now I\r\nmake up for all the time lost in years gone by. Do you care for books?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never read very much, and I feel very ignorant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that is only for want of opportunity, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe glanced at the brown-paper parcel. Acting on an impulse which\r\nperturbed her, Monica began to slip off the loosely-tied string, and to\r\nunfold the paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought it was a book!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Widdowson merrily, when she had\r\nrevealed a part of her present.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you told me your name,\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica, \xe2\x80\x9cI ought perhaps to have\r\ntold you mine. It\xe2\x80\x99s written here. My sisters gave me this to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe offered the little volume. He took it as though it were something\r\nfragile, and\xe2\x80\x94the sculls fixed under his elbows\xe2\x80\x94turned to the fly-leaf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? It is _your_ birthday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I am twenty-one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you let me shake hands with you?\xe2\x80\x9d His pressure of her fingers was\r\nthe lightest possible. \xe2\x80\x9cNow that\xe2\x80\x99s rather a strange thing\xe2\x80\x94isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\r\nOh, I remember this book very well, though I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen it or heard\r\nof it for twenty years. My mother used to read it on Sundays. And it is\r\nreally your birthday? I am more than twice your age, Miss Madden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe last remark was uttered anxiously, mournfully. Then, as if to\r\nreassure himself by exerting physical strength, he drove the boat along\r\nwith half a dozen vigorous strokes. Monica was rustling over the pages,\r\nbut without seeing them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think,\xe2\x80\x9d said her companion presently, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are very well\r\ncontented with your life in that house of business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard a good deal of the hardships of such a life. Will you\r\ntell me something about yours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nReadily she gave him a sketch of her existence from Sunday to Sunday,\r\nbut without indignation, and as if the subject had no great interest\r\nfor her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must be very strong,\xe2\x80\x9d was Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s comment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe lady I went to see this afternoon told me I looked ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I can see the effects of overwork. My wonder is that you\r\nendure it at all. Is that lady an old acquaintance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica answered with all necessary detail, and went on to mention the\r\nproposal that had been made to her. The hearer reflected, and put\r\nfurther questions. Unwilling to speak of the little capital she\r\npossessed, Monica told him that her sisters might perhaps help her to\r\nlive whilst she was learning a new occupation. But Widdowson had become\r\nabstracted; he ceased pulling, crossed his arms on the oars, and\r\nwatched other boats that were near. Two deep wrinkles, rippling in\r\ntheir course, had formed across his forehead, and his eyes widened in a\r\ngaze of complete abstraction at the farther shore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d fell from him at length, as though in continuation of something\r\nhe had been saying, \xe2\x80\x9cI began to earn my bread when I was fourteen. My\r\nfather was an auctioneer at Brighton. A few years after his marriage he\r\nhad a bad illness, which left him completely deaf. His partnership with\r\nanother man was dissolved, and as things went worse and worse with him,\r\nmy mother started a lodging-house, which somehow supported us for a\r\nlong time. She was a sensible, good, and brave woman. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid my\r\nfather had a good many faults that made her life hard. He was of a\r\nviolent temper, and of course the deafness didn\xe2\x80\x99t improve it. Well, one\r\nday a cab knocked him down in the King\xe2\x80\x99s Road, and from that injury,\r\nthough not until a year after, he died. There were only two children; I\r\nwas the elder. My mother couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep me at school very long, so, at\r\nfourteen, I was sent into the office of the man who had been my\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s partner, to serve him and learn the business. I did serve him\r\nfor years, and for next to no payment, but he taught me nothing more\r\nthan he could help. He was one of those heartless, utterly selfish men\r\nthat one meets too often in the business world. I ought never to have\r\nbeen sent there, for my father had always an ill opinion of him; but he\r\npretended a friendly interest in me\xe2\x80\x94just, I am convinced, to make the\r\nuse of me that he did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was silent, and began rowing again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat happened them?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Monica.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mustn\xe2\x80\x99t make out that I was a faultless boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, with the\r\nsmile that graved wrinkles about his eyes; \xe2\x80\x9cquite the opposite. I had a\r\ngood deal of my father\xe2\x80\x99s temper; I often behaved very badly to my\r\nmother; what I needed was some stern but conscientious man to look\r\nafter me and make me work. In my spare time I lay about on the shore,\r\nor got into mischief with other boys. It needed my mother\xe2\x80\x99s death to\r\nmake a more sensible fellow of me, and by that time it was too late. I\r\nmean I was too old to be trained into profitable business habits. Up to\r\nnineteen I had been little more than an errand and office boy, and all\r\nthrough the after years I never got a much better position.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t understand that,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Monica thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to\xe2\x80\x94to be the kind of man that would make your way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I?\xe2\x80\x9d The description pleased him; he laughed cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nnever found what my way was to be. I have always hated office work, and\r\nbusiness of every kind; yet I could never see an opening in any other\r\ndirection. I have been all my life a clerk\xe2\x80\x94like so many thousands of\r\nother men. Nowadays, if I happen to be in the City when all the clerks\r\nare coming away from business, I feel an inexpressible pity for them. I\r\nfeel I should like to find two or three of the hardest driven, and just\r\ndivide my superfluous income between. A clerk\xe2\x80\x99s life\xe2\x80\x94a life of the\r\noffice without any hope of rising\xe2\x80\x94that is a hideous fate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your brother got on well. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t he help you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t agree. We always quarrelled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you really so ill-tempered?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was asked in Monica\xe2\x80\x99s most naive tone, with a serious air of\r\ninvestigation which at first confused Widdowson, then made him laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince I was a lad,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, \xe2\x80\x9cI have never quarrelled with any one\r\nexcept my brother. I think it\xe2\x80\x99s only very unreasonable people that\r\nirritate me. Some men have told me that I was far too easy-going, too\r\ngood-natured. Certainly I _desire_ to be good-natured. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\neasily make friends; as a rule I can\xe2\x80\x99t talk to strangers. I keep so\r\nmuch to myself that those who know me only a little think me surly and\r\nunsociable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo your brother always refused to help you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wasn\xe2\x80\x99t easy for him to help me. He got into a stockbroker\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\nwent on step by step until he had saved a little money; then he\r\nspeculated in all sorts of ways. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t employ me himself\xe2\x80\x94and if\r\nhe could have done so, we should never have got on together. It was\r\nimpossible for him to recommend me to any one except as a clerk. He was\r\na born money-maker. I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you an example of how he grew rich. In\r\nconsequence of some mortgage business he came into possession of a\r\nfield at Clapham. As late as 1875 this field brought him only a rent of\r\nforty pounds; it was freehold property, and he refused many offers of\r\npurchase. Well, in 1885, the year before he died, the ground-rents from\r\nthat field\xe2\x80\x94now covered with houses\xe2\x80\x94were seven hundred and ninety\r\npounds a year. That\xe2\x80\x99s how men get on who have capital and know how to\r\nuse it. If _I_ had had capital, it would never have yielded me more\r\nthan three or four per cent. I was doomed to work for other people who\r\nwere growing rich. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter much now, except that so many\r\nyears of life have been lost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad your brother any children?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo children. All the same, it astonished me when I heard his will; I\r\nhad expected nothing. In one day\xe2\x80\x94in one hour\xe2\x80\x94I passed from slavery to\r\nfreedom, from poverty to more than comfort. We never _hated_ each\r\nother; I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to think that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t it bring you friends as well as comfort?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d he laughed, \xe2\x80\x9cI am not so rich as to have people pressing for my\r\nacquaintance. I have only about six hundred a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica drew in her breath silently, then gazed at the distance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t made any new friends. The one or two men I care for are\r\nnot much better off than I used to be, and I always feel ashamed to ask\r\nthem to come and see me. Perhaps they think I shun them because of\r\ntheir position, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to justify myself. Life has always\r\nbeen full of worrying problems for me. I can\xe2\x80\x99t take things in the\r\nsimple way that comes natural to other men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think we ought to be turning back, Mr. Widdowson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we will. I am sorry the time goes so quickly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen a few minutes had passed in silence, he asked,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you feel that I am no longer quite a stranger to you, Miss Madden?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94you have told me so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very kind of you to listen so patiently. I wish I had more\r\ninteresting things to tell, but you see what a dull life mine has\r\nbeen.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused, and let the boat waver on the stream for a moment.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I dared to speak to you last Sunday I had only the faintest hope\r\nthat you would grant me your acquaintance. You can\xe2\x80\x99t, I am sure, repent\r\nof having done me that kindness\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne never knows. I doubted whether I ought to talk with a stranger\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRightly\xe2\x80\x94quite rightly. It was my perseverance\xe2\x80\x94you saw, I hope, that\r\nI could never dream of giving you offence. The rule is necessary, but\r\nyou see there may be exceptional cases.\xe2\x80\x9d He was giving a lazy stroke\r\nnow and then, which, as the tide was still, just moved the boat\r\nonwards. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw something in your face that _compelled_ me to speak to\r\nyou. And now we may really be friends, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I can think of you as a friend, Mr. Widdowson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA large boat was passing with four or five young men and girls who sang\r\nin good time and tune. Only a song of the music-hall or of the nigger\r\nminstrels, but it sounded pleasantly with the plash of the oars. A fine\r\nsunset had begun to glow upon the river; its warmth gave a tone to\r\nMonica\xe2\x80\x99s thin cheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will let me see you again before long? Let me drive you to\r\nHampton Court next Sunday\xe2\x80\x94or any other place you would choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely I shall be invited to my friend\xe2\x80\x99s in Chelsea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you seriously think of leaving the shop?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I must have time to think about it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94yes. But if I write a line to you, say on Friday, would you let\r\nme know whether you can come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease to let me refuse for next Sunday. The one after, perhaps\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bent his head, looked desperately grave, and drove the boat on.\r\nMonica was disturbed, but held to her resolution, which Widdowson\r\nsilently accepted. The rest of the way they exchanged only brief\r\nsentences, about the beauty of the sky, the scenes on river or bank,\r\nand other impersonal matters. After landing, they walked in silence\r\ntowards Chelsea Bridge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I must go quickly home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy train\xe2\x80\x94from York Road to Walworth Road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWiddowson cast a curious glance at her. One would have imagined that he\r\nfound something to disapprove in this ready knowledge of London transit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go with you to the station, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWithout a word spoken, they walked the short distance to York Road.\r\nMonica took her ticket, and offered a hand for good-bye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may write to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Widdowson, his face set in an expression of\r\nanxiety, \xe2\x80\x9cand make an appointment, if possible, for the Sunday after\r\nnext?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be glad to come\xe2\x80\x94if I can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be a very long time to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a faint smile, Monica hurried away to the platform. In the train\r\nshe looked like one whose mind is occupied with grave trouble. Fatigue\r\nhad suddenly overcome her; she leaned back and closed her eyes.\r\n\r\nAt a street corner very near to Messrs. Scotcher\xe2\x80\x99s establishment she\r\nwas intercepted by a tall, showily-dressed, rather coarse-featured\r\ngirl, who seemed to have been loitering about. It was Miss Eade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to speak to you, Miss Madden. Where did you go with Mr.\r\nBullivant this morning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice could not have been more distinctive of a London shop-girl;\r\nits tone signified irritation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith Mr. Bullivant? I went nowhere with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I _saw_ you both get into the bus in Kennington Park Road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you?\xe2\x80\x9d Monica returned coldly. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help it if Mr. Bullivant\r\nhappened to be going the same way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very well! I thought you was to be trusted. It\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou behave very foolishly, Miss Eade,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the other, whose\r\nnerves at this moment would not allow her to use patience with the\r\njealous girl. \xe2\x80\x9cI can only tell you that I have never thought again of\r\nMr. Bullivant since he left the bus somewhere in Clapham Road. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ntired of talking about such things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, see here, don\xe2\x80\x99t be cross. Come and walk a bit and tell me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m too tired. And there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing whatever to tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, if you\xe2\x80\x99re going to be narsty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica walked on, but the girl caught her up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be so sharp with me, Miss Madden. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say as you wanted him\r\nto go in the bus with you. But you might tell me what he had to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing at all; except that he wished to know where I was going, which\r\nwas no business of his. I did what I could for you. I told him that if\r\nhe asked you to go up the river with him I felt sure you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nrefuse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you did!\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Eade threw up her head. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was a\r\nvery delicate thing to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very unreasonable. I myself don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was very delicate,\r\nbut haven\xe2\x80\x99t you worried me to say something of the kind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I haven\xe2\x80\x99t! Worried you, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen please never to speak to me on the subject again. I\xe2\x80\x99m tired of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did _he_ say, when you\xe2\x80\x99d said that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you _are_ narsty to-day! Really you are! If it had been the other\r\nway about, I\xe2\x80\x99d never have treated _you_ like this, that I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were close to the door by which Messrs. Scotcher\xe2\x80\x99s resident\r\nemployees entered at night. Monica had taken out her latchkey. But Miss\r\nEade could not endure the thought of being left in torturing ignorance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Do_ tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything for you I can. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe unkind, Miss Madden!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica turned back again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were you, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so silly. I can\xe2\x80\x99t do more than assure you\r\nand promise you that I shall never listen to Mr. Bullivant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what did he say about _me_, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Eade kept a mortified silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had much better not think of him at all. I would have more pride.\r\nI wish I could make you see him as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you did really speak about me? Oh, I do wish you\xe2\x80\x99d find some one\r\nto go out with. Then perhaps\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica stood still, hesitated, and at length said,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I _have_ found some one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have?\xe2\x80\x9d The girl all but danced with joy. \xe2\x80\x9cYou really have?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94so now don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble me any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time she was allowed to turn back and enter the house.\r\n\r\nNo one else had yet come in. Monica ate a mouthful of bread and cheese,\r\nwhich was in readiness on the long table down in the basement, and at\r\nonce went to bed. But no welcome drowsiness fell upon her. At half-past\r\neleven, when two of the other five girls who slept in the room made\r\ntheir appearance, she was still changing uneasily from side to side.\r\nThey lit the gas (it was not turned off till midnight, after which hour\r\nthe late arrivals had to use a candle of their own procuring), and\r\nbegan a lively conversation on the events of the day. Afraid of being\r\nobliged to talk, Monica feigned sleep.\r\n\r\nAt twelve, just as the gas went out, another pair came to repose. They\r\nhad been quarrelling, and were very gloomy. After a long and\r\nacrimonious discussion in the dark as to which of them should find a\r\ncandle\xe2\x80\x94it ended in one of the girls who was in bed impatiently\r\nsupplying a light\xe2\x80\x94they began sullenly to throw off their garments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Miss Madden awake?\xe2\x80\x9d said one of them, looking in Monica\xe2\x80\x99s direction.\r\n\r\nThere was no reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s picked up some feller to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the speaker, lowering\r\nher voice, and glancing round at her companions with a grin. \xe2\x80\x9cOr else\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s had him all along\xe2\x80\x94I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHeads were put forward eagerly, and inquiries whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s oldish, I should say. I caught sight of them just as they was\r\ngoing off in a boat from Battersea Park, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see his face\r\nvery well. He looked rather like Mr. Thomas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Thomas was a member of the drapery firm, a man of fifty, ugly and\r\naustere. At this description the listeners giggled and uttered\r\nexclamations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas he a swell?\xe2\x80\x9d asked one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder if he was. You can trust Miss M. to keep her eyes\r\nopen. She\xe2\x80\x99s one of the sly and quiet \xe2\x80\x9cuns.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, is she?\xe2\x80\x9d murmured another enviously. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s just one of those as\r\ngets made a fool of\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s _my_ opinion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe point was argued for some minutes. It led to talk about Miss Eade,\r\nwho was treated with frank contempt because of her ill-disguised\r\npursuit of a mere counter-man. These other damsels had, at present,\r\nmore exalted views, for they were all younger than Miss Eade.\r\n\r\nJust before one o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when silence had reigned for a quarter of an\r\nhour, there entered with much bustle the last occupant of the bedroom.\r\nShe was a young woman with a morally unenviable reputation, though some\r\nof her colleagues certainly envied her. Money came to her with\r\nremarkable readiness whenever she had need of it. As usual, she began\r\nto talk very loud, at first with innocent vulgarity; exciting a little\r\nlaughter, she became anecdotic and very scandalous. It took her a long\r\ntime to disrobe, and when the candle was out, she still had her richest\r\nstory to relate\xe2\x80\x94of point so Rabelaisian that one or two voices made\r\nthemselves heard in serious protest. The gifted anecdotist replied with\r\na long laugh, then cried, \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, young ladies!\xe2\x80\x9d and sank\r\npeacefully to slumber.\r\n\r\nAs for Monica, she saw the white dawn peep at the window, and closed\r\nher tear-stained eyes only when the life of a new week had begun\r\nnoisily in Walworth Road.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\n\r\nA CAMP OF THE RESERVE\r\n\r\n\r\nIn consequence of letters exchanged during the week, next Sunday\r\nbrought the three Miss Maddens to Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road to lunch with Miss\r\nBarfoot. Alice had recovered from her cold, but was still ailing, and\r\ntook rather a gloomy view of the situation she had lately reviewed with\r\nsuch courage. Virginia maintained her enthusiastic faith in Miss Nunn,\r\nand was prepared to reverence Miss Barfoot with hardly less fervour.\r\nBoth of them found it difficult to understand their young sister, who,\r\nin her letters, had betrayed distaste for the change of career proposed\r\nto her. They were received with the utmost kindness, and all greatly\r\nenjoyed their afternoon, for not even Monica\xe2\x80\x99s prejudice against a\r\nhouse, which in her own mind she had stigmatized as \xe2\x80\x9can old-maid\r\nfactory,\xe2\x80\x9d could resist the charm of the hostess.\r\n\r\nThough Miss Barfoot had something less than a woman\xe2\x80\x99s average stature,\r\nthe note of her presence was personal dignity. She was handsome, and\r\nher carriage occasionally betrayed a consciousness of the fact.\r\nAccording to circumstances, she bore herself as the lady of\r\naristocratic tastes, as a genial woman of the world, or as a fervid\r\nprophetess of female emancipation, and each character was supported\r\nwith a spontaneity, a good-natured confidence, which inspired liking\r\nand respect. A brilliant complexion and eyes that sparkled with\r\nhabitual cheerfulness gave her the benefit of doubt when her age was in\r\nquestion; her style of dress, gracefully ornate, would have led a\r\nstranger to presume her a wedded lady of some distinction. Yet Mary\r\nBarfoot had known many troubles, poverty among them. Her experiences\r\nand struggles bore a close resemblance to those which Rhoda Nunn had\r\ngone through, and the time of trial had lasted longer. Mental and moral\r\nstamina would have assured her against such evils of celibacy as\r\nappeared in the elder Maddens, but it was to a change of worldly\r\nfortune that she owed this revival of youthful spirit and energy in\r\nmiddle life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou and I must be friends,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Monica, holding the girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsoft little hand. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are both black but comely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe compliment to herself seemed the most natural thing in the world.\r\nMonica blushed with pleasure, and could not help laughing.\r\n\r\nIt was all but decided that Monica should become a pupil at the school\r\nin Great Portland Street. In a brief private conversation, Miss Barfoot\r\noffered to lend her the money that might be needful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing but a business transaction, Miss Madden. You can give me\r\nsecurity; you will repay me at your convenience. If, in the end, this\r\noccupation doesn\xe2\x80\x99t please you, you will at all events have regained\r\nhealth. It is clear to me that you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t go on in that dreadful place\r\nyou described to Miss Nunn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe visitors took their leave at about five o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor things! Poor things!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Miss Barfoot, when she was alone\r\nwith her friend. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can we possibly do for the older ones?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are excellent creatures,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda; \xe2\x80\x9ckind, innocent women; but\r\nuseful for nothing except what they have done all their lives. The\r\neldest can\xe2\x80\x99t teach seriously, but she can keep young children out of\r\nmischief and give them a nice way of speaking. Her health is breaking\r\ndown, you can see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor woman! One of the saddest types.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDecidedly. Virginia isn\xe2\x80\x99t quite so depressing\xe2\x80\x94but how childish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey all strike me as childish. Monica is a dear little girl; it\r\nseemed a great absurdity to talk to her about business. Of course she\r\nmust find a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda\xe2\x80\x99s tone of slighting concession amused her companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, after all we don\xe2\x80\x99t desire the end of the race.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I suppose not,\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda admitted with a laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA word of caution. Your zeal is eating you up. At this rate, you will\r\nhinder our purpose. We have no mission to prevent girls from marrying\r\nsuitably\xe2\x80\x94only to see that those who can\xe2\x80\x99t shall have a means of living\r\nwith some satisfaction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat chance is there that this girl will marry suitably?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, who knows? At all events, there will be more likelihood of it if\r\nshe comes into our sphere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally? Do you know any man that would dream of marrying her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not, at present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was clear that Miss Barfoot stood in some danger of becoming\r\nsubordinate to her more vehement friend. Her little body, for all its\r\nnatural dignity, put her at a disadvantage in the presence of Rhoda,\r\nwho towered above her with rather imperious stateliness. Her suavity\r\nwas no match for Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s vigorous abruptness. But the two were very\r\nfond of each other, and by this time thought themselves able safely to\r\ndispense with the forms at first imposed by their mutual relations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she marry at all,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Miss Nunn, \xe2\x80\x9cshe will marry badly. The\r\nfamily is branded. They belong to the class we know so well\xe2\x80\x94with no\r\nsocial position, and unable to win an individual one. I must find a\r\nname for that ragged regiment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot regarded her friend thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRhoda, what comfort have you for the poor in spirit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone whatever, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid. My mission is not to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a pause, she added,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have their religious faith, I suppose; and it\xe2\x80\x99s answerable for a\r\ngood deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a terrible responsibility to rob them of it,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the\r\nelder woman gravely.\r\n\r\nRhoda made a gesture of impatience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a terrible responsibility to do anything at all. But I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nglad\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she laughed scornfully\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat it\xe2\x80\x99s not my task to release them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary Barfoot mused, a compassionate shadow on her fine face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think we can do without the spirit of that religion,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nat length\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe essential human spirit. These poor women\xe2\x80\x94one ought to\r\nbe very tender with them. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like your \xe2\x80\x9cragged regiment\xe2\x80\x9d phrase.\r\nWhen I grow old and melancholy, I think I shall devote myself to poor\r\nhopeless and purposeless women\xe2\x80\x94try to warm their hearts a little\r\nbefore they go hence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmirable!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Rhoda, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cBut in the meantime they cumber\r\nus; we have to fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe threw forward her arms, as though with spear and buckler. Miss\r\nBarfoot was smiling at this Palladin attitude when a servant announced\r\ntwo ladies\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Smallbrook and Miss Haven. They were aunt and niece;\r\nthe former a tall, ungainly, sharp-featured widow; the latter a\r\nsweet-faced, gentle, sensible-looking girl of five-and-twenty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad you are back again,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the widow, as she shook\r\nhands with Miss Barfoot, speaking in a hard, unsympathetic voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI do\r\nso want to ask your advice about an interesting girl who has applied to\r\nme. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid her past won\xe2\x80\x99t bear looking into, but most certainly she\r\nis a reformed character. Winifred is most favourably impressed with\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Haven, the Winifred in question, began to talk apart with Rhoda\r\nNunn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do wish my aunt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t exaggerate so,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a subdued\r\nvoice, whilst Mrs. Smallbrook still talked loudly and urgently. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nnever said that I was favourably impressed. The girl protests far too\r\nmuch; she has played on aunt\xe2\x80\x99s weaknesses, I fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut who is she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, some one who lost her character long ago, and lives, I should say,\r\non charitable people. Just because I said that she must once have had a\r\nvery nice face, aunt misrepresents me in this way\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s too bad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she an educated person?\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Barfoot was heard to ask.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot precisely well educated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the lower classes, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like that term, you know. Of the _poorer_ classes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe never was a lady,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Miss Haven quietly but decidedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I fear I can be of no use,\xe2\x80\x9d said the hostess, betraying some of\r\nher secret satisfaction in being able thus to avoid Mrs. Smallbrook\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrequest. Winifred, a pupil at Great Portland Street, was much liked by\r\nboth her teachers; but the aunt, with her ceaseless philanthropy at\r\nother people\xe2\x80\x99s expense, could only be considered a bore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you don\xe2\x80\x99t limit your humanity, Miss Barfoot, by the\r\nartificial divisions of society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think those divisions are anything but artificial,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the\r\nhostess good-humouredly. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the uneducated classes I have no interest\r\nwhatever. You have heard me say so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I cannot think\xe2\x80\x94isn\xe2\x80\x99t that just a little narrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps so. I choose my sphere, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. Let those work for the\r\nlower classes (I must call them lower, for they are, in every sense),\r\nlet those work for them who have a call to do so. I have none. I must\r\nkeep to my own class.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely, Miss Nunn,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the widow, turning to Rhoda, \xe2\x80\x9cwe work\r\nfor the abolition of all unjust privilege? To us, is not a woman a\r\nwoman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am obliged to agree with Miss Barfoot. I think that as soon as we\r\nbegin to meddle with uneducated people, all our schemes and views are\r\nunsettled. We have to learn a new language, for one thing. But your\r\nmissionary enterprise is admirable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor my part,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Mrs. Smallbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cI aim at the solidarity of\r\nwoman. You, at all events, agree with me, Winifred?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really don\xe2\x80\x99t think, aunt, that there can be any solidarity of ladies\r\nwith servant girls,\xe2\x80\x9d responded Miss Haven, encouraged by a look from\r\nRhoda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I grieve that your charity falls so far below the Christian\r\nstandard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot firmly guided the conversation to a more hopeful subject.\r\n\r\nNot many people visited this house. Every Wednesday evening, from\r\nhalf-past eight to eleven, Miss Barfoot was at home to any of her\r\nacquaintances, including her pupils, who chose to call upon her; but\r\nthis was in the nature of an association with recognized objects. Of\r\nsociety in the common sense Miss Barfoot saw very little; she had no\r\ntime to sacrifice in the pursuit of idle ceremonies. By the successive\r\ndeaths of two relatives, a widowed sister and an uncle, she had come\r\ninto possession of a modest fortune; but no thought of a life such as\r\nwould have suggested itself to most women in her place ever tempted\r\nher. Her studies had always been of a very positive nature; her\r\nabilities were of a kind uncommon in women, or at all events very\r\nrarely developed in one of her sex. She could have managed a large and\r\ncomplicated business, could have filled a place on a board of\r\ndirectors, have taken an active part in municipal government\xe2\x80\x94nay,\r\nperchance in national. And this turn of intellect consisted with many\r\ntraits of character so strongly feminine that people who knew her best\r\nthought of her with as much tenderness as admiration. She did not seek\r\nto become known as the leader of a \xe2\x80\x9cmovement,\xe2\x80\x9d yet her quiet work was\r\nprobably more effectual than the public career of women who\r\npropagandize for female emancipation. Her aim was to draw from the\r\noverstocked profession of teaching as many capable young women as she\r\ncould lay hands on, and to fit them for certain of the pursuits\r\nnowadays thrown open to their sex. She held the conviction that\r\nwhatever man could do, woman could do equally well\xe2\x80\x94those tasks only\r\nexcepted which demand great physical strength. At her instance, and\r\nwith help from her purse, two girls were preparing themselves to be\r\npharmaceutical chemists; two others had been aided by her to open a\r\nbookseller\xe2\x80\x99s shop; and several who had clerkships in view received an\r\nadmirable training at her school in Great Portland Street.\r\n\r\nThither every weekday morning Miss Barfoot and Rhoda repaired; they\r\narrived at nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and with an hour\xe2\x80\x99s interval work went on until\r\nfive.\r\n\r\nEntering by the private door of a picture-cleaner\xe2\x80\x99s shop, they ascended\r\nto the second story, where two rooms had been furnished like\r\ncomfortable offices; two smaller on the floor above served for\r\ndressing-rooms. In one of the offices, typewriting and occasionally\r\nother kinds of work that demanded intelligence were carried on by three\r\nor four young women regularly employed. To superintend this department\r\nwas Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s chief duty, together with business correspondence under\r\nthe principal\xe2\x80\x99s direction. In the second room Miss Barfoot instructed\r\nher pupils, never more than three being with her at a time. A bookcase\r\nfull of works on the Woman Question and allied topics served as a\r\ncirculating library; volumes were lent without charge to the members of\r\nthis little society. Once a month Miss Barfoot or Miss Nunn, by turns,\r\ngave a brief address on some set subject; the hour was four o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\r\nand about a dozen hearers generally assembled. Both worked very hard.\r\nMiss Barfoot did not look upon her enterprise as a source of pecuniary\r\nprofit, but she had made the establishment more than self-supporting.\r\nHer pupils increased in number, and the working department promised\r\noccupation for a larger staff than was at present engaged. The young\r\nwomen in general answered their friend\xe2\x80\x99s expectations, but of course\r\nthere were disappointing instances. One of these had caused Miss\r\nBarfoot special distress. A young girl whom she had released from a\r\nlife of much hardship, and who, after a couple of months\xe2\x80\x99 trial, bade\r\nfair to develop noteworthy ability, of a sudden disappeared. She was\r\nwithout relatives in London, and Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s endeavours to find her\r\nproved for several weeks very futile. Then came news of her; she was\r\nliving as the mistress of a married man. Every effort was made to bring\r\nher back, but the girl resisted; presently she again passed out of\r\nsight, and now more than a year had elapsed since Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s last\r\ninterview with her.\r\n\r\nThis Monday morning, among letters delivered at the house, was one from\r\nthe strayed girl. Miss Barfoot read it in private, and throughout the\r\nday remained unusually grave. At five o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when staff and pupils\r\nhad all departed, she sat for a while in meditation, then spoke to\r\nRhoda, who was glancing over a book by the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a letter I should like you to read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething that has been troubling you since morning, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda took the sheet and quickly ran through its contents. Her face\r\nhardened, and she threw down the letter with a smile of contempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you advise?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the elder woman, closely observing her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn answer in two lines\xe2\x80\x94with a cheque enclosed, if you see fit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes that really meet the case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than meets it, I should say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot pondered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am doubtful. That is a letter of despair, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t close my ears\r\nto it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had an affection for the girl. Help her, by all means, if you feel\r\ncompelled to. But you would hardly dream of taking her back again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the point. Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor one thing,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Rhoda, looking coldly down upon her friend,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou will never do any good with her. For another, she isn\xe2\x80\x99t a suitable\r\ncompanion for the girls she would meet here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t be sure of either objection. She acted with deplorable\r\nrashness, with infatuation, but I never discovered any sign of evil in\r\nher. Did you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvil? Well, what does the word mean? I am not a Puritan, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\njudge her as the ordinary woman would. But I think she has put herself\r\naltogether beyond our sympathy. She was twenty-two years old\xe2\x80\x94no\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x94and she acted with her eyes open. No deceit was practised with\r\nher. She knew the man had a wife, and she was base enough to accept a\r\nshare of his attentions. Do you advocate polygamy? That is an\r\nintelligible position, I admit. It is one way of meeting the social\r\ndifficulty. But not mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Rhoda, don\xe2\x80\x99t enrage yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try not to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t see the temptation to do so. Come and sit down, and talk\r\nquietly. No, I have no fondness for polygamy. I find it very hard to\r\nunderstand how she could act as she did. But a mistake, however\r\nwretched, mustn\xe2\x80\x99t condemn a woman for life. That\xe2\x80\x99s the way of the\r\nworld, and decidedly it mustn\xe2\x80\x99t be ours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn this point I practically agree with the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you do, and it astonishes me. You are going through curious\r\nchanges, in several respects. A year ago you didn\xe2\x80\x99t speak of her like\r\nthis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPartly because I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you well enough to speak my mind. Partly\r\nyes, I have changed a good deal, no doubt. But I should never have\r\nproposed to take her by the hand and let bygones be bygones. That is an\r\namiable impulse, but anti-social.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA favourite word on your lips just now, Rhoda. Why is it anti-social?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause one of the supreme social needs of our day is the education of\r\nwomen in self-respect and self-restraint. There are plenty of\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x94men chiefly, but a few women also of a certain temperament\xe2\x80\x94who\r\ncry for a reckless individualism in these matters. They would tell you\r\nthat she behaved laudably, that she was _living out herself_\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthings of that kind. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think you shared such views.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t, altogether. \xe2\x80\x9cThe education of women in self-respect.\xe2\x80\x9d Very\r\nwell. Here is a poor woman whose self-respect has given way under\r\ngrievous temptation. Circumstances have taught her that she made a wild\r\nmistake. The man gives her up, and bids her live as she can; she is\r\nreduced to beggary. Now, in that position a girl is tempted to sink\r\nstill further. The letter of two lines and an enclosed cheque would as\r\nlikely as not plunge her into depths from which she could never be\r\nrescued. It would assure her that there was no hope. On the other hand,\r\nwe have it in our power to attempt that very education of which you\r\nspeak. She has brains, and doesn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to the vulgar. It seems to me\r\nthat you are moved by illogical impulses\xe2\x80\x94and certainly anything but\r\nkind ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda only grew more stubborn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say she yielded to a grievous temptation. What temptation? Will it\r\nbear putting into words?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I think it will,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Miss Barfoot, with her gentlest\r\nsmile. \xe2\x80\x9cShe fell in love with the man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFell in love!\xe2\x80\x9d Concentration of scorn was in this echo. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, for what\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t that phrase responsible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRhoda, let me ask you a question on which I have never ventured. Do\r\nyou know what it is to be in love?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s strong features were moved as if by a suppressed laugh; the\r\ncolour of her cheeks grew very slightly warm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a normal human being,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, with an impatient gesture.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand perfectly well what the phrase signifies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is no answer, my dear. Have you ever been in love with any man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. When I was fifteen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd not since,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the other, shaking her head and smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\r\nnot since?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank Heaven, no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are not very well able to judge this case. I, on the other\r\nhand, can judge it with the very largest understanding. Don\xe2\x80\x99t smile so\r\nwitheringly, Rhoda. I shall neglect your advice for once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will bring this girl back, and continue teaching her as before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have no one here that knows her, and with prudence she need never\r\nbe talked about by those of our friends who did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, weak\xe2\x80\x94weak\xe2\x80\x94weak!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor once I must act independently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and at a stroke change the whole character of your work. You\r\nnever proposed keeping a reformatory. Your aim is to help chosen girls,\r\nwho promise to be of some use in the world. This Miss Royston\r\nrepresents the profitless average\xe2\x80\x94no, she is below the average. Are\r\nyou so blind as to imagine that any good will ever come of such a\r\nperson? If you wish to save her from the streets, do so by all means.\r\nBut to put her among your chosen pupils is to threaten your whole\r\nundertaking. Let it once become known\xe2\x80\x94and it _would_ become\r\nknown\xe2\x80\x94that a girl of that character came here, and your usefulness is\r\nat an end. In a year\xe2\x80\x99s time you will have to choose between giving up\r\nthe school altogether and making it a refuge for outcasts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot was silent. She tapped with her fingers on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPersonal feeling is misleading you,\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda pursued. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Royston had\r\na certain cleverness, I grant; but do you think I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know that she\r\nwould never become what you hoped? All her spare time was given to\r\nnovel-reading. If every novelist could be strangled and thrown into the\r\nsea we should have some chance of reforming women. The girl\xe2\x80\x99s nature\r\nwas corrupted with sentimentality, like that of all but every woman who\r\nis intelligent enough to read what is called the best fiction, but not\r\nintelligent enough to understand its vice. Love\xe2\x80\x94love\xe2\x80\x94love; a\r\nsickening sameness of vulgarity. What is more vulgar than the ideal of\r\nnovelists? They won\xe2\x80\x99t represent the actual world; it would be too dull\r\nfor their readers. In real life, how many men and women _fall in love_?\r\nNot one in every ten thousand, I am convinced. Not one married pair in\r\nten thousand have felt for each other as two or three couples do in\r\nevery novel. There is the sexual instinct, of course, but that is quite\r\na different thing; the novelists daren\xe2\x80\x99t talk about that. The paltry\r\ncreatures daren\xe2\x80\x99t tell the one truth that would be profitable. The\r\nresult is that women imagine themselves noble and glorious when they\r\nare most near the animals. This Miss Royston\xe2\x80\x94when she rushed off to\r\nperdition, ten to one she had in mind some idiot heroine of a book. Oh,\r\nI tell you that you are losing sight of your first duty. There are\r\npeople enough to act the good Samaritan; _you_ have quite another task\r\nin life. It is your work to train and encourage girls in a path as far\r\nas possible from that of the husband-hunter. Let them marry later, if\r\nthey must; but at all events you will have cleared their views on the\r\nsubject of marriage, and put them in a position to judge the man who\r\noffers himself. You will have taught them that marriage is an alliance\r\nof intellects\xe2\x80\x94not a means of support, or something more ignoble still.\r\nBut to do this with effect you must show yourself relentless to female\r\nimbecility.
