If a girl gets to know that you have received back such a\r\nperson as Miss Royston she will be corrupted by your spirit of\r\ncharity\xe2\x80\x94corrupted, at all events, for our purposes. The endeavour to\r\ngive women a new soul is so difficult that we can\xe2\x80\x99t be cumbered by\r\nside-tasks, such as fishing foolish people out of the mud they have\r\nwalked into. Charity for human weakness is all very well in its place,\r\nbut it is precisely one of the virtues that you must _not_ teach. You\r\nhave to set an example of the sterner qualities\xe2\x80\x94to discourage anything\r\nthat resembles sentimentalism. And think if you illustrate in your own\r\nbehaviour a sympathy for the very vice of character we are trying our\r\nhardest to extirpate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a terrible harangue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Barfoot, when the passionate\r\nvoice had been silent for a few ticks of the clock. \xe2\x80\x9cI quite enter into\r\nyour point of view, but I think you go beyond practical zeal. However,\r\nI will help the girl in some other way, if possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have offended you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible to take offence at such obvious sincerity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you grant the force of what I say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe differ a good deal, Rhoda, on certain points which as a rule would\r\nnever come up to interfere with our working in harmony. You have come\r\nto dislike the very thought of marriage\xe2\x80\x94and everything of that kind. I\r\nthink it\xe2\x80\x99s a danger you ought to have avoided. True, we wish to prevent\r\ngirls from marrying just for the sake of being supported, and from\r\ndegrading themselves as poor Bella Royston has done; but surely between\r\nourselves we can admit that the vast majority of women would lead a\r\nwasted life if they did not marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI maintain that the vast majority of women lead a vain and miserable\r\nlife because they _do_ marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you blame the institution of marriage with what is chargeable to\r\nhuman fate? A vain and miserable life is the lot of nearly all mortals.\r\nMost women, whether they marry or not, will suffer and commit endless\r\nfollies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost women\xe2\x80\x94as life is at present arranged for them. Things are\r\nchanging, and we try to have our part in hastening a new order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, we use words in a different sense. I speak of human nature, not of\r\nthe effect of institutions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it is you who are unpractical. Those views lead only to pessimism\r\nand paralysis of effort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give in to your objection against bringing the girl back to work\r\nhere. I will help her in other ways. It\xe2\x80\x99s quite true that she isn\xe2\x80\x99t to\r\nbe relied upon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible to trust her in any detail of life. The pity is that her\r\ndegradation can\xe2\x80\x99t be used as an object lesson for our other girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere again we differ. You are quite mistaken in your ideas of how the\r\nmind is influenced. The misery of Bella Royston would not in the least\r\naffect any other girl\xe2\x80\x99s way of thinking about the destiny of her sex.\r\nWe must avoid exaggeration. If our friends get to think of us as\r\nfanatics, all our usefulness is over. The ideal we set up must be\r\nhuman. Do you think now that we know one single girl who in her heart\r\nbelieves it is better never to love and never to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Rhoda, more cheerful now that she had gained\r\nher point. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we know several who will not dream of marrying unless\r\nreason urges them as strongly as inclination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray, who ever distinguished in such a case between reason and\r\ninclination?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are most unusually sceptical to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, with an\r\nimpatient laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my dear. We happen to be going to the root of things, that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\r\nPerhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s as well to do so now and then. Oh, I admire you immensely,\r\nRhoda. You are the ideal adversary of those care-nothing and\r\nbelieve-nothing women who keep the world back. But don\xe2\x80\x99t prepare for\r\nyourself a woeful disillusion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake the case of Winifred Haven,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Miss Nunn. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is a\r\ngood-looking and charming girl, and some one or other will want to\r\nmarry her some day, no doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive my interrupting you. There is great doubt. She has no money\r\nbut what she can earn, and such girls, unless they are exceptionally\r\nbeautiful, are very likely indeed to remain unsought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGranted. But let us suppose she has an offer. Should you fear for her\r\nprudence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWinifred has much good sense,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted the other. \xe2\x80\x9cI think she is in\r\nas little danger as any girl we know. But it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t startle me if she\r\nmade the most lamentable mistake. Certainly I don\xe2\x80\x99t fear it. The girls\r\nof our class are not like the uneducated, who, for one reason or\r\nanother, will marry almost any man rather than remain single. They have\r\nat all events personal delicacy. But what I insist upon is, that\r\nWinifred would rather marry than not. And we must carefully bear that\r\nfact in mind. A strained ideal is as bad, practically, as no ideal at\r\nall. Only the most exceptional girl will believe it her duty to remain\r\nsingle as an example and support to what we call the odd women; yet\r\n_that_ is the most human way of urging what you desire. By taking up\r\nthe proud position that a woman must be altogether independent of\r\nsexual things, you damage your cause. Let us be glad if we put a few of\r\nthem in the way of living single with no more discontent than an\r\nunmarried man experiences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely that\xe2\x80\x99s an unfortunate comparison,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda coldly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat man\r\nlives in celibacy? Consider that unmentionable fact, and then say\r\nwhether I am wrong in refusing to forgive Miss Royston. Women\xe2\x80\x99s battle\r\nis not only against themselves. The necessity of the case demands what\r\nyou call a strained ideal. I am seriously convinced that before the\r\nfemale sex can be raised from its low level there will have to be a\r\nwidespread revolt against sexual instinct. Christianity couldn\xe2\x80\x99t spread\r\nover the world without help of the ascetic ideal, and this great\r\nmovement for woman\xe2\x80\x99s emancipation must also have its ascetics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t declare that you are wrong in that. Who knows? But it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngood policy to preach it to our young disciples.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall respect your wish; but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda paused and shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder woman gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cbelieve me that the less we\r\ntalk or think about such things the better for the peace of us all. The\r\nodious fault of working-class girls, in town and country alike, is that\r\nthey are absorbed in preoccupation with their animal nature. We, thanks\r\nto our education and the tone of our society, manage to keep that in\r\nthe background. Don\xe2\x80\x99t interfere with this satisfactory state of things.\r\nBe content to show our girls that it is their duty to lead a life of\r\neffort\xe2\x80\x94to earn their bread and to cultivate their minds. Simply ignore\r\nmarriage\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the wisest. Behave as if the thing didn\xe2\x80\x99t exist. You\r\nwill do positive harm by taking the other course\xe2\x80\x94the aggressive\r\ncourse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall obey you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood, humble creature!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Miss Barfoot. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, let us be off to\r\nChelsea. Did Miss Grey finish that copy for Mr. Houghton?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it has gone to post.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook, here\xe2\x80\x99s a big manuscript from our friend the antiquary. Two of\r\nthe girls must get to work on it at once in the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nManuscripts entrusted to them were kept in a fire-proof safe. When this\r\nhad been locked up, the ladies went to their dressing-room and prepared\r\nfor departure. The people who lived on the premises were responsible\r\nfor cleaning the rooms and other care; to them Rhoda delivered the\r\ndoor-keys.\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot was grave and silent on the way home. Rhoda, annoyed at\r\nthe subject that doubtless occupied her friend\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts, gave herself\r\nup to reflections of her own.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\nA SOCIAL ADVANCE\r\n\r\n\r\nA week\xe2\x80\x99s notice to her employers would release Monica from the\r\nengagement in Walworth Road. Such notice must be given on Monday, so\r\nthat, if she could at once make up her mind to accept Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noffer, the coming week would be her last of slavery behind the counter.\r\nOn the way home from Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road, Alice and Virginia pressed for\r\nimmediate decision; they were unable to comprehend how Monica could\r\nhesitate for another moment. The question of her place of abode had\r\nalready been discussed. One of Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s young women, who lived at\r\na convenient distance from Great Portland Street, would gladly accept a\r\npartner in her lodging\xe2\x80\x94an arrangement to be recommended for its\r\neconomy. Yet Monica shrank from speaking the final word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether it\xe2\x80\x99s worth while,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after a long\r\nsilence, as they drew near to York Road Station, whence they were to\r\ntake train for Clapham Junction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot worth while?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Virginia. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t think it would be an\r\nimprovement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose it would. I shall see how I feel about it to-morrow\r\nmorning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spent the evening at Lavender Hill, but without change in the mood\r\nthus indicated. A strange inquietude appeared in her behaviour. It was\r\nas though she were being urged to undertake something hard and\r\nrepugnant.\r\n\r\nOn her return to Walworth Road, just as she came within sight of the\r\nshop, she observed a man\xe2\x80\x99s figure some twenty yards distant, which\r\ninstantly held her attention. The dim gaslight occasioned some\r\nuncertainty, but she believed the figure was that of Widdowson. He was\r\nwalking on the other side of the street, and away from her. When the\r\nman was exactly opposite Scotcher\xe2\x80\x99s establishment he gazed in that\r\ndirection, but without stopping. Monica hastened, fearing to be seen\r\nand approached. Already she had reached the door, when Widdowson\xe2\x80\x94yes,\r\nhe it was\xe2\x80\x94turned abruptly to walk back again. His eye was at once upon\r\nher; but whether he recognized her or not Monica could not know. At\r\nthat moment she opened the door and passed in.\r\n\r\nA fit of trembling seized her, as if she had barely escaped some peril.\r\nIn the passage she stood motionless, listening with the intensity of\r\ndread. She could hear footsteps on the pavement; she expected a ring at\r\nthe door-bell. If he were so thoughtless as to come to the door, she\r\nwould on no account see him.\r\n\r\nBut there was no ring, and after a few minutes\xe2\x80\x99 waiting she recovered\r\nher self-command. She had not made a mistake; even his features had\r\nbeen discernible as he turned towards her. Was this the first time that\r\nhe had come to look at the place where she lived\xe2\x80\x94possibly to spy upon\r\nher? She resented this behaviour, yet the feeling was confused with a\r\ncertain satisfaction.\r\n\r\nFrom one of the dormitories there was a view of Walworth Road. She ran\r\nupstairs, softly opened the door of that room, and peeped in. The low\r\nburning gas showed her that only one bed had an occupant, who appeared\r\nto be asleep. Softly she went to the window, drew the blind aside, and\r\nlooked down into the street. But Widdowson had disappeared. He might of\r\ncourse be on this side of the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d suddenly asked a voice from the occupied bed.\r\n\r\nThe speaker was Miss Eade. Monica looked at her, and nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou? What are you doing here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to see if some one was standing outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean _him_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a beastly headache. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hold myself up, and I had to\r\ncome home at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock. There\xe2\x80\x99s such pains all down my back too. I\r\nshan\xe2\x80\x99t stay at this beastly place much longer. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get ill,\r\nlike Miss Radford. Somebody went to see her at the hospital this\r\nafternoon, and she\xe2\x80\x99s awfully bad. Well, have you seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone. Good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Monica left the room.\r\n\r\nNext day she notified her intention of leaving her employment. No\r\nquestions were asked; she was of no particular importance; fifty, or,\r\nfor the matter of that, five score, young women equally capable could\r\nbe found to fill her place.\r\n\r\nOn Tuesday morning there came a letter from Virginia\xe2\x80\x94a few lines\r\nrequesting her to meet her sisters, as soon as possible after closing\r\ntime that evening, in front of the shop. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have something _very\r\ndelightful_ to tell you. We _do hope_ you gave notice to-day, as things\r\nare getting so bright in every direction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt a quarter to ten she was able to run out, and close at hand were the\r\ntwo eagerly awaiting her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Darby has found a place for Alice,\xe2\x80\x9d began Virginia. \xe2\x80\x9cWe heard by\r\nthe afternoon post yesterday. A lady at Yatton wants a governess for\r\ntwo young children. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it fortunate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo delightfully convenient for what we were thinking of,\xe2\x80\x9d put in the\r\neldest, with her croaking voice. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing could have been better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean about the school?\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica dreamily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the school,\xe2\x80\x9d Virginia replied, with trembling earnestness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYatton is convenient both for Clevedon and Weston. Alice will be able\r\nto run over to both places and make enquiries, and ascertain where the\r\nbest opening would be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion, hitherto but timidly discussed, had taken hold\r\nupon their minds as soon as Alice received the practical call to her\r\nnative region. Both were enthusiastic for the undertaking. It afforded\r\nthem a novel subject of conversation, and inspirited them by seeming to\r\nrestore their self-respect. After all, they might have a mission, a\r\ntask in the world. They pictured themselves the heads of a respectable\r\nand thriving establishment, with subordinate teachers, with pleasant\r\nsocial relations; they felt young again, and capable of indefinite\r\nactivity. Why had they not thought of this long ago? and thereupon they\r\nreverted to antistrophic laudation of Rhoda Nunn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it a good place?\xe2\x80\x9d their younger sister inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, pretty good. Only twelve pounds a year, but nice people, Mrs.\r\nDarby says. They want me at once, and it is very likely that in a few\r\nweeks I shall go with them to the seaside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _could_ have been better?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Virginia. \xe2\x80\x9cHer health will be\r\nestablished, and in half a year, or less, we shall be able to come to a\r\ndecision about the great step. Oh, and have you given notice, darling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth clapped their hands like children. It was an odd little scene on\r\nthe London pavement at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock at night; so intimately domestic\r\namid surroundings the very antithesis of domesticity. Only a few yards\r\naway, a girl, to whom the pavement was a place of commerce, stood\r\nlaughing with two men. The sound of her voice hinted to Monica the\r\nadvisability of walking as they conversed, and they moved towards\r\nWalworth Road Station.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thought at first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Virginia, \xe2\x80\x9cthat when Alice had gone you\r\nmight like to share my room; but then the distance from Great Portland\r\nStreet would be a decided objection. I might move, but we doubt whether\r\nthat would be worth while. It is so comfortable with Mrs. Conisbee, and\r\nfor the short remaining time\xe2\x80\x94Christmas, I should think, would be a\r\nvery good time for opening. If it were possible to decide upon dear old\r\nClevedon, of course we should prefer it; but perhaps Weston will offer\r\nmore scope. Alice will weigh all the arguments on the spot. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nenvy her, Monica? Think of being _there_ in this summer weather!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you go as well?\xe2\x80\x9d Monica asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? And take lodgings, you mean? We never thought of that. But we still\r\nhave to consider expenditure very seriously, you know. If possible, I\r\nmust find employment for the rest of the year. Remember how very likely\r\nit is that Miss Nunn will have something to suggest for me. And when I\r\nthink it will be of so much practical use for me to see her frequently\r\nfor a few weeks. Already I have learnt so much from her and from Miss\r\nBarfoot. Their conversation is so encouraging. I feel that it is a\r\ntraining of the mind to be in contact with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I quite share that view,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alice, with tremulous earnestness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVirginia can reap much profit from intercourse with them. They have\r\nthe new ideas in education, and it would be so good if our school began\r\nwith the advantage of quite a modern system.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica became silent. When her sisters had talked in the same strain\r\nfor a quarter of an hour, she said absently,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wrote to Miss Barfoot last night, so I suppose I shall be able to\r\nmove to those lodgings next Sunday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock before they parted. Having taken leave of her\r\nsisters near the station, Monica turned to walk quickly home. She had\r\ngone about half the way, when her name was spoken just behind her, in\r\nWiddowson\xe2\x80\x99s voice. She stopped, and there stood the man, offering his\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you here at this time?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked in an unsteady voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot by chance. I had a hope that I might see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was gloomy, and looked at her searchingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mustn\xe2\x80\x99t wait to talk now, Mr. Widdowson. It\xe2\x80\x99s very late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery late indeed. It surprised me to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurprised you? Why should it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that it seemed so very unlikely\xe2\x80\x94at this hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen how could you have hoped to see me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica walked on, with an air of displeasure, and Widdowson kept beside\r\nher, incessantly eyeing her countenance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t really think of seeing you, Miss Madden. I wished to be\r\nnear the place where you were, that was all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw me come out I dare say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you had done, you would have known that I came to meet two ladies,\r\nmy sisters. I walked with them to the station, and now I am going home.\r\nYou seem to think an explanation necessary\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo forgive me! What right have I to ask anything of the kind? But I\r\nhave been very restless since Sunday. I wished so to meet you, if only\r\nfor a few minutes. Only an hour or two ago I posted a letter to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was to ask you to meet me next Sunday, as we arranged. Shall you be\r\nable to do so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I can\xe2\x80\x99t. At the end of this week I leave my place here, and\r\non Sunday I shall be moving to another part of London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are leaving? You have decided to make the change you spoke of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you tell me where you are going to live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn lodgings near Great Portland Street. I must say good-night, Mr.\r\nWiddowson. I must, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease\xe2\x80\x94do give me one moment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stay\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94good-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was impossible for him to detain her. Ungracefully he caught at his\r\nhat, made the salute, and moved away with rapid, uneven strides. In\r\nless than half an hour he was back again at this spot. He walked past\r\nthe shop many times without pausing; his eyes devoured the front of the\r\nbuilding, and noted those windows in which there was a glimmer of\r\nlight. He saw girls enter by the private door, but Monica did not again\r\nshow herself. Some time after midnight, when the house had long been\r\ndark and perfectly quiet, the uneasy man took a last look, and then\r\nsought a cab to convey him home.\r\n\r\nThe letter of which he had spoken reached Monica\xe2\x80\x99s hands next morning.\r\nIt was a very respectful invitation to accompany the writer on a drive\r\nin Surrey. Widdowson proposed to meet her at Herne Hill railway\r\nstation, where his vehicle would be waiting. \xe2\x80\x9cIn passing, I shall be\r\nable to point out to you the house which has been my home for about a\r\nyear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs circumstances were, it would be hardly possible to accept this\r\ninvitation without exciting curiosity in her sisters. The Sunday\r\nmorning would be occupied, probably, in going to the new lodgings and\r\nmaking the acquaintance of her future companion there; in the\r\nafternoon, her sisters were to pay her a visit, as Alice had decided\r\nto start for Somerset on the Monday. She must write a refusal, but it\r\nwas by no means her wish to discourage Widdowson altogether. The note\r\nwhich at length satisfied her ran thus:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDEAR MR. WIDDOWSON\xe2\x80\x94I am very sorry that it will be impossible for me\r\nto see you next Sunday. All day I shall be occupied. My eldest sister\r\nis leaving London, and Sunday will be my last day with her, perhaps for\r\na long time. Please do not think that I make light of your kindness.\r\nWhen I am settled in my new life, I hope to be able to let you know how\r\nit suits me.\xe2\x80\x94Sincerely yours,\r\n\r\nMONICA MADDEN.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a postscript she mentioned her new address. It was written in very\r\nsmall characters\xe2\x80\x94perhaps an unpurposed indication of the misgiving\r\nwith which she allowed herself to pen the words.\r\n\r\nTwo days went by, and again a letter from Widdowson was delivered,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDEAR MISS MADDEN\xe2\x80\x94My chief purpose in writing again so soon is to\r\napologize sincerely for my behaviour on Tuesday evening. It was quite\r\nunjustifiable. The best way of confessing my fault is to own that I had\r\na foolish dislike of your walking in the streets unaccompanied at so\r\nlate an hour. I believe that any man who had newly made your\r\nacquaintance, and had thought as much about you as I have, would have\r\nexperienced the same feeling. The life which made it impossible for you\r\nto see friends at any other time of the day was so evidently unsuited\r\nto one of your refinement that I was made angry by the thought of it.\r\nHappily it is coming to an end, and I shall be greatly relieved when I\r\nknow that you have left the house of business.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remember that we are to be friends. I should be much less than\r\nyour friend if I did not desire for you a position very different from\r\nthat which necessity forced upon you. Thank you very much for the\r\npromise to tell me how you like the new employment and your new\r\nfriends. Shall you not henceforth be at leisure on other days besides\r\nSunday? As you will now be near Regent\xe2\x80\x99s Park, perhaps I may hope to\r\nmeet you there some evening before long. I would go any distance to see\r\nyou and speak with you for only a few minutes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo forgive my impertinence, and believe me, dear Miss Madden.\xe2\x80\x94 Ever\r\nyours,\r\n\r\nEDMUND WIDDOWSON.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nNow this undoubtedly might be considered a love-letter, and it was the\r\nfirst of its kind that Monica had ever received. No man had ever\r\nwritten to her that he was willing to go \xe2\x80\x9cany distance\xe2\x80\x9d for the reward\r\nof looking on her face. She read the composition many times, and with\r\nmany thoughts. It did not enchant her; presently she felt it to be dull\r\nand prosy\xe2\x80\x94anything but the ideal of a love-letter, even at this early\r\nstage.\r\n\r\nThe remarks concerning Widdowson made in the bedroom by the girl who\r\nfancied her asleep had greatly disturbed her conception of him. He was\r\nold, and looked still older to a casual eye. He had a stiff dry way,\r\nand already had begun to show how precise and exacting he could be. A\r\nyear or two ago the image of such a man would have repelled her. She\r\ndid not think it possible to regard him with warm feelings; yet, if he\r\nasked her to marry him\xe2\x80\x94and that seemed likely to happen very\r\nsoon\xe2\x80\x94almost certainly her answer would be yes. Provided, of course,\r\nthat all he had told her about himself could be in some satisfactory\r\nway confirmed.\r\n\r\nHer acquaintance with him was an extraordinary thing. With what\r\namazement and rapture would any one of her shop companions listen to\r\nthe advances of a man who had six hundred a year! Yet Monica did not\r\ndoubt his truthfulness and the honesty of his intentions. His\r\nlife-story sounded credible enough, and the very dryness of his manner\r\ninspired confidence. As things went in the marriage war, she might\r\nesteem herself a most fortunate young woman. It seemed that he had\r\nreally fallen in love with her; he might prove a devoted husband. She\r\nfelt no love in return; but between the prospect of a marriage of\r\nesteem and that of no marriage at all there was little room for\r\nhesitation. The chances were that she might never again receive an\r\noffer from a man whose social standing she could respect.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime there had come a civil little note from the girl whose\r\nrooms she was to share. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Barfoot has spoken of you so favourably\r\nthat I did not think it necessary to see you before consenting to what\r\nshe suggested. Perhaps she has told you that I have my own furniture;\r\nit is very plain, but, I think, comfortable. For the two rooms, with\r\nattendance, I pay eight and sixpence a week; my landlady will ask\r\neleven shillings when there are two of us, so that your share would be\r\nfive and six. I hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t think this is too much. I am a quiet and\r\nI think a very reasonable person.\xe2\x80\x9d The signature was \xe2\x80\x9cMildred H.\r\nVesper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe day of release arrived. As it poured with rain all the morning,\r\nMonica the less regretted that she had been obliged to postpone her\r\nmeeting with Widdowson. At breakfast-time she said good-bye to the\r\nthree or four girls in whom she had any interest. Miss Eade was\r\ndelighted to see her go. This rival finally out of the way, Mr.\r\nBullivant might perchance turn his attention to the faithful admirer\r\nwho remained.\r\n\r\nShe went by train to Great Portland Street, and thence by cab, with her\r\ntwo boxes, to Rutland Street, Hampstead Road\xe2\x80\x94an uphill little street\r\nof small houses. When the cab stopped, the door of the house she sought\r\nat once opened, and on the threshold appeared a short, prim,\r\nplain-featured girl, who smiled a welcome.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are Miss Vesper?\xe2\x80\x9d Monica said, approaching her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94very pleased to see you, Miss Madden. As London cabmen have a\r\nnarrow view of their duties, I\xe2\x80\x99ll help you to get the boxes in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica liked the girl at once. Jehu condescending to hand down the\r\nluggage, they transferred it to the foot of the staircase, then, the\r\nfare having been paid, went up to the second floor, which was the top\r\nof the house. Miss Vesper\xe2\x80\x99s two rooms were very humble, but homely. She\r\nlooked at Monica to remark the impression produced by them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill it do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very nicely indeed. After my quarters in Walworth Road! But I feel\r\nashamed to intrude upon you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been trying to find someone to share my rent,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other,\r\nwith a simple frankness that was very agreeable. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Barfoot was full\r\nof your praises\xe2\x80\x94and indeed I think we may suit each other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall try to be as little disturbance to you as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I to you. The street is a very quiet one. Up above here is\r\nCumberland Market; a hay and straw market. Quite pleasant\r\nodours\xe2\x80\x94country odours\xe2\x80\x94reach us on market day. I am country-bred;\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s why I speak of such a trifle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica. \xe2\x80\x9cI come from Somerset.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I from Hampshire. Do you know, I have a strong suspicion that all\r\nthe really nice girls in London _are_ country girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica had to look at the speaker to be sure that this was said in\r\npleasantry. Miss Vesper was fond of making dry little jokes in the\r\ngravest tone; only a twinkle of her eyes and a movement of her tight\r\nlittle lips betrayed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I ask the landlady to help me up with the luggage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are rather pale, Miss Madden. Better let me see to that. I have to\r\ngo down to remind Mrs. Hocking to put salt into the saucepan with the\r\npotatoes. She cooks for me only on Sunday, and if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t remind her\r\nevery week she would boil the potatoes without salt. Such a state of\r\nmind is curious, but one ends by accepting it as a fact in nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey joined in merry laughter. When Miss Vesper gave way to open mirth,\r\nshe enjoyed it so thoroughly that it was a delight to look at her.\r\n\r\nBy the time dinner was over they were on excellent terms, and had\r\nexchanged a great deal of personal information. Mildred Vesper seemed\r\nto be one of the most contented of young women. She had sisters and\r\nbrothers, whom she loved, all scattered about England in pursuit of a\r\nlivelihood; it was rare for any two of them to see each other, but she\r\nspoke of this as quite in the order of things. For Miss Barfoot her\r\nrespect was unbounded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe had made more of me than any one else could have done. When I\r\nfirst met her, three years ago, I was a simpleton; I thought myself\r\nill-used because I had to work hard for next to no payment and live in\r\nsolitude. Now I should be ashamed to complain of what falls to the lot\r\nof thousands of girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you like Miss Nunn?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Monica.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so well as Miss Barfoot, but I think very highly of her. Her zeal\r\nmakes her exaggerate a little now and then, but then the zeal is so\r\nsplendid. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t it myself\xe2\x80\x94not in that form.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that I feel a shameful delight when I hear of a girl getting\r\nmarried. It\xe2\x80\x99s very weak, no doubt; perhaps I shall improve as I grow\r\nolder. But I have half a suspicion, do you know, that Miss Barfoot is\r\nnot without the same weakness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica laughed, and spoke of something else. She was in good spirits;\r\nalready her companion\xe2\x80\x99s view of life began to have an effect upon her;\r\nshe thought of people and things in a more lightsome way, and was less\r\ndisposed to commiserate herself.\r\n\r\nThe bedroom which both were to occupy might with advantage have been\r\nlarger, but they knew that many girls of instinct no less delicate than\r\ntheir own had to endure far worse accommodation in London\xe2\x80\x94where\r\npoverty pays for its sheltered breathing-space at so much a square\r\nfoot. It was only of late that Miss Vesper had been able to buy\r\nfurniture (four sovereigns it cost in all), and thus to allow herself\r\nthe luxury of two rooms at the rent she previously paid for one. Miss\r\nBarfoot did not remunerate her workers on a philanthropic scale, but\r\nstrictly in accordance with market prices; common sense dictated this\r\nprinciple. In talking over their arrangements, Monica decided to expend\r\na few shillings on the purchase of a chair-bedstead for her own use.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI often have nightmares,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cand kick a great deal. It\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be nice to give you bruises.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA week passed. Alice had written from Yatton, and in a cheerful tone.\r\nVirginia, chronically excited, had made calls at Rutland Street and at\r\nQueen\xe2\x80\x99s Road; she talked like one who had suddenly received a great\r\nillumination, and her zeal in the cause of independent womanhood\r\nrivalled Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s. Without enthusiasm, but seemingly contented,\r\nMonica worked at the typewriting machine, and had begun certain studies\r\nwhich her friends judged to be useful. She experienced a growth of\r\nself-respect. It was much to have risen above the status of shop-girl,\r\nand the change of moral atmosphere had a very beneficial effect upon\r\nher.\r\n\r\nMildred Vesper was a studious little person, after a fashion of her\r\nown. She possessed four volumes of Maunder\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cTreasuries,\xe2\x80\x9d and to one\r\nor other of these she applied herself for at least an hour every\r\nevening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy nature,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, when Monica sought an explanation of this study,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmy mind is frivolous. What I need is a store of solid information, to\r\nreflect upon. No one could possibly have a worse memory, but by\r\npersevering I manage to learn one or two facts a day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica glanced at the books now and then, but had no desire to\r\ncultivate Maunder\xe2\x80\x99s acquaintance. Instead of reading, she meditated the\r\nproblems of her own life.\r\n\r\nEdmund Widdowson, of course, wrote to her at the new address. In her\r\nreply she again postponed their meeting. Whenever she went out in the\r\nevening, it was with expectation of seeing him somewhere in the\r\nneighbourhood; she felt assured that he had long ago come to look at\r\nthe house, and more likely than not his eyes had several times been\r\nupon her. That did not matter; her life was innocent, and Widdowson\r\nmight watch her coming and going as much as he would.\r\n\r\nAt length, about nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock one evening, she came face to face with\r\nhim. It was in Hampstead Road; she had been buying at a draper\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\ncarried the little parcel. At the moment of recognition, Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nface so flushed and brightened that Monica could not help a sympathetic\r\nfeeling of pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you so cruel to me?\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a low voice, as she gave her\r\nhand. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a time since I saw you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that really true?\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, with an air more resembling\r\ncoquetry than any he had yet seen in her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince I spoke to you, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you see me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree evenings ago. You were walking in Tottenham Court Road with a\r\nyoung lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Vesper, the friend I live with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you give me a few minutes now?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked humbly. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it too late?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor reply Monica moved slowly on. They turned up one of the ways\r\nparallel with Rutland Street, and so came into the quiet district that\r\nskirts Regent\xe2\x80\x99s Park, Widdowson talking all the way in a strain of all\r\nbut avowed tenderness, his head bent towards her and his voice so much\r\nsubdued that occasionally she lost a few words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t live without seeing you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at length. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you refuse to\r\nmeet me, I have no choice but to come wandering about the places where\r\nyou are. Don\xe2\x80\x99t, pray don\xe2\x80\x99t think I spy upon you. Indeed, it is only\r\njust to see your face or your form as you walk along. When I have had\r\nmy journey in vain I go back in misery. You are never out of my\r\nthoughts\xe2\x80\x94never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for that, Mr. Widdowson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSorry? Are you really sorry? Do you think of me with less friendliness\r\nthan when we had our evening on the river?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, not with less friendliness. But if I only make you unhappy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn one way unhappy, but as no one else ever had the power to. If you\r\nwould let me meet you at certain times my restlessness would be at an\r\nend. The summer is going so quickly. Won\xe2\x80\x99t you come for that drive with\r\nme next Sunday? I will be waiting for you at any place you like to\r\nappoint. If you could imagine what joy it would give me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently Monica assented. If it were fine, she would be by the\r\nsouth-east entrance to Regent\xe2\x80\x99s Park at two o\xe2\x80\x99clock. He thanked her with\r\nwords of the most submissive gratitude, and then they parted.\r\n\r\nThe day proved doubtful, but she kept her appointment. Widdowson was on\r\nthe spot with horse and trap. These were not, as he presently informed\r\nMonica, his own property, but hired from a livery stable, according to\r\nhis custom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t rain,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, gazing at the sky. \xe2\x80\x9cIt _shan\xe2\x80\x99t_ rain!\r\nThese few hours are too precious to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be very awkward if it _did_,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica replied, in merry\r\nhumour, as they drove along.\r\n\r\nThe sky threatened till sundown, but Widdowson was able to keep\r\ndeclaring that rain would not come. He took a south-westward course,\r\ncrossed Waterloo Bridge, and thence by the highways made for Herne\r\nHill. Monica observed that he made a short detour to avoid Walworth\r\nRoad. She asked his reason.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate the road!\xe2\x80\x9d Widdowson answered, with vehemence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou hate it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you slaved and suffered there. If I had the power, I would\r\ndestroy it\xe2\x80\x94every house. Many a time,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, in a lower voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhen you were lying asleep, I walked up and down there in horrible\r\nmisery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust because I had to stand at a counter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot only that. It wasn\xe2\x80\x99t fit for you to work in that way\xe2\x80\x94but the\r\npeople about you! I hated every face of man or woman that passed along\r\nthe street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should hope not. Of course, I know you didn\xe2\x80\x99t. Why did you ever come\r\nto such a place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was severity rather than sympathy in his look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was tired of the dull country life,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica replied frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nthen I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what the shops and the people were like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you need a life of excitement?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, with a sidelong glance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcitement? No, but one must have change.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they reached Herne Hill, Widdowson became silent, and presently he\r\nallowed the horse to walk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my house, Miss Madden\xe2\x80\x94the right-hand one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica looked, and saw two little villas, built together with stone\r\nfacings, porches at the doors and ornamented gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only wanted to show it you,\xe2\x80\x9d he added quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing\r\npretty or noticeable about it, and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t at all grandly furnished.\r\nMy old housekeeper and one servant manage to keep it in order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey passed, and Monica did not allow herself to look back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it\xe2\x80\x99s a nice house,\xe2\x80\x9d she said presently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll my life I have wished to have a house of my own, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t dare\r\nto hope I ever should. Men in general don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to care so long as\r\nthey have lodgings that suit them\xe2\x80\x94I mean unmarried men. But I always\r\nwanted to live alone\xe2\x80\x94without strangers, that is to say. I told you\r\nthat I am not very sociable. When I got my house, I was like a child\r\nwith a toy; I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep for satisfaction. I used to walk all over\r\nit, day after day, before it was furnished. There was something that\r\ndelighted me in the sound of my footsteps on the staircases and the\r\nbare floors. Here I shall live and die, I kept saying to myself. Not in\r\nsolitude, I hoped. Perhaps I might meet some one\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica interrupted him to ask a question about some object in the\r\nlandscape. He answered her very briefly, and for a long time neither\r\nspoke. Then the girl, glancing at him with a smile of apology, said in\r\na gentle tone\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were telling me how the house pleased you. Have you still the same\r\npleasure in living there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. But lately I have been hoping\xe2\x80\x94I daren\xe2\x80\x99t say more. You will\r\ninterrupt me again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich way are we going now, Mr. Widdowson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Streatham, then on to Carshalton. At five o\xe2\x80\x99clock we will use our\r\nright as travellers, and get some innkeeper to make tea for us. Look,\r\nthe sun is trying to break through; we shall have a fine evening yet.\r\nMay I, without rudeness, say that you look better since you left that\r\nabominable place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I feel better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter keeping his look fixed for a long time on the horse\xe2\x80\x99s ears,\r\nWiddowson turned gravely to his companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you about my sister-in-law. Would you be willing to make her\r\nacquaintance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t feel able to do that, Mr. Widdowson,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica answered with\r\ndecision.\r\n\r\nPrepared for this reply, he began a long and urgent persuasion. It was\r\nuseless; Monica listened quietly, but without sign of yielding. The\r\nsubject dropped, and they talked of indifferent things.\r\n\r\nOn the homeward drive, when the dull sky grew dusk about them, and the\r\nsuburban street-lamps began to show themselves in long glimmering\r\nlines, Widdowson returned with shamefaced courage to the subject which\r\nfor some hours had been in abeyance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t part from you this evening without a word of hope to remember.\r\nYou know that I want you to be my wife. Will you tell me if there is\r\nanything I can say or do to make your consent possible? Have you any\r\ndoubt of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt whatever of your sincerity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn one sense, I am still a stranger to you. Will you give me the\r\nopportunity of making things between us more regular? Will you allow me\r\nto meet some friend of yours whom you trust?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had rather you didn\xe2\x80\x99t yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wish to know still more of me, personally?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I think I must know you much better before I can consent to any\r\nstep of that kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d he urged, \xe2\x80\x9cif we became acquaintances in the ordinary way, and\r\nknew each other\xe2\x80\x99s friends, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t that be most satisfactory to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might be. But you forget that so much would have to be explained. I\r\nhave behaved very strangely. If I told everything to my friends I\r\nshould leave myself no choice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, why not? You would be absolutely free. I could no more than try to\r\nrecommend myself to you. If I am so unhappy as to fail, how would you\r\nbe anything but quite free?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you must understand me. In this position, I must either not\r\nspeak of you at all, or make it known that I am engaged to you. I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave it taken for granted that I am engaged to you when I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to\r\nbe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWiddowson\xe2\x80\x99s head drooped; he set his lips in a hard gloomy expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have behaved very imprudently,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the girl. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t see\xe2\x80\x94what else I could have done. Things are so badly\r\narranged. It wasn\xe2\x80\x99t possible for us to be introduced by any one who\r\nknew us both, so I had either to break off your acquaintance after that\r\nfirst conversation, or conduct myself as I have been doing. I think\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s a very hard position. My sisters would call me an immodest girl,\r\nbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is true. I may perhaps come to feel you as a girl\r\nought to when she marries, and how else can I tell unless I meet you\r\nand talk with you? And your position is just the same. I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame\r\nyou for a moment; I think it would be ridiculous to blame you. Yet we\r\nhave gone against the ordinary rule, and people would make us suffer\r\nfor it\xe2\x80\x94or me, at all events.\r\n\r\nHer voice at the close was uncertain. Widdowson looked at her with eyes\r\nof passionate admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you for saying that\xe2\x80\x94for putting it so well, and so kindly for\r\nme. Let us disregard people, then. Let us go on seeing each other. I\r\nlove you with all my soul\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he choked a little at this first utterance\r\nof the solemn word\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand your rules shall be mine. Give me a chance of\r\nwinning you. Tell me if I offend you in anything\xe2\x80\x94if there\xe2\x80\x99s anything\r\nyou dislike in me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you cease coming to look for me when I don\xe2\x80\x99t know of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI promise you. I will never come again. And you will meet me a little\r\noftener?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will see you once every week. But I must still be perfectly free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly! I will only try to win you as any man may who loves a\r\nwoman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tired horse clattered upon the hard highway and clouds gathered for\r\na night of storm.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\nCOUSIN EVERARD\r\n\r\n\r\nAs Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s eye fell on the letters brought to her at\r\nbreakfast-time, she uttered an exclamation, doubtful in its\r\nsignificance. Rhoda Nunn, who rarely had a letter from any one, looked\r\nup inquiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am greatly mistaken if that isn\xe2\x80\x99t my cousin Everard\xe2\x80\x99s writing. I\r\nthought so. He is in London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda made no remark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray read it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other, handing her friend the epistle after\r\nshe had gone through it.\r\n\r\nThe handwriting was remarkably bold, but careful. Punctuation was\r\nstrictly attended to, and in places a word had been obliterated with a\r\ncircular scrawl which left it still legible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDEAR COUSIN MARY,\xe2\x80\x94I hear that you are still active in an original\r\nway, and that civilization is more and more indebted to you. Since my\r\narrival in London a few weeks ago, I have several times been on the\r\npoint of calling at your house, but scruples withheld me. Our last\r\ninterview was not quite friendly on your side, you will remember, and\r\nperhaps your failure to write to me means continued displeasure; in\r\nthat case I might be rejected at your door, which I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like, for\r\nI am troubled with a foolish sense of personal dignity. I have taken a\r\nflat, and mean to stay in London for at least half a year. Please let\r\nme know whether I may see you. Indeed I should like to. Nature meant us\r\nfor good friends, but prejudice came between us. Just a line, either of\r\nwelcome or \xe2\x80\x9cget thee behind me!\xe2\x80\x9d In spite of your censures, I always\r\nwas, and still am, affectionately yours,\r\n\r\nEVERARD BARFOOT.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda perused the sheet very attentively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn impudent letter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Barfoot. \xe2\x80\x9cJust like him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere does he appear from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJapan, I suppose. \xe2\x80\x9cBut prejudice came between us.\xe2\x80\x9d I like that! Moral\r\nconviction is always prejudice in the eyes of these advanced young men.\r\nOf course he must come. I am anxious to see what time has made of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it really moral censure that kept you from writing to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ninquired Rhoda, with a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDecidedly. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t approve of him at all, as I have frequently told\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I gather that he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t changed much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in theories,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Miss Barfoot. \xe2\x80\x9cThat isn\xe2\x80\x99t to be expected. He\r\nis far too stubborn. But in mode of life he may possibly be more\r\ntolerable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter two or three years in Japan,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Rhoda, with a slight\r\nraising of the eyebrows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is about three-and-thirty, and before he left England I think he\r\nshowed possibilities of future wisdom. Of course I disapprove of him,\r\nand, if necessary, shall let him understand that quite as plainly as\r\nbefore. But there\xe2\x80\x99s no harm in seeing if he has learnt to behave\r\nhimself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard Barfoot received an invitation to dine. It was promptly\r\naccepted, and on the evening of the appointment he arrived at half-past\r\nseven. His cousin sat alone in the drawing-room. At his entrance she\r\nregarded him with keen but friendly scrutiny.\r\n\r\nHe had a tall, muscular frame, and a head of striking outline, with\r\nlarge nose, full lips, deep-set eyes, and prominent eyebrows. His hair\r\nwas the richest tone of chestnut; his moustache and beard\xe2\x80\x94the latter\r\npeaking slightly forward\xe2\x80\x94inclined to redness. Excellent health\r\nmanifested itself in the warm purity of his skin, in his cheerful\r\naspect, and the lightness of his bearing. The lower half of his\r\nforehead was wrinkled, and when he did not fix his look on anything in\r\nparticular, his eyelids drooped, giving him for the moment an air of\r\nlanguor. On sitting down, he at once abandoned himself to a posture of\r\nthe completest ease, which his admirable proportions made graceful.\r\nFrom his appearance one would have expected him to speak in rather loud\r\nand decided tones; but he had a soft voice, and used it with all the\r\ndiscretion of good breeding, so that at times it seemed to caress the\r\near. To this mode of utterance corresponded his smile, which was\r\nfrequent, but restrained to the expression of a delicate, good-natured\r\nirony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one had told me of your return,\xe2\x80\x9d were Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s first words as\r\nshe shook hands with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy because no one knew. You were the first of my kinsfolk to whom\r\nI wrote.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch honour, Everard. You look very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to be able to say the same of you. And yet I hear that you\r\nwork harder than ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is the source of your information about me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had an account of you from Tom, in a letter that caught me at\r\nConstantinople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom? I thought he had forgotten my existence. Who told him about me I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t imagine. So you didn\xe2\x80\x99t come straight home from Japan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarfoot was nursing his knee, his head thrown back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I loitered a little in Egypt and Turkey. Are you living quite\r\nalone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drawled slightly on the last word, its second vowel making quite a\r\nmusical note, of wonderful expressiveness. The clear decision of his\r\ncousin\xe2\x80\x99s reply was a sharp contrast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA lady lives with me\xe2\x80\x94Miss Nunn. She will join us in a moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Nunn?\xe2\x80\x9d He smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cA partner in your activity?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe gives me valuable help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must hear all about it\xe2\x80\x94if you will kindly tell me some day. It will\r\ninterest me greatly. You always were the most interesting of our\r\nfamily. Brother Tom promised to be a genius, but marriage has blighted\r\nthe hope, I fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe marriage was a very absurd one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it? I feared so; but Tom seems satisfied. I suppose they will stay\r\nat Madeira.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUntil his wife is tired of her imaginary phthisis, and amuses herself\r\nwith imagining some other ailment that requires them to go to Siberia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that kind of person, is she?\xe2\x80\x9d He smiled indulgently, and played\r\nfor a moment with the lobe of his right ear. His ears were small, and\r\nof the ideal contour; the hand, too, thus displayed, was a fine example\r\nof blended strength and elegance.\r\n\r\nRhoda came in, so quietly that she was able to observe the guest before\r\nhe had detected her presence. The movement of Miss Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s eyes first\r\ninformed him that another person was in the room. In the quietest\r\npossible way the introduction was performed, and all seated themselves.\r\n\r\nDressed, like the hostess, in black, and without ornaments of any kind\r\nsave a silver buckle at her waist, Rhoda seemed to have endeavoured to\r\nliken herself to the suggestion of her name by the excessive plainness\r\nwith which she had arranged her hair; its tight smoothness was nothing\r\nlike so becoming as the mode she usually adopted, and it made her look\r\nolder. Whether by accident or design, she took an upright chair, and\r\nsat upon it in a stiff attitude. Finding it difficult to suspect Rhoda\r\nof shyness, Miss Barfoot once or twice glanced at her with curiosity.\r\nFor settled conversation there was no time; a servant announced dinner\r\nalmost immediately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere shall be no forms, cousin Everard,\xe2\x80\x9d said the hostess. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease to\r\nfollow us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDoing so, Everard examined Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s figure, which in its way was\r\nstrong and shapely as his own. A motion of his lips indicated amused\r\napproval, but at once he commanded himself, and entered the dining-room\r\nwith exemplary gravity. Naturally, he sat opposite Rhoda, and his eyes\r\noften skimmed her face; when she spoke, which was very seldom, he gazed\r\nat her with close attention.\r\n\r\nDuring the first part of the meal, Miss Barfoot questioned her relative\r\nconcerning his Oriental experiences. Everard spoke of them in a light,\r\nagreeable way, avoiding the tone of instruction, and, in short, giving\r\nevidence of good taste. Rhoda listened with a look of civil interest,\r\nbut asked no question, and smiled only when it was unavoidable.\r\nPresently the talk turned to things of home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you heard of your friend Mr. Poppleton?\xe2\x80\x9d the hostess asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoppleton? Nothing whatever. I should like to see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry to tell you he is in a lunatic asylum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Barfoot kept the silence of astonishment, his cousin went on to tell\r\nhim that the unhappy man seemed to have lost his wits among business\r\ntroubles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet I should have suggested another explanation,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the young\r\nman, in his most discreet tone, \xe2\x80\x9cYou never met Mrs. Poppleton?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeeing that Miss Nunn had looked up with interest, he addressed himself\r\nto her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend Poppleton was one of the most delightful men\xe2\x80\x94perhaps the\r\nbest and kindest I ever knew, and so overflowing with natural wit and\r\nhumour that there was no resisting his cheerful influence. To the\r\namazement of every one who knew him, he married perhaps the dullest\r\nwoman he could have found. Mrs. Poppleton not only never made a joke,\r\nbut couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand what joking meant. Only the flattest literalism\r\nwas intelligible to her; she could follow nothing but the very macadam\r\nof conversation\xe2\x80\x94had no palate for anything but the suet-pudding of\r\ntalk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda\xe2\x80\x99s eyes twinkled, and Miss Barfoot laughed. Everard was allowing\r\nhimself a freedom in expression which hitherto he had sedulously\r\navoided.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cshe was by birth a lady\xe2\x80\x94which made the\r\ninfliction harder to bear. Poor old Poppleton! Again and again I have\r\nheard him\xe2\x80\x94what do you think?\xe2\x80\x94laboriously _explaining_ jests to her.\r\nThat was a trial, as you may imagine. There we sat, we three, in the\r\nunbeautiful little parlour\xe2\x80\x94for they were anything but rich. Poppleton\r\nwould say something that convulsed me with laughter\xe2\x80\x94in spite of my\r\nefforts, for I always dreaded the result so much that I strove my\r\nhardest to do no more than smile appreciation. My laugh compelled Mrs.\r\nPoppleton to stare at me\xe2\x80\x94oh, her eyes! Thereupon, her husband began\r\nhis dread performance. The patience, the heroic patience, of that dear,\r\ngood fellow! I have known him explain, and re-explain, for a quarter of\r\nan hour, and invariably without success. It might be a mere pun; Mrs.\r\nPoppleton no more understood the nature of a pun than of the binomial\r\ntheorem. But worse was when the jest involved some allusion. When I\r\nheard Poppleton begin to elucidate, to expound, the perspiration\r\nalready on his forehead, I looked at him with imploring anguish. Why\r\n_would_ he attempt the impossible? But the kind fellow couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndisregard his wife\xe2\x80\x99s request. Shall I ever forget her. \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94yes\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nsee\xe2\x80\x9d?\xe2\x80\x94when obviously she saw nothing but the wall at which she sat\r\nstaring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have known her like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Barfoot merrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am convinced his madness didn\xe2\x80\x99t come from business anxiety. It was\r\nthe necessity, ever recurring, ever before him, of expounding jokes to\r\nhis wife. Believe me, it was nothing but that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems very probable,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Rhoda dryly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen there\xe2\x80\x99s another friend of yours whose marriage has been\r\nunfortunate,\xe2\x80\x9d said the hostess. \xe2\x80\x9cThey tell me that Mr. Orchard has\r\nforsaken his wife, and without intelligible reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, too, I can offer an explanation,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Barfoot quietly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthough you may doubt whether it justifies him. I met Orchard a few\r\nmonths ago in Alexandria, met him by chance in the street, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nrecognize him until he spoke to me. He was worn to skin and bone. I\r\nfound that he had abandoned all his possessions to Mrs. Orchard, and\r\njust kept himself alive on casual work for the magazines, wandering\r\nabout the shores of the Mediterranean like an uneasy spirit. He showed\r\nme the thing he had last written, and I see it is published in this\r\nmonth\xe2\x80\x99s _Macmillan_. Do read it. An exquisite description of a night in\r\nAlexandria. One of these days he will starve to death. A pity; he might\r\nhave done fine work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we await your explanation. What business has he to desert his wife\r\nand children?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me give an account of a day I spent with him at Tintern, not long\r\nbefore I left England. He and his wife were having a holiday there, and\r\nI called on them. We went to walk about the Abbey. Now, for some two\r\nhours\xe2\x80\x94I will be strictly truthful\xe2\x80\x94whilst we were in the midst of that\r\nlovely scenery, Mrs. Orchard discoursed unceasingly of one subject\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndifficulty she had with her domestic servants. Ten or twelve of these\r\nhandmaidens were marshalled before our imagination; their names, their\r\nages, their antecedents, the wages they received, were carefully\r\nspecified. We listened to a _catalogue raisonne_ of the plates, cups,\r\nand other utensils that they had broken. We heard of the enormities\r\nwhich in each case led to their dismissal. Orchard tried repeatedly to\r\nchange the subject, but only with the effect of irritating his wife.\r\nWhat could he or I do but patiently give ear? Our walk was ruined, but\r\nthere was no help for it. Now, be good enough to extend this kind of\r\nthing over a number of years. Picture Orchard sitting down in his home\r\nto literary work, and liable at any moment to an invasion from Mrs.\r\nOrchard, who comes to tell him, at great length, that the butcher has\r\ncharged for a joint they have not consumed\xe2\x80\x94or something of that kind.\r\nHe assured me that his choice lay between flight and suicide, and I\r\nfirmly believed him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he concluded, his eyes met those of Miss Nunn, and the latter\r\nsuddenly spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy will men marry fools?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarfoot was startled. The\r\nclaret had no particular quality to recommend it, and in any case he\r\nwould have drunk very little, for as regards the bottle his nature was\r\nabstemious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is as I expected,\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Barfoot was saying to her friend in the\r\ndrawing-room. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has changed very noticeably.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Barfoot isn\xe2\x80\x99t quite the man your remarks had suggested to me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nRhoda replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy he is no longer the man I knew. Which?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo young, of course. You deliberately intend to waste your life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo enjoy it, I said. I am not prompted to any business or profession;\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s all over for me; I have learnt all I care to of the active\r\nworld.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what do you understand by enjoyment?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss Barfoot, with\r\nknitted brows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t the spectacle of existence quite enough to occupy one through a\r\nlifetime?
