From quite a child, Everard was at odds with his\r\nfather. A strange thing, for in so many respects they resembled each\r\nother very closely. Physically, Everard is his father walking the earth\r\nagain. In character, too, I think they must be very much alike. They\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t talk about the simplest thing without disagreeing. My uncle\r\nhad risen from the ranks but he disliked to be reminded of it. He\r\ndisliked the commerce by which he made his fortune. His desire was to\r\nwin social position; if baronetcies could be purchased in our time, he\r\nwould have given a huge sum to acquire one. But he never distinguished\r\nhimself, and one of the reasons was, no doubt, that he married too\r\nsoon. I have heard him speak bitterly, and very indiscreetly, of early\r\nmarriages; his wife was dead then, but every one knew what he meant.\r\nRhoda, when one thinks how often a woman is a clog upon a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nambition, no wonder they regard us as they do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, women are always retarding one thing or another. But men\r\nare intensely stupid not to have remedied that long ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe determined that his boys should be gentlemen. Tom, the elder,\r\nfollowed his wishes exactly; he was remarkably clever, but idleness\r\nspoilt him, and now he has made that ridiculous marriage\xe2\x80\x94the end of\r\npoor Tom. Everard went to Eton, and the school had a remarkable effect\r\nupon him; it made him a furious Radical. Instead of imitating the young\r\naristocrats he hated and scorned them. There must have been great force\r\nof originality in the boy. Of course I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether any Etonians\r\nof his time preached Radicalism, but it seems unlikely. I think it was\r\nsheer vigour of character, and the strange desire to oppose his father\r\nin everything. From Eton he was of course to pass to Oxford, but at\r\nthat stage came practical rebellion. No, said the boy; he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\nto a university, to fill his head with useless learning; he had made up\r\nhis mind to be an engineer. This was an astonishment to every one;\r\nengineering didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem at all the thing for him; he had very little\r\nability in mathematics, and his bent had always been to liberal\r\nstudies. But nothing could shake his idea. He had got it into his head\r\nthat only some such work as engineering\xe2\x80\x94something of a practical kind,\r\nthat called for strength and craftsmanship\xe2\x80\x94was worthy of a man with\r\nhis opinions. He would rank with the classes that keep the world going\r\nwith their sturdy toil: that was how he spoke. And, after a great\r\nfight, he had his way. He left Eton to study civil engineering.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda was listening with an amused smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued her friend, \xe2\x80\x9ccame another display of firmness or\r\nobstinacy, whichever you like to call it. He soon found out that he had\r\nmade a complete mistake. The studies didn\xe2\x80\x99t suit him at all, as others\r\nhad foreseen. But he would have worked himself to death rather than\r\nconfess his error; none of us knew how he was feeling till long after.\r\nEngineering he had chosen, and an engineer he would be, cost him what\r\neffort it might. His father shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t triumph over him. And from the\r\nage of eighteen till nearly thirty he stuck to a profession which I am\r\nsure he loathed. By force of resolve he even got on to it, and reached\r\na good position with the firm he worked for. Of course his father\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t assist him with money after he came of age; he had to make his\r\nway just like any young man who has no influence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll this puts him in quite another light,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Rhoda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it would be all very well, if there were no vices to add to the\r\npicture. I never experienced such a revulsion of feeling as the day\r\nwhen I learnt shameful things about Everard. You know, I always\r\nregarded him as a boy, and very much as if he had been my younger\r\nbrother; then came the shock\xe2\x80\x94a shock that had a great part in shaping\r\nmy life thenceforward. Since, I have thought of him as I have spoken of\r\nhim to you\xe2\x80\x94as an illustration of evils we have to combat. A man of the\r\nworld would tell you that I grossly magnified trifles; it is very\r\nlikely that Everard was on a higher moral level than most men. But I\r\nshall never forgive him for destroying my faith in his honour and\r\nnobility of feeling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda had a puzzled look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps even now you are unintentionally misleading me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave supposed him an outrageous profligate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was vicious and cowardly\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t say any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that was the immediate cause of his father\xe2\x80\x99s leaving him poorly\r\nprovided for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt had much to do with it, I have no doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see. I imagined that he was cast out of all decent society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf society were really decent, he would have been. It\xe2\x80\x99s strange how\r\ncompletely his Radicalism has disappeared. I believe he never had a\r\ngenuine sympathy with the labouring classes. And what\xe2\x80\x99s more, I fancy\r\nhe had a great deal of his father\xe2\x80\x99s desire for command and social\r\ndistinction. If he had seen his way to become a great engineer, a\r\ndirector of vast enterprises, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have abandoned his work. An\r\nincredible stubbornness has possibly spoilt his whole life. In a\r\ncongenial pursuit he might by this time have attained to something\r\nnoteworthy. It\xe2\x80\x99s too late now, I fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda meditated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he aim at nothing whatever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t admit any ambition. He has no society. His friends are nearly\r\nall obscure people, like those you heard him speak of this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all, what ambition should he have?\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, with a laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s one advantage in being a woman. A woman with brains and will\r\nmay hope to distinguish herself in the greatest movement of our\r\ntime\xe2\x80\x94that of emancipating her sex. But what can a man do, unless he\r\nhas genius?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s the emancipation of the working classes. That is the great\r\nsphere for men; and Everard cares no more for the working classes than\r\nI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it enough to be free oneself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that he has task enough in striving to be an honourable man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps. I hardly know what I meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot mused, and her face lighted up with a glad thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right. It\xe2\x80\x99s better to be a woman, in our day. With us is all\r\nthe joy of advance, the glory of conquering. Men have only material\r\nprogress to think about. But we\xe2\x80\x94we are winning souls, propagating a\r\nnew religion, purifying the earth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda nodded thrice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy cousin is a fine specimen of a man, after all, in body and mind.\r\nBut what a poor, ineffectual creature compared with _you_, Rhoda! I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t flatter you, dear. I tell you bluntly of your faults and\r\nextravagances. But I am proud of your magnificent independence, proud\r\nof your pride, dear, and of your stainless heart. Thank Heaven we are\r\nwomen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was rare indeed for Miss Barfoot to be moved to rhapsody. Again\r\nRhoda nodded, and then they laughed together, with joyous confidence in\r\nthemselves and in their cause.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\nTHE SIMPLE FAITH\r\n\r\n\r\nSeated in the reading-room of a club to which he had newly procured\r\nadmission, Everard Barfoot was glancing over the advertisement columns\r\nof a literary paper. His eye fell on an announcement that had a\r\npersonal interest to him, and at once he went to the writing-table to\r\npen a letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDEAR MICKLETHWAITE,\xe2\x80\x94I am back in England, and ought before this to\r\nhave written to you. I see you have just published a book with an\r\nalarming title, \xe2\x80\x9cA Treatise on Trilinear Co-ordinates.\xe2\x80\x9d My hearty\r\ncongratulations on the completion of such a labour; were you not the\r\nmost disinterested of mortals, I would add a hope that it may somehow\r\nbenefit you financially. I presume there _are_ people who purchase such\r\nworks. But of course the main point with you is to have delivered your\r\nsoul on Trilinear Co-ordinates. Shall I run down to Sheffield to see\r\nyou, or is there any chance of the holidays bringing you this way? I\r\nhave found a cheap flat, poorly furnished, in Bayswater; the man who\r\nlet it to me happens to be an engineer, and is absent on Italian\r\nrailway work for a year or so. My stay in London won\xe2\x80\x99t, I think, be for\r\nlonger than six months, but we must see each other and talk over old\r\ntimes,\xe2\x80\x9d etc.\r\n\r\nThis he addressed to a school at Sheffield. The answer, directed to the\r\nclub, reached him in three days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy DEAR BARFOOT,\xe2\x80\x94I also am in London; your letter has been forwarded\r\nfrom the school, which I quitted last Easter. Disinterested or not, I\r\nam happy to tell you that I have got a vastly better appointment. Let\r\nme know when and where to meet you; or if you like, come to these\r\nlodgings of mine. I don\xe2\x80\x99t enter upon duties till end of October, and am\r\nat present revelling in mathematical freedom. There\xe2\x80\x99s a great deal to\r\ntell.\xe2\x80\x94Sincerely yours,\r\n\r\nTHOMAS MICKLETHWAITE.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving no occupation for his morning, Barfoot went at once to the\r\nobscure little street by Primrose Hill where his friend was lodging. He\r\nreached the house about noon, and, as he had anticipated, found the\r\nmathematician deep in study. Micklethwaite was a man of forty, bent in\r\nthe shoulders, sallow, but not otherwise of unhealthy appearance; he\r\nhad a merry countenance, a great deal of lank, disorderly hair, and a\r\nbeard that reached to the middle of his waistcoat. Everard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nacquaintance with him dated from ten years ago, when Micklethwaite had\r\nacted as his private tutor in mathematics.\r\n\r\nThe room was a musty little back-parlour on the ground floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuiet, perfectly quiet,\xe2\x80\x9d declared its occupant, \xe2\x80\x9cand that\xe2\x80\x99s all I care\r\nfor. Two other lodgers in the house; but they go to business every\r\nmorning at half-past eight, and are in bed by ten at night. Besides,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s only temporary. I have great things in view\xe2\x80\x94portentous changes!\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you all about it presently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe insisted, first of all, on hearing a full account of Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhistory since they both met. They had corresponded about twice a year,\r\nbut Everard was not fond of letter-writing, and on each occasion gave\r\nonly the briefest account of himself. In listening, Micklethwaite\r\nassumed extraordinary positions, the result, presumably, of a need of\r\nphysical exercise after hours spent over his work. Now he stretched\r\nhimself at full length on the edge of his chair, his arms extended\r\nabove him; now he drew up his legs, fixed his feet on the chair, and\r\nlocked his hands round his knees; thus perched, he swayed his body\r\nbackwards and forwards, till it seemed likely that he would pitch head\r\nforemost on to the floor. Barfoot knew these eccentricities of old, and\r\npaid no attention to them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is the appointment you have got?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked at length,\r\ndismissing his own affairs with impatience.\r\n\r\nIt was that of mathematical lecturer at a London college.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have a hundred and fifty a year, and be able to take private\r\npupils. On two hundred, at least, I can count, and there are\r\npossibilities I won\xe2\x80\x99t venture to speak of, because it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do to be\r\ntoo hopeful. Two hundred a year is a great advance for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite enough, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Everard kindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot\xe2\x80\x94not enough. I must make a little more somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHollo! Why this spirit of avarice all at once?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe mathematician gave a shrill, cackling laugh, and rolled upon his\r\nchair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must have more than two hundred. I should be satisfied with _three_\r\nhundred, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll take as much more as I can get.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy revered tutor, this is shameless. I came to pay my respects to a\r\nphilosopher, and I find a sordid worldling. Look at me! I am a man of\r\nthe largest needs, spiritual and physical, yet I make my pittance of\r\nfour hundred and fifty suffice, and never grumble. Perhaps you aim at\r\nan income equal to my own?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do! What\xe2\x80\x99s four hundred and fifty? If you were a man of enterprise\r\nyou would double or treble it. I put a high value on money. I wish to\r\nbe _rich_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are either mad or are going to get married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMicklethwaite cackled louder than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am planning a new algebra for school use. If I\xe2\x80\x99m not much mistaken,\r\nI can turn out something that will supplant all the present books.\r\nThink! If Micklethwaite\xe2\x80\x99s Algebra got accepted in all the schools, what\r\nwould that mean to Mick? Hundreds a year, my boy\xe2\x80\x94hundreds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never knew you so indecent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am renewing my youth. Nay, for the first time I am youthful. I never\r\nhad time for it before. At the age of sixteen I began to teach in a\r\nschool, and ever since I have pegged away at it, school and private.\r\nNow luck has come to me, and I feel five-and-twenty. When I was really\r\nfive-and-twenty, I felt forty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what has that to do with money-making?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter Mick\xe2\x80\x99s Algebra would follow naturally Mick\xe2\x80\x99s Arithmetic, Mick\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nEuclid, Mick\xe2\x80\x99s Trigonometry. Twenty years hence I should have an income\r\nof thousands\xe2\x80\x94thousands! I would then cease to teach (resign my\r\nprofessorship\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, for of course I should be professor),\r\nand devote myself to a great work on Probability. Many a man has begun\r\nthe best of his life at sixty\xe2\x80\x94the most enjoyable part of it, I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarfoot was perplexed. He knew his friend\xe2\x80\x99s turn for humorous\r\nexaggeration, but had never once heard him scheme for material\r\nadvancement, and evidently this present talk meant something more than\r\na jest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I right or not? You are going to get married?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMicklethwaite glanced at the door, then said in a tone of caution,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care to talk about it here. Let us go somewhere and eat\r\ntogether. I invite you to have dinner with me\xe2\x80\x94or lunch, as I suppose\r\nyou would call it, in your aristocratic language.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you had better have lunch with me. Come to my club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfound your impudence! Am I not your father in mathematics?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe so good as to put on a decent pair of trousers, and brush your\r\nhair. Ah, here is your Trilinear production. I\xe2\x80\x99ll look over it whilst\r\nyou make yourself presentable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a bad misprint in the Preface. Let me show you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all the same to me, my dear fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Micklethwaite was not content until he had indicated the error, and\r\nhad talked for five minutes about the absurdities that it involved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you suppose I got the thing published?\xe2\x80\x9d he then asked. \xe2\x80\x9cOld\r\nBennet, the Sheffield headmaster, is security for loss if the book\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t pay for itself in two years\xe2\x80\x99 time. Kind of him, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it? He\r\npressed the offer upon me, and I think he\xe2\x80\x99s prouder of the book than I\r\nam myself. But it\xe2\x80\x99s quite remarkable how kind people are when one is\r\nfortunate. I fancy a great deal of nonsense is talked about the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenviousness. Now as soon as it got known that I was coming to this post\r\nin London, people behaved to me with surprising good nature all round.\r\nOld Bennet talked in quite an affectionate strain. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cI have long known that you ought to be in a better place than\r\nthis; your payment is altogether inadequate; if it had depended upon\r\n_me_, I should long ago have increased it. I truly rejoice that you\r\nhave found a more fitting sphere for your remarkable abilities.\xe2\x80\x9d No; I\r\nmaintain that the world is always ready to congratulate you with\r\nsincerity, if you will only give it a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery gracious of you to give it the chance. But, by-the-bye, how did\r\nit come about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I ought to tell you that. Why, about a year ago, I wrote an\r\nanswer to a communication signed by a Big Gun in one of the scientific\r\npapers. It was a question in Probability\xe2\x80\x94you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand it.\r\nMy answer was printed, and the Big Gun wrote privately to me\xe2\x80\x94a very\r\nflattering letter. That correspondence led to my appointment; the Big\r\nGun exerted himself on my behalf. The fact is, the world is bursting\r\nwith good nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObviously. And how long did it take you to write this little book?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, only about seven years\xe2\x80\x94the actual composition. I never had much\r\ntime to myself, you must remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a good soul, Thomas. Go and equip yourself for civilized\r\nsociety.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo the club they repaired on foot. Micklethwaite would talk of anything\r\nbut that which his companion most desired to hear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are solemnities in life,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered to an impatient question,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthings that can\xe2\x80\x99t be spoken of in the highway. When we have eaten, let\r\nus go to your flat, and there I will tell you everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey lunched joyously. The mathematician drank a bottle of excellent\r\nhock, and did corresponding justice to the dishes. His eyes gleamed\r\nwith happiness; again he enlarged upon the benevolence of mankind, and\r\nthe admirable ordering of the world. From the club they drove to\r\nBayswater, and made themselves comfortable in Barfoot\xe2\x80\x99s flat, which was\r\nvery plainly, but sufficiently, furnished. Micklethwaite, cigar in\r\nmouth, threw his legs over the side of the easy-chair in which he was\r\nsitting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d he began gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind telling you that your conjecture\r\nwas right. I _am_ going to be married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have reached the age of discretion. I must\r\nsuppose that you know what you are about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think I do. The story is unexciting. I am not a romantic\r\nperson, nor is my future wife. Now, you must know that when I was about\r\ntwenty-three years old I fell in love. You never suspected me of that,\r\nI dare say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I did fall in love. The lady was a clergyman\xe2\x80\x99s daughter at\r\nHereford, where I had a place in a school; she taught the infants in an\r\nelementary school connected with ours; her age was exactly the same as\r\nmy own. Now, the remarkable thing was that she took a liking for me,\r\nand when I was scoundrel enough to tell her of my feeling, she didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreject me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScoundrel enough? Why scoundrel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? But I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t a penny in the world. I lived at the school, and\r\nreceived a salary of thirty pounds, half of which had to go towards the\r\nsupport of my mother. What could possibly have been more villainous?\r\nWhat earthly prospect was there of my being able to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, grant the monstrosity of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis lady\xe2\x80\x94a very little lower than the angels\xe2\x80\x94declared that she was\r\ncontent to wait an indefinite time. She believed in me, and hoped for\r\nmy future. Her father\xe2\x80\x94the mother was dead\xe2\x80\x94sanctioned our engagement.\r\nShe had three sisters, one of them a governess, another keeping house,\r\nand the third a blind girl. Excellent people, all of them. I was at\r\ntheir house as often as possible, and they made much of me. It was a\r\npity, you know, for in those few leisure hours I ought to have been\r\nworking like a nigger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlainly you ought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFortunately, I left Hereford, and went to a school at Gloucester,\r\nwhere I had thirty-five pounds. How we gloried over that extra five\r\npounds! But it\xe2\x80\x99s no use going on with the story in this way; it would\r\ntake me till to-morrow morning. Seven years went by; we were thirty\r\nyears old, and no prospect whatever of our engagement coming to\r\nanything. I had worked pretty hard; I had taken my London degree; but\r\nnot a penny had I saved, and all I could spare was still needful to my\r\nmother. It struck me all at once that I had no right to continue the\r\nengagement. On my thirtieth birthday I wrote a letter to Fanny\xe2\x80\x94that is\r\nher name\xe2\x80\x94and begged her to be free. Now, would you have done the same,\r\nor not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, I am not imaginative enough to put myself in such a position.\r\nIt would need a stupendous effort, at all events.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut was there anything gross in the proceeding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe lady took it ill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in the sense of being offended. But she said it had caused her\r\nmuch suffering. She begged me to consider _myself_ free. She would\r\nremain faithful, and if, in time to come, I cared to write to her\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x94After all these years, I can\xe2\x80\x99t speak of it without huskiness. It\r\nseemed to me that I had behaved more like a scoundrel than ever. I\r\nthought I had better kill myself, and even planned ways of doing it\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ndid indeed. But after all we decided that our engagement should\r\ncontinue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think it natural? Well, the engagement has continued till this\r\nday. A month ago I was forty, so that we have waited for seventeen\r\nyears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMicklethwaite paused on a note of awe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo of Fanny\xe2\x80\x99s sisters are dead; they never married. The blind one\r\nFanny has long supported, and she will come to live with us. Long, long\r\nago we had both of us given up thought of marriage. I have never spoken\r\nto any one of the engagement; it was something too absurd, and also too\r\nsacred.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe smile died from Everard\xe2\x80\x99s face, and he sat in thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, when are _you_ going to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Micklethwaite, with a\r\nrevival of his cheerfulness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProbably never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I think you will neglect a grave duty. Yes. It is the duty of\r\nevery man, who has sufficient means, to maintain a wife. The life of\r\nunmarried women is a wretched one; every man who is able ought to save\r\none of them from that fate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like my cousin Mary and her female friends to hear you talk\r\nin that way. They would overwhelm you with scorn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot sincere scorn, is my belief. Of course I have heard of that kind\r\nof woman. Tell me something about them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarfoot was led on to a broad expression of his views.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admire your old-fashioned sentiment, Micklethwaite. It sits well on\r\nyou, and you\xe2\x80\x99re a fine fellow. But I have much more sympathy with the\r\nnew idea that women should think of marriage only as men do\xe2\x80\x94I mean,\r\nnot to grow up in the thought that they must marry or be blighted\r\ncreatures. My own views are rather extreme, perhaps; strictly, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbelieve in marriage at all. And I haven\xe2\x80\x99t anything like the respect for\r\nwomen, as women, that you have. You belong to the Ruskin school; and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x94well, perhaps my experience has been unusual, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nso. You know, by-the-bye, that my relatives consider me a blackguard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat affair you told me about some years ago?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChiefly that. I have a good mind to tell you the true story; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare to at the time. I accepted the charge of black-guardism; it didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmatter much. My cousin will never forgive me, though she has an air of\r\nfriendliness once more. And I suspect she had told her friend Miss Nunn\r\nall about me. Perhaps to put Miss Nunn on her guard\xe2\x80\x94Heaven knows!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe laughed merrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Nunn, I dare say, needs no protection against you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had an odd thought whilst I was there.\xe2\x80\x9d Everard leaned his head\r\nback, and half closed his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Nunn, I warrant, considers\r\nherself proof against any kind of wooing. She is one of the grandly\r\nsevere women; a terror, I imagine, to any young girl at their place who\r\nbetrays weak thoughts of matrimony. Now, it\xe2\x80\x99s rather a temptation to a\r\nman of my kind. There would be something piquant in making vigorous\r\nlove to Miss Nunn, just to prove her sincerity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMicklethwaite shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnworthy of you, Barfoot. Of course you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really do such a\r\nthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut such women really challenge one. If she were rich, I think I could\r\ndo it without scruple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to be taking it for granted,\xe2\x80\x9d said the mathematician,\r\nsmiling, \xe2\x80\x9cthat this lady would\xe2\x80\x94would respond to your love-making.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confess to you that women have spoilt me. And I am rather resentful\r\nwhen any one cries out against me for lack of respect to womanhood. I\r\nhave been the victim of this groundless veneration for females. Now you\r\nshall hear the story; and bear in mind that you are the only person to\r\nwhom I have ever told it. I never tried to defend myself when I was\r\nvilified on all hands. Probably the attempt would have been useless;\r\nand then it would certainly have increased the odium in which I stood.\r\nI think I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell cousin Mary the truth some day; it would be good for\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe listener looked uneasy, but curious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now, I was staying in the summer with some friends of ours at a\r\nlittle place called Upchurch, on a branch line from Oxford. The people\r\nwere well-to-do\xe2\x80\x94Goodall their name\xe2\x80\x94and went in for philanthropy. Mrs.\r\nGoodall always had a lot of Upchurch girls about her, educated and not;\r\nher idea was to civilize one class by means of the other, and to give a\r\nnew spirit to both. My cousin Mary was staying at the house whilst I\r\nwas there. She had more reasonable views than Mrs. Goodall, but took a\r\ngreat interest in what was going on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow one of the girls in process of spiritualization was called Amy\r\nDrake. In the ordinary course of things I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have met her, but\r\nshe served in a shop where I went two or three times to get a\r\nnewspaper; we talked a little\xe2\x80\x94with absolute propriety on my part, I\r\nassure you\xe2\x80\x94and she knew that I was a friend of the Goodalls. The girl\r\nhad no parents, and she was on the point of going to London to live\r\nwith a married sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt happened that by the very train which took me back to London, when\r\nmy visit was over, this girl also travelled, and alone. I saw her at\r\nUpchurch Station, but we didn\xe2\x80\x99t speak, and I got into a smoking\r\ncarriage. We had to change at Oxford, and there, as I walked about the\r\nplatform, Amy put herself in my way, so that I was obliged to begin\r\ntalking with her. This behaviour rather surprised me. I wondered what\r\nMrs. Goodall would think of it. But perhaps it was a sign of innocent\r\nfreedom in the intercourse of men and women. At all events, Amy managed\r\nto get me into the same carriage with herself, and on the way to London\r\nwe were alone. You foresee the end of it. At Paddington Station the\r\ngirl and I went off together, and she didn\xe2\x80\x99t get to her sister\xe2\x80\x99s till\r\nthe evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I take it for granted that you believe my account of the\r\nmatter. Miss Drake was by no means the spiritual young person that Mrs.\r\nGoodall thought her, or hoped to make her; plainly, she was a reprobate\r\nof experience. This, you will say, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t alter the fact that I also\r\nbehaved like a reprobate. No; from the moralist\xe2\x80\x99s point of view I was\r\nto blame. But I had no moral pretentions, and it was too much to expect\r\nthat I should rebuke the young woman and preach her a sermon. You admit\r\nthat, I dare say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe mathematician, frowning uncomfortably, gave a nod of assent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmy was not only a reprobate, but a rascal. She betrayed me to the\r\npeople at Upchurch, and, I am quite sure, meant from the first to do\r\nso. Imagine the outcry. I had committed a monstrous crime\xe2\x80\x94had led\r\nastray an innocent maiden, had outraged hospitality\xe2\x80\x94and so on. In\r\nAmy\xe2\x80\x99s case there were awkward results. Of course I must marry the girl\r\nforthwith. But of course I was determined to do no such thing. For the\r\nreasons I have explained, I let the storm break upon me. I had been a\r\nfool, to be sure, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help myself. No one would have believed\r\nmy plea\xe2\x80\x94no one would have allowed that the truth was an excuse. I was\r\nabused on all hands. And when, shortly after, my father made his will\r\nand died, doubtless he cut me off with my small annuity on this very\r\naccount. My cousin Mary got a good deal of the money that would\r\notherwise have been mine. The old man had been on rather better terms\r\nwith me just before that; in a will that he destroyed I believe he had\r\ntreated me handsomely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Micklethwaite, \xe2\x80\x9cevery one knows there are detestable\r\nwomen to be found. But you oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to let this affect your view of\r\nwomen in general. What became of the girl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made her a small allowance for a year and a half. Then her child\r\ndied, and the allowance ceased. I know nothing more of her. Probably\r\nshe has inveigled some one into marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Barfoot,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other, rolling about in his chair, \xe2\x80\x9cmy\r\nopinion remains the same. You are in debt to some worthy woman to the\r\nextent of half your income. Be quick and find her. It will be better\r\nfor you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Everard, with a smile of indulgence, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nI could marry on four hundred and fifty a year?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeavens! Why not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite impossible. A wife _might_ be acceptable to me; but marriage\r\nwith poverty\xe2\x80\x94I know myself and the world too well for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoverty!\xe2\x80\x9d screamed the mathematician. \xe2\x80\x9cFour hundred and fifty pounds!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrinding poverty\xe2\x80\x94for married people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMicklethwaite burst into indignant eloquence, and Everard sat listening\r\nwith the restrained smile on his lips.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\nFIRST PRINCIPLES\r\n\r\n\r\nHaving allowed exactly a week to go by, Everard Barfoot made use of his\r\ncousin\xe2\x80\x99s permission, and called upon her at nine in the evening. Miss\r\nBarfoot\xe2\x80\x99s dinner-hour was seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock; she and Rhoda, when alone,\r\nrarely sat for more than half an hour at table, and in this summer\r\nseason they often went out together at sunset to enjoy a walk along the\r\nriver. This evening they had returned only a few minutes before\r\nEverard\xe2\x80\x99s ring sounded at the door. Miss Barfoot (they were just\r\nentering the library) looked at her friend and smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder if that is the young man. Very flattering if he has\r\ncome again so soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe visitor was in mirthful humour, and met with a reception of\r\ncorresponding tone. He remarked at once that Miss Nunn had a much\r\npleasanter aspect than a week ago; her smile was ready and agreeable;\r\nshe sat in a sociable attitude and answered a jesting triviality with\r\nindulgence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of my reasons for coming to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Everard, \xe2\x80\x9cwas to tell you a\r\nremarkable story. It connects\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he addressed his cousin\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwith our talk\r\nabout the matrimonial disasters of those two friends of mine. Do you\r\nremember the name of Micklethwaite\xe2\x80\x94a man who used to cram me with\r\nmathematics? I thought you would. He is on the point of marrying, and\r\nhis engagement has lasted just seventeen years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe wisest of your friends, I should say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn excellent fellow. He is forty, and the lady the same. An\r\nastonishing case of constancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how is it likely to turn out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t predict, as the lady is unknown to me. But,\xe2\x80\x9d he added with\r\nfacetious gravity, \xe2\x80\x9cI think it likely that they are tolerably well\r\nacquainted with each other. Nothing but sheer poverty has kept them\r\napart. Pathetic, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think? I have a theory that when an\r\nengagement has lasted ten years, with constancy on both sides, and\r\npoverty still prevents marriage, the State ought to make provision for\r\na man in some way, according to his social standing. When one thinks of\r\nit, a whole socialistic system lies in that suggestion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Rhoda, \xe2\x80\x9cit were first provided that no marriage should\r\ntake place until _after_ a ten years\xe2\x80\x99 engagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Barfoot assented, in his smoothest and most graceful tone. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\ncompletes the system. Unless you like to add that no engagement is\r\npermitted except between people who have passed a certain examination;\r\nequivalent, let us say, to that which confers a university degree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmirable. And no marriage, except where both, for the whole\r\ndecennium, have earned their living by work that the State recognizes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow would that affect Mr. Micklethwaite\xe2\x80\x99s betrothed?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss\r\nBarfoot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe she has supported herself all along by teaching.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the other impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd more likely than\r\nnot, with loathing of her occupation. The usual kind of drudgery, was\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all, there must be some one to teach children to read and write.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but people who are thoroughly well trained for the task, and who\r\ntake a pleasure in it. This lady may be an exception; but I picture her\r\nas having spent a lifetime of uncongenial toil, longing miserably for\r\nthe day when poor Mr. Micklethwaite was able to offer her a home.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s the ordinary teacher-woman, and we must abolish her altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you to do that?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Everard suavely. \xe2\x80\x9cThe average man\r\nlabours that he may be able to marry, and the average woman certainly\r\nhas the same end in view. Are female teachers to be vowed to celibacy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing of the kind. But girls are to be brought up to a calling in\r\nlife, just as men are. It\xe2\x80\x99s because they have no calling that, when\r\nneed comes, they all offer themselves as teachers. They undertake one\r\nof the most difficult and arduous pursuits as if it were as simple as\r\nwashing up dishes. We can\xe2\x80\x99t earn money in any other way, but we can\r\nteach children! A man only becomes a schoolmaster or tutor when he has\r\ngone through laborious preparation\xe2\x80\x94anything but wise or adequate, of\r\ncourse, but still conscious preparation; and only a very few men,\r\ncomparatively, choose that line of work. Women must have just as wide a\r\nchoice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s plausible, cousin Mary. But remember that when a man chooses\r\nhis calling he chooses it for life. A girl cannot but remember that if\r\nshe marries her calling at once changes. The old business is thrown\r\naside\xe2\x80\x94henceforth profitless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Not henceforth profitless! There\xe2\x80\x99s the very point I insist upon.\r\nSo far is it from profitless, that it has made her a wholly different\r\nwoman from what she would otherwise have been. Instead of a moping,\r\nmawkish creature, with\xe2\x80\x94in most instances\xe2\x80\x94a very unhealthy mind, she\r\nis a complete human being. She stands on an equality with the man. He\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t despise her as he now does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Everard, observing Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s satisfied smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like that view very much. But what about the great number of girls\r\nwho are claimed by domestic duties? Do you abandon them, with a\r\nhelpless sigh, to be moping and mawkish and unhealthy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the first place, there needn\xe2\x80\x99t be a great number of unmarried women\r\nclaimed by such duties. Most of those you are thinking of are not\r\nfulfilling a duty at all; they are only pottering about the house,\r\nbecause they have nothing better to do. And when the whole course of\r\nfemale education is altered; when girls are trained as a matter of\r\ncourse to some definite pursuit; then those who really are obliged to\r\nremain at home will do their duty there in quite a different spirit.\r\nHome work will be their serious business, instead of a disagreeable\r\ndrudgery, or a way of getting through the time till marriage offers. I\r\nwould have no girl, however wealthy her parent, grow up without a\r\nprofession. There should be no such thing as a class of females\r\nvulgarized by the necessity of finding daily amusement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor of males either, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Everard, stroking his beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor of males either, cousin Everard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou thoroughly approve all this, Miss Nunn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes. But I go further. I would have girls taught that marriage is a\r\nthing to be avoided rather than hoped for. I would teach them that for\r\nthe majority of women marriage means disgrace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Now do let me understand you. Why does it mean disgrace?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause the majority of men are without sense of honour. To be bound\r\nto them in wedlock is shame and misery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard\xe2\x80\x99s eyelids drooped, and he did not speak for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you seriously think, Miss Nunn, that by persuading as many women\r\nas possible to abstain from marriage you will improve the character of\r\nmen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no hope of sudden results, Mr. Barfoot. I should like to save\r\nas many as possible of the women now living from a life of dishonour;\r\nbut the spirit of our work looks to the future. When _all_ women, high\r\nand low alike, are trained to self-respect, then men will regard them\r\nin a different light, and marriage may be honourable to both.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Everard was silent, and seemingly impressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go on with this discussion another time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Barfoot,\r\nwith cheerful interruption. \xe2\x80\x9cEverard, do you know Somerset at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever was in that part of England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Nunn is going to take her holiday at Cheddar and we have been\r\nlooking over some photographs of that district taken by her brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFrom the table she reached a scrapbook, and Everard turned it over with\r\ninterest. The views were evidently made by an amateur, but in general\r\nhad no serious faults. Cheddar cliffs were represented in several\r\naspects.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea the scenery was so fine. Cheddar cheese has quite\r\novershadowed the hills in my imagination. This might be a bit of\r\nCumberland, or of the Highlands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my playground when I was a child,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were born at Cheddar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; at Axbridge, a little place not far off. But I had an uncle at\r\nCheddar, a farmer, and very often stayed with him. My brother is\r\nfarming there now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAxbridge? Here is a view of the market-place. What a delightful old\r\ntown!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the sleepiest spots in England, I should say. The railway goes\r\nthrough it now, but hasn\xe2\x80\x99t made the slightest difference. Nobody pulls\r\ndown or builds; nobody opens a new shop; nobody thinks of extending his\r\ntrade. A delicious place!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you find no pleasure in that kind of thing, Miss Nunn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes\xe2\x80\x94at holiday time. I shall doze there for a fortnight, and\r\nforget all about the \xe2\x80\x9cso-called nineteenth century.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can hardly believe it. There will be a disgraceful marriage at this\r\nbeautiful old church, and the sight of it will exasperate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda laughed gaily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it will be a marriage of the golden age! Perhaps I shall remember\r\nthe bride when she was a little girl; and I shall give her a kiss, and\r\npat her on the rosy cheek, and wish her joy. And the bridegroom will be\r\nsuch a good-hearted simpleton, unable to pronounce _f_ and _s_. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind that sort of marriage a bit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe listeners were both regarding her\xe2\x80\x94Miss Barfoot with an\r\naffectionate smile, Everard with a puzzled, searching look, ending in\r\namusement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must run down into that country some day,\xe2\x80\x9d said the latter.\r\n\r\nHe did not stay much longer, but left only because he feared to burden\r\nthe ladies with too much of his company.\r\n\r\nAgain a week passed, and the same evening found Barfoot approaching the\r\nhouse in Queen\xe2\x80\x99s Road. To his great annoyance he learnt that Miss\r\nBarfoot was not at home; she had dined, but afterwards had gone out. He\r\ndid not venture to ask for Miss Nunn, and was moving disappointedly\r\naway, when Rhoda herself, returning from a walk, came up to the door.\r\nShe offered her hand gravely, but with friendliness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Barfoot, I am sorry to say, has gone to visit one of our girls\r\nwho is ill. But I think she will very soon be back. Will you come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGladly. I had so counted on an hour\xe2\x80\x99s talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda led him to the drawing-room, excused herself for a few moments,\r\nand came back in her ordinary evening dress. Barfoot noticed that her\r\nhair was much more becomingly arranged than when he first saw her; so\r\nit had been on the last occasion, but for some reason its appearance\r\nattracted his eyes this evening. He scrutinized her, at discreet\r\nintervals, from head to foot. To Everard, nothing female was alien;\r\nwoman, merely as woman, interested him profoundly. And this example of\r\nher sex had excited his curiosity in no common degree. His concern with\r\nher was purely intellectual; she had no sensual attraction for him, but\r\nhe longed to see further into her mind, to probe the sincerity of the\r\nmotives she professed, to understand her mechanism, her process of\r\ngrowth. Hitherto he had enjoyed no opportunity of studying this type.\r\nFor his cousin was a very different person; by habit he regarded her as\r\nold, whereas Miss Nunn, in spite of her thirty years, could not\r\npossibly be considered past youth.\r\n\r\nHe enjoyed her air of equality; she sat down with him as a male\r\nacquaintance might have done, and he felt sure that her behaviour would\r\nbe the same under any circumstances. He delighted in the frankness of\r\nher speech; it was doubtful whether she regarded any subject as\r\nimproper for discussion between mature and serious people. Part cause\r\nof this, perhaps, was her calm consciousness that she had not a\r\nbeautiful face. No, it was not beautiful; yet even at the first meeting\r\nit did not repel him. Studying her features, he saw how fine was their\r\nexpression. The prominent forehead, with its little unevenness that\r\nmeant brains; the straight eyebrows, strongly marked, with deep\r\nvertical furrows generally drawn between them; the chestnut-brown eyes,\r\nwith long lashes; the high-bridged nose, thin and delicate; the\r\nintellectual lips, a protrusion of the lower one, though very slight,\r\nmarking itself when he caught her profile; the big, strong chin; the\r\nshapely neck\xe2\x80\x94why, after all, it was a kind of beauty. The head might\r\nhave been sculptured with fine effect. And she had a well-built frame.\r\nHe observed her strong wrists, with exquisite vein-tracings on the pure\r\nwhite. Probably her constitution was very sound; she had good teeth,\r\nand a healthy brownish complexion.\r\n\r\nWith reference to the sick girl whom Miss Barfoot was visiting, Everard\r\nbegan what was practically a resumption of their last talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you a formal society, with rules and so on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no; nothing of the kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you of course select the girls whom you instruct or employ?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery carefully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow I should like to see them all!\xe2\x80\x94I mean,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with a laugh,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cit would be so very interesting. The truth is, my sympathies are\r\nstrongly with you in much of what you said the other day about women\r\nand marriage. We regard the matter from different points of view, but\r\nour ends are the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda moved her eyebrows, and asked calmly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you serious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly. You are absorbed in your present work, that of\r\nstrengthening women\xe2\x80\x99s minds and character; for the final issue of this\r\nyou can\xe2\x80\x99t care much. But to me that is the practical interest. In my\r\nmind, you are working for the happiness of men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed?\xe2\x80\x9d escaped Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s lips, which had curled in irony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t misunderstand me. I am not speaking cynically or trivially. The\r\ngain of women is also the gain of men. You are bitter against the\r\naverage man for his low morality; but that fault, on the whole, is\r\ndirectly traceable to the ignobleness of women. Think, and you will\r\ngrant me this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see what you mean. Men have themselves to thank for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly they have. I say that I am on your side. Our civilization in\r\nthis point has always been absurdly defective. Men have kept women at a\r\nbarbarous stage of development, and then complain that they are\r\nbarbarous. In the same way society does its best to create a criminal\r\nclass, and then rages against the criminals. But, you see, I am one of\r\nthe men, and an impatient one too. The mass of women I see about me are\r\nso contemptible that, in my haste, I use unjust language. Put yourself\r\nin the man\xe2\x80\x99s place. Say that there are a million or so of us very\r\nintelligent and highly educated. Well, the women of corresponding mind\r\nnumber perhaps a few thousands. The vast majority of men must make a\r\nmarriage that is doomed to be a dismal failure. We fall in love it is\r\ntrue; but do we really deceive ourselves about the future? A very young\r\nman may; why, we know of very young men who are so frantic as to marry\r\ngirls of the working class\xe2\x80\x94mere lumps of human flesh. But most of us\r\nknow that our marriage is a _pis aller_. At first we are sad about it;\r\nthen we grow cynical, and snap our fingers at moral obligation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaking a bad case very much worse, instead of bravely bettering it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but human nature is human nature. I am only urging to you the\r\ncase of average intelligent men. As likely as not\xe2\x80\x94so preposterous are\r\nour conventions\xe2\x80\x94you have never heard it put honestly. I tell you the\r\nsimple truth when I say that more than half these men regard their\r\nwives with active disgust. They will do anything to be relieved of the\r\nsight of them for as many hours as possible at a time. If circumstances\r\nallowed, wives would be abandoned very often indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou regret that it isn\xe2\x80\x99t done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI prefer to say that I approve it when it is done without disregard of\r\ncommon humanity. There\xe2\x80\x99s my friend Orchard. With him it was suicide or\r\nfreedom from his hateful wife. Most happily, he was able to make\r\nprovision for her and the children, and had strength to break his\r\nbonds. If he had left them to starve, I should have _understood_ it,\r\nbut couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have approved it. There are men who might follow his\r\nexample, but prefer to put up with a life of torture. Well, they _do_\r\nprefer it, you see. I may think that they are foolishly weak, but I can\r\nonly recognize that they make a choice between two forms of suffering.\r\nThey have tender consciences; the thought of desertion is too painful\r\nto them. And in a great number of cases, mere considerations of money\r\nand the like keep a man bound. But conscience and habit\xe2\x80\x94detestable\r\nhabit\xe2\x80\x94and fear of public opinion generally hold him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll this is very interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, with grave irony.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy-the-bye, under the head of detestable habit you would put love of\r\nchildren?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarfoot hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a motive I oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to have left out. Yet I believe, for most\r\nmen, it is represented by conscience. The love of children would not\r\ngenerally, in itself, be strong enough to outweigh matrimonial\r\nwretchedness. Many an intelligent and kind-hearted man has been driven\r\nfrom his wife notwithstanding thought for his children. He provides for\r\nthem as well as he can\xe2\x80\x94but, and even for their sakes, he must save\r\nhimself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe expression of Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s countenance suddenly changed. An extreme\r\nmobility of facial muscles was one of the things in her that held\r\nEverard\xe2\x80\x99s attention.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s something in your way of putting it that I don\xe2\x80\x99t like,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, with much frankness; \xe2\x80\x9cbut of course I agree with you in the\r\nfacts. I am convinced that most marriages are hateful, from every point\r\nof view. But there will be no improvement until women have revolted\r\nagainst marriage, from a reasonable conviction of its hatefulness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you all success\xe2\x80\x94most sincerely I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused, looked about the room, and stroked his ear. Then, in a grave\r\ntone,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy own ideal of marriage involves perfect freedom on both sides. Of\r\ncourse it could only be realized where conditions are favourable;\r\npoverty and other wretched things force us so often to sin against our\r\nbest beliefs. But there are plenty of people who might marry on these\r\nideal terms. Perfect freedom, sanctioned by the sense of intelligent\r\nsociety, would abolish most of the evils we have in mind. But women\r\nmust first be civilized; you are quite right in that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door opened, and Miss Barfoot came in. She glanced from one to the\r\nother, and without speaking gave her hand to Everard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is your patient?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little better, I think. It is nothing dangerous. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a letter\r\nfrom your brother Tom. Perhaps I had better read it at once; there may\r\nbe news you would like to hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sat down and broke the envelope. Whilst she was reading the letter\r\nto herself, Rhoda quietly left the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there is news,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Barfoot presently, \xe2\x80\x9cand of a\r\ndisagreeable kind. A few weeks ago\xe2\x80\x94before writing, that is\xe2\x80\x94he was\r\nthrown off a horse and had a rib fractured.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh? How is he going on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGetting right again, he says. And they are coming back to England; his\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s consumptive symptoms have disappeared, of course, and she is\r\nvery impatient to leave Madeira. It is to be hoped she will allow poor\r\nTom time to get his rib set. Probably that consideration doesn\xe2\x80\x99t weigh\r\nmuch with her. He says that he is writing to you by the same mail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor old fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d said Everard, with feeling. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes he complain about\r\nhis wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe never has done till now, but there\xe2\x80\x99s a sentence here that reads\r\ndoubtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cMuriel,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9chas been terribly upset about my\r\naccident. I can\xe2\x80\x99t persuade her that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t get thrown on purpose; yet\r\nI assure you I didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf old Tom becomes ironical, he must be hard driven. I have no great\r\nlonging to meet Mrs. Thomas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a silly and a vulgar woman. But I told him that in plain terms\r\nbefore he married. It says much for his good nature that he remains so\r\nfriendly with me. Read the letter, Everard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94very kind things about me. Good old Tom! Why don\xe2\x80\x99t I marry? Well,\r\nnow, one would have thought that his own experience\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot began to talk about something else. Before very long Rhoda\r\ncame back, and in the conversation that followed it was mentioned that\r\nshe would leave for her holiday in two days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been reading about Cheddar,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Everard, with animation.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a flower grows among the rocks called the Cheddar pink. Do you\r\nknow it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very well,\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bring you some specimens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you? That\xe2\x80\x99s very kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring _me_ a genuine pound or two of the cheese, Rhoda,\xe2\x80\x9d requested\r\nMiss Barfoot gaily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will. What they sell in the shops there is all sham, Mr.\r\nBarfoot\xe2\x80\x94like so much else in this world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI care nothing about the cheese. That\xe2\x80\x99s all very well for a\r\nmatter-of-fact person like cousin Mary, but _I_ have a strong vein of\r\npoetry; you must have noticed it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they shook hands,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will really bring me the flowers?\xe2\x80\x9d Everard said in a voice\r\nsensibly softened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will make a note of it,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reassuring answer.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\nAT NATURE\xe2\x80\x99S BIDDING\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sick girl whom Miss Barfoot had been to see was Monica Madden.\r\n\r\nWith strange suddenness, after several weeks of steady application to\r\nher work, in a cheerful spirit which at times rose to gaiety, Monica\r\nbecame dull, remiss, unhappy; then violent headaches attacked her, and\r\none morning she declared herself unable to rise. Mildred Vesper went to\r\nGreat Portland Street at the usual hour, and informed Miss Barfoot of\r\nher companion\xe2\x80\x99s illness. A doctor was summoned; to him it seemed\r\nprobable that the girl was suffering from consequences of overstrain at\r\nher old employment; there was nervous collapse, hysteria, general\r\ndisorder of the system. Had the patient any mental disquietude? Was\r\ntrouble of any kind (the doctor smiled) weighing upon her? Miss\r\nBarfoot, unable to answer these questions, held private colloquy with\r\nMildred; but the latter, though she pondered a good deal with\r\ncorrugated brows, could furnish no information.\r\n\r\nIn a day or two Monica was removed to her sister\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings at Lavender\r\nHill. Mrs. Conisbee managed to put a room at her disposal, and Virginia\r\ntended her. Thither Miss Barfoot went on the evening when Everard found\r\nher away; she and Virginia, talking together after being with the\r\ninvalid for a quarter of an hour, agreed that there was considerable\r\nimprovement, but felt a like uneasiness regarding Monica\xe2\x80\x99s state of\r\nmind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the visitor, \xe2\x80\x9cthat she regrets the step I\r\npersuaded her to take?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ think that! She has been so delighted with her progress\r\neach time I have seen her. No, I feel sure it\xe2\x80\x99s only the results of\r\nwhat she suffered at Walworth Road. In a very short time we shall have\r\nher at work again, and brighter than ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot was not convinced. After Everard\xe2\x80\x99s departure that evening\r\nshe talked of the matter with Rhoda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Nunn, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Monica is rather a silly girl. She\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t know her own mind. If this kind of thing is repeated, we had\r\nbetter send her back to the country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo shop work again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might be better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like the thought of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda had one of her fits of wrathful eloquence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow could one have a better instance than this Madden family of the\r\ncrime that middle-class parents commit when they allow their girls to\r\ngo without rational training? Of course I know that Monica was only a\r\nlittle child when they were left orphans; but her sisters had already\r\ngrown up into uselessness, and their example has been harmful to her\r\nall along. Her guardians dealt with her absurdly; they made her half a\r\nlady and half a shop-girl. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think she\xe2\x80\x99ll ever be good for much.\r\nAnd the elder ones will go on just keeping themselves alive; you can\r\nsee that. They\xe2\x80\x99ll never start the school that there\xe2\x80\x99s so much talk of.\r\nThat poor, helpless, foolish Virginia, alone there in her miserable\r\nlodging! How can we hope that any one will take her as a companion? And\r\nyet they are capitalists; eight hundred pounds between them. Think what\r\ncapable women might do with eight hundred pounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am really afraid to urge them to meddle with the investments.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course; so am I. One is afraid to do or propose anything. Virginia\r\nis starving, _must_ be starving. Poor creature! I can never forget how\r\nher eyes shone when I put that joint of meat before her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do, do wish,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Miss Barfoot, with a pained smile, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I knew\r\nsome honest man who would be likely to fall in love with little Monica!\r\nIn spite of you, my dear, I would devote myself to making the match.\r\nBut there\xe2\x80\x99s no one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I would help,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Rhoda, not unkindly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s fit for nothing\r\nelse, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid. We mustn\xe2\x80\x99t look for any kind of heroism in Monica.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLess than half an hour after Miss Barfoot had left the house at\r\nLavender Hill, Mildred Vesper made a call there. It was about half-past\r\nnine; the invalid, after sitting up since midday, had gone to bed, but\r\ncould not sleep. Summoned to the house-door, Virginia acquainted Miss\r\nVesper with the state of affairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you might see her for a few minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to, if you please, Miss Madden,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mildred, who\r\nhad a rather uneasy look.\r\n\r\nShe went upstairs and entered the bedroom, where a lamp was burning. At\r\nthe sight of her friend Monica showed much satisfaction; they kissed\r\neach other affectionately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood old girl! I had made up my mind to come back to-morrow, or at all\r\nevents the day after. It\xe2\x80\x99s so frightfully dull here. Oh, and I wanted\r\nto know if anything\xe2\x80\x94any letter\xe2\x80\x94had come for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just why I came to see you to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMildred took a letter from her pocket, and half averted her face as she\r\nhanded it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nothing particular,\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica, putting it away under her\r\npillow. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you, dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut her cheeks had become hot, and she trembled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonica\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care to tell me about\xe2\x80\x94anything? You don\xe2\x80\x99t think it would\r\nmake your mind easier?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a minute Monica lay back, gazing at the wall, then she looked round\r\nquickly, with a shamefaced laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very silly of me not to have told you long before this. But\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re so sensible; I was afraid. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you everything. Not now,\r\nbut as soon as I get to Rutland Street. I shall come to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think you can? You look dreadfully bad still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t get any better here,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the invalid in a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor\r\nVirgie does depress me so. She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t understand that I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to\r\nhear her repeating the kind of things she has heard from Miss Barfoot\r\nand Miss Nunn. She tries so hard to look forward hopefully\xe2\x80\x94but I\r\n_know_ she is miserable, and it makes me more miserable still. I\r\noughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to have left you; I should have been all right in a day or\r\ntwo, with you to help me. You don\xe2\x80\x99t make-believe, Milly; it\xe2\x80\x99s all real\r\nand natural good spirits. It has done me good only to see your dear old\r\nface.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re a flatterer. And do you really feel better?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much better. I shall go to sleep very soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe visitor took her leave. When, a few minutes after, Monica had\r\nbidden good-night to her sister (requesting that the lamp might be\r\nleft), she read what Mildred had brought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMY DEAREST MONICA,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the missive began\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you not written\r\nbefore this? I have been dreadfully uneasy ever since receiving your\r\nlast letter. Your headache soon went away, I hope? Why haven\xe2\x80\x99t you made\r\nanother appointment? It is all I can do to keep from breaking my\r\npromise and coming to ask about you. Write at once, I implore you, my\r\ndearest. It\xe2\x80\x99s no use telling me that I must not use these words of\r\naffection; they come to my lips and to my pen irresistibly. You know so\r\nwell that I love you with all my heart and soul; I can\xe2\x80\x99t address you\r\nlike I did when we first corresponded. My darling! My dear, sweet,\r\nbeautiful little girl\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFour close pages of this, with scarce room at the end for \xe2\x80\x9cE.W.\xe2\x80\x9d When\r\nshe had gone through it, Monica turned her face upon the pillow and lay\r\nso for a long time. A clock in the house struck eleven; this roused\r\nher, and she slipped out of the bed to hide the letter in her\r\ndress-pocket. Not long after she was asleep.\r\n\r\nThe next day, on returning from her work and opening the sitting-room\r\ndoor, Mildred Vesper was greeted with a merry laugh. Monica had been\r\nhere since three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and had made tea in readiness for her\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s arrival. She looked very white, but her eyes gleamed with\r\npleasure, and she moved about the room as actively as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVirgie came with me, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay. She says she has a most\r\nimportant letter to write to Alice\xe2\x80\x94about the school, of course. Oh,\r\nthat school! I do wish they could make up their minds. I\xe2\x80\x99ve told them\r\nthey may have all my money, if they like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you? I should like the sensation of offering hundreds of pounds\r\nto some one. It must give a strange feeling of dignity and importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, only _two_ hundred! A wretched little sum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a person of large ideas, as I have often told you. Where did\r\nyou get them, I wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t put on that face! It\xe2\x80\x99s the one I like least of all your many\r\nfaces. It\xe2\x80\x99s suspicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMildred went to take off her things, and was quickly at the tea-table.\r\nShe had a somewhat graver look than usual, and chose rather to listen\r\nthan talk.\r\n\r\nNot long after tea, when there had been a long and unnatural silence,\r\nMildred making pretence of absorption in a \xe2\x80\x9cTreasury\xe2\x80\x9d and her companion\r\nstanding at the window, whence she threw back furtive glances, the\r\nthunder of a postman\xe2\x80\x99s knock downstairs caused both of them to start,\r\nand look at each other in a conscience-stricken way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Monica, stepping to the door. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go and\r\nlook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer conjecture was right. Another letter from Widdowson, still more\r\nalarmed and vehement than the last. She read it rapidly on the\r\nstaircase, and entered the room with sheet and envelope squeezed\r\ntogether in her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to tell you all about this, Milly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other nodded and assumed an attitude of sober attention. In\r\nrelating her story, Monica moved hither and thither; now playing with\r\nobjects on the mantlepiece, now standing in the middle of the floor,\r\nhands locked nervously behind her. Throughout, her manner was that of\r\ndefence; she seemed doubtful of herself, and anxious to represent the\r\ncase as favourably as possible; not for a moment had her voice the ring\r\nof courageous passion, nor the softness of tender feeling. The\r\nnarrative hung together but awkwardly, and in truth gave a very\r\nindistinct notion of how she had comported herself at the various\r\nstages of the irregular courtship. Her behaviour had been marked by far\r\nmore delicacy and scruple than she succeeded in representing. Painfully\r\nconscious of this, she exclaimed at length,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see your opinion of me has suffered. You don\xe2\x80\x99t like this story. You\r\nwonder how I could do such things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear, I certainly wonder how you could begin,\xe2\x80\x9d Mildred made\r\nanswer, with her natural directness, but gently. \xe2\x80\x9cAfterwards, of\r\ncourse, it was different. When you had once got to be sure that he was\r\na gentleman\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was sure of that so soon,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Monica, her cheeks still red.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will understand it much better when you have seen him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wish me to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to write now, and say that I will marry him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey looked long at each other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are\xe2\x80\x94really?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I made up my mind last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Monica\xe2\x80\x94you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t mind my speaking plainly\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you\r\nlove him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I love him well enough to feel that I am doing right in marrying\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d She sat down by the table, and propped her head on her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nloves me; I can\xe2\x80\x99t doubt that. If you could read his letters, you would\r\nsee how strong his feeling is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook with the cold induced by excitement; her voice was at moments\r\nall but choked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, putting love aside,\xe2\x80\x9d went on the other, very gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do\r\nyou really know of Mr. Widdowson? Nothing whatever but what he has told\r\nyou himself. Of course you will let your friends make inquiries for\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I shall tell my sisters, and no doubt they will go to Miss Nunn\r\nat once. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to do anything rash. But it will be all right\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nmean, he has told me the truth about everything. You would be sure of\r\nthat if you knew him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMildred, with hands before her on the table, made the tips of her\r\nfingers meet. Her lips were drawn in; her eyes seemed looking for\r\nsomething minute on the cloth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at length, \xe2\x80\x9cI suspected what was going on. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNaturally I thought it was some one whose acquaintance you had made at\r\nthe shop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow _could_ I think of marrying any one of that kind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have been grieved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may believe me, Milly; Mr. Widdowson is a man you will respect and\r\nlike as soon as you know him. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have behaved to me with more\r\ndelicacy. Not a word from him, spoken or written, has ever pained\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94except that he tells me he suffers so dreadfully, and of course I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t hear that without pain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo respect, and even to like, a man, isn\xe2\x80\x99t at all the same as loving\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said _you_ would respect and like him,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Monica, with\r\nhumorous impatience. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want _you_ to love him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMildred laughed, with constraint.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never loved any one yet, dear, and it\xe2\x80\x99s very unlikely I ever shall.\r\nBut I think I know the signs of the feeling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica came behind her, and leaned upon her shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe loves me so much that he has made me think I _must_ marry him. And\r\nI am glad of it. I\xe2\x80\x99m not like you, Milly; I can\xe2\x80\x99t be contented with\r\nthis life. Miss Barfoot and Miss Nunn are very sensible and good\r\npeople, and I admire them very much, but I _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ go their way. It\r\nseems to me that it would be dreadful, dreadful, to live one\xe2\x80\x99s life\r\nalone. Don\xe2\x80\x99t turn round and snap at me; I want to tell you the truth\r\nwhilst you can\xe2\x80\x99t see me. Whenever I think of Alice and Virginia, I am\r\nfrightened; I had rather, oh, far rather, kill myself than live such a\r\nlife at their age. You can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine how miserable they are, really.\r\nAnd I have the same nature as theirs, you know. Compared with you and\r\nMiss Haven I\xe2\x80\x99m very weak and childish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter drumming on the table for a moment, with wrinkled brows, Mildred\r\nmade grave response.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must let _me_ tell the truth as well. I have heard the most dreadful\r\nstories of quarrelling and all sorts of unhappiness between people I\r\nthought safe from any such dangers. You _may_ be fortunate; I only say\r\nthat the chances are very much against it, marrying from such motives\r\nas you confess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica drew herself up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t confessed any motive to be ashamed of, Milly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say you have decided to marry now because you are afraid of never\r\nhaving another chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; that\xe2\x80\x99s turning it very unkindly. I only said that _after_ I had\r\ntold you that I did love him. And I do love him. He has made me love\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I have no right to say any more. I can only wish you happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMildred heaved a sigh, and pretended to give her attention to Maunder.\r\n\r\nAfter waiting irresolutely for some minutes, Monica looked for\r\nnotepaper, and took it, together with her inkstand, into the bedroom.\r\nShe was absent half an hour. On her return there was a stamped letter\r\nin her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is going, Milly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, dear. I have nothing more to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou give me up for lost. We shall see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was spoken light-heartedly. Again she left the room, put on her\r\nout-of-door things, and went to post the letter. By this time she began\r\nto feel the results of exertion and excitement; headache and tremulous\r\nfailing of her strength obliged her to go to bed almost as soon as she\r\nreturned. Mildred waited upon her with undiminished kindness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica murmured, as her head sank on the pillow. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nfeel so relieved and so glad\xe2\x80\x94so happy\xe2\x80\x94now I have done it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the other, with a kiss, and went back to\r\nher semblance of reading.\r\n\r\nTwo days later Monica called unexpectedly at Mrs. Conisbee\xe2\x80\x99s. Being\r\ntold by that worthy woman that Miss Madden was at home, she ran\r\nupstairs and tapped at the door. Virginia\xe2\x80\x99s voice inquired hurriedly\r\nwho was there, and on Monica\xe2\x80\x99s announcing herself there followed a\r\nstartled exclamation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust a minute, my love! Only a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the door opened Monica was surprised by a disorder in her sister\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nappearance. Virginia had flushed cheeks, curiously vague eyes, and hair\r\nruffled as if she had just risen from a nap. She began to talk in a\r\nhurried, disconnected way, trying to explain that she had not been\r\nquite well, and was not yet properly dressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a strange smell!\xe2\x80\x9d Monica exclaimed, looking about the room. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlike brandy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou notice it? I have\xe2\x80\x94I was obliged to get\xe2\x80\x94to ask Mrs. Conisbee\r\nfor\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to alarm you, dear, but I felt rather faint. Indeed,\r\nI thought I should have a fainting fit. I was obliged to call Mrs.\r\nConisbee\xe2\x80\x94But don\xe2\x80\x99t think anything about it. It\xe2\x80\x99s all over. The weather\r\nis very trying\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed nervously and began to pat Monica\xe2\x80\x99s hand. The girl was not\r\nquite satisfied, and pressed many questions, but in the end she\r\naccepted Virginia\xe2\x80\x99s assurances that nothing serious had happened. Then\r\nher own business occupied her; she sat down, and said with a smile,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have brought you astonishing news. If you didn\xe2\x80\x99t faint before you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe very likely to do so now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer sister exhibited fresh agitation, and begged not to be kept in\r\nsuspense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy nerves are in a shocking state to-day. It _must_ be the weather.\r\nWhat _can_ you have to tell me, Monica?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I shan\xe2\x80\x99t need to go on with typewriting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? What are you going to do, child?\xe2\x80\x9d the other asked sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVirgie\xe2\x80\x94I am going to be married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shock was a severe one. Virginia\xe2\x80\x99s hands fell, her eyes started,\r\nher mouth opened; she became the colour of clay, even her lips losing\r\nfor the moment all their colour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarried?\xe2\x80\x9d she at length gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x94who is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome one you have never heard of. His name is Mr. Edmund Widdowson. He\r\nis very well off, and has a house at Herne Hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA private gentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. He used to be in business, but is retired. Now, I am not going to\r\ntell you much more about him until you have made his acquaintance.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask a lot of questions. You are to come with me this afternoon to\r\nhis house. He lives alone, but a relative of his, his sister-in-law, is\r\ngoing to be with him to meet us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but it\xe2\x80\x99s so sudden! I can\xe2\x80\x99t go to pay a call like that at a\r\nmoment\xe2\x80\x99s notice. Impossible, darling! What _does_ it all mean? You are\r\ngoing to be married, Monica?
