And I have come to you because I feared\r\nyou would believe my husband\xe2\x80\x94as I see you have done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda, though she thought it very unlikely that all this should be\r\nadmirable acting, showed that the explanation had by no means fully\r\nsatisfied her. Unwilling to put the crucial question, she waited, with\r\ngravity which had none of the former harshness, for what else Mrs.\r\nWiddowson might choose to say. A look of suffering appeal obliged her\r\nto break the silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry you have laid this task upon yourself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill Monica looked at her, and at length murmured,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf only I could know that I had done any good\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, with a searching glance, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t wish me to\r\nrepeat what you have said?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was only for you. I thought\xe2\x80\x94if you felt able to let Mr. Barfoot\r\nknow that you had no longer any\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA flash of stern intelligence shot from the listener\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen him then?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked with abrupt directness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot since.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has written to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94still in the same voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed he has not. Mr. Barfoot never wrote to me. I know nothing\r\nwhatever about him. No one asked me to come to you\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t think that.\r\nNo one knows of what I have been telling you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Rhoda was oppressed by the difficulty of determining how much\r\ncredit was due to such assertions. Monica understood her look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I have said so much I must tell you all. It would be dreadful after\r\nthis to go away uncertain whether you believed me or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuman feeling prompted the listener to declare that she had no doubts\r\nleft. Yet she could not give utterance to the words. She knew they\r\nwould sound forced, insincere. Shame at inflicting shame caused her to\r\nbend her head. Already she had been silent too long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you everything,\xe2\x80\x9d Monica was saying in low, tremulous\r\ntones. \xe2\x80\x9cIf no one else believes me, you at all events shall. I have not\r\ndone what\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t hear this,\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda broke in, the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s voice affecting\r\nher too powerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI will believe you without this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica broke into sobbing. The strain of this last effort had overtaxed\r\nher strength.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe won\xe2\x80\x99t talk any more of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, with an endeavour to speak\r\nkindly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have done all that could be asked of you. I am grateful to\r\nyou for coming on my account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other controlled herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you hear what I have to say, Miss Nunn? Will you hear it as a\r\nfriend? I want to put myself right in your thoughts. I have told no one\r\nelse; I shall be easier in mind if you will hear me. My husband will\r\nknow everything before very long\xe2\x80\x94but perhaps I shall not be alive\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething in Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s face suggested to Monica that her meaning was\r\nunderstood. Perhaps, notwithstanding her denial, Virginia had told more\r\nwhen she was here than she had permission to make known.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you wish to tell _me_?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rhoda uneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you are so strong. You will say something that will help me. I\r\nknow you think that I have committed a sin which it is a shame to speak\r\nof. That isn\xe2\x80\x99t true. If it were true I should never consent to go and\r\nlive in my husband\xe2\x80\x99s house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are returning to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgot that I haven\xe2\x80\x99t told you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Monica related the agreement that had been arrived at. When she\r\nspoke of the time that must elapse before she would make a confession\r\nto her husband, it again seemed to her that Miss Nunn understood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a reason why I consent to be supported by him,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ncontinued. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it were true that I had sinned as he suspects I would\r\nrather kill myself than pretend still to be his wife. The day before he\r\nhad me watched I thought I had left him forever. I thought that if I\r\nwent back to the house again it would only be to get a few things that\r\nI needed. It was some one who lived in the same building as Mr.\r\nBarfoot. You have met him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe raised her eyes for an instant, and they encountered the\r\nlistener\xe2\x80\x99s. Rhoda was at no loss to supply the omitted name; she saw at\r\nonce how plain things were becoming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has left England,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Monica in a hurried but clear voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthought then that I should go away with him. But\xe2\x80\x94it was impossible. I\r\nloved him\xe2\x80\x94or thought I loved him; but I was guiltless of anything more\r\nthan consenting to leave my husband. Will you believe me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Monica, I do believe you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you have any doubt, I can show you a letter he wrote to me from\r\nabroad, which will prove\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe you absolutely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut let me tell you more. I must explain how the misunderstanding\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRapidly she recounted the incidents of that fatal Saturday afternoon.\r\nAt the conclusion her self-command was again overcome; she shed tears,\r\nand murmured broken entreaties for kindness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I do, Miss Nunn? How can I live until\xe2\x80\x94? I know it\xe2\x80\x99s only\r\nfor a short time. My wretched life will soon be at an end\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonica\xe2\x80\x94there is one thing you must remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice was so gentle, though firm\xe2\x80\x94so unlike what she had expected\r\nto hear\xe2\x80\x94that the sufferer looked up with grateful attention.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me\xe2\x80\x94give me what help you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLife seems so bitter to you that you are in despair. Yet isn\xe2\x80\x99t it your\r\nduty to live as though some hope were before you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica gazed in uncertainty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you will understand. I am not speaking of your husband.\r\nWhether you have duties to him or not I can\xe2\x80\x99t say; that is for your own\r\nmind and heart to determine. But isn\xe2\x80\x99t it true that your health has a\r\ngraver importance than if you yourself only were concerned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94you have understood me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it your duty to remember at every moment that your thoughts,\r\nyour actions, may affect another life\xe2\x80\x94that by heedlessness, by\r\nabandoning yourself to despair, you may be the cause of suffering it\r\nwas in your power to avert?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerself strongly moved, Rhoda had never spoken so impressively, had\r\nnever given counsel of such earnest significance. She felt her power in\r\nquite a new way, without touch of vanity, without posing or any trivial\r\nself-consciousness. When she least expected it an opportunity had come\r\nfor exerting the moral influence on which she prided herself, and which\r\nshe hoped to make the ennobling element of her life. All the better\r\nthat the case was one calling for courage, for contempt of vulgar\r\nreticences; the combative soul in her became stronger when faced by\r\nsuch conditions. Seeing that her words were not in vain, she came\r\nnearer to Monica and spoke yet more kindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you encourage that fear of your life coming to an end?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s more a hope than a fear\xe2\x80\x94at most times. I can see nothing before\r\nme. I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s morbid. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t yourself that speaks, but your trouble. You\r\nare young and strong, and in a year\xe2\x80\x99s time very much of this\r\nunhappiness will have passed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have felt it like a certainty\xe2\x80\x94as if it had been foretold to\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94ever since I knew\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it very likely that young wives have often the same dread. It\r\nis physical, Monica, and in your case there is so little relief from\r\ndark brooding. But again you must think of your responsibility. You\r\nwill live, because the poor little life will need your care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica turned her head away and moaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not love my child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you will. And that love, that duty, is the life to which you must\r\nlook forward. You have suffered a great deal, but after such sorrow as\r\nyours there comes quietness and resignation. Nature will help you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, if you could give me some of _your_ strength! I have never been\r\nable to look at life as you do. I should never have married him if I\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t been tempted by the thoughts of living easily\xe2\x80\x94and I feared\r\nso\xe2\x80\x94that I might always be alone\xe2\x80\x94My sisters are so miserable; it\r\nterrified me to think of struggling on through life as they do\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mistake was in looking only at the weak women. You had other\r\nexamples before you\xe2\x80\x94girls like Miss Vesper and Miss Haven, who live\r\nbravely and work hard and are proud of their place in the world. But\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s idle to talk of the past, and just as foolish to speak as if you\r\nwere sorrowing without hope. How old are you, Monica?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo-and-twenty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am two-and-thirty\xe2\x80\x94and I don\xe2\x80\x99t call myself old. When you have\r\nreached my age I prophesy you will smile at your despair of ten years\r\nago. At your age one talks so readily of \xe2\x80\x9cwrecked life\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9chopeless\r\nfuture,\xe2\x80\x9d and all that kind of thing. My dear girl, you may live to be\r\none of the most contented and most useful women in England. Your life\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t wrecked at all\xe2\x80\x94nonsense! You have gone through a storm, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntrue; but more likely than not you will be all the better for it. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntalk or think about _sins_; simply make up your mind that you won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nbeaten by trials and hardships. There cannot\xe2\x80\x94can there?\xe2\x80\x94be the least\r\ndoubt as to how you ought to live through these next coming months.\r\nYour duty is perfectly clear. Strengthen yourself in body and mind. You\r\n_have_ a mind, which is more than can be said of a great many women.\r\nThink bravely and nobly of yourself! Say to yourself: This and that it\r\nis in me to do, and I will do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonica bent suddenly forward and took one of her friend\xe2\x80\x99s hands, and\r\nclung to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you could say something that would help me. You have a way of\r\nspeaking. But it isn\xe2\x80\x99t only now. I shall be so far away, and so lonely,\r\nall through the dark winter. Will you write to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGladly. And tell you all we are doing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda\xe2\x80\x99s voice sank for a moment; her eyes wandered; but she recovered\r\nthe air of confidence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe seemed to have lost you; but before long you will be one of us\r\nagain. I mean, you will be one of the women who are fighting in woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncause. You will prove by your life that we can be responsible human\r\nbeings\xe2\x80\x94trustworthy, conscious of purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me\xe2\x80\x94do you think it right for me to live with my husband when I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t even regard him as a friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that I dare not counsel you. If you _can_ think of him as a friend,\r\nin time to come, surely it will be better. But here you must guide\r\nyourself. You seem to have made a very sensible arrangement, and before\r\nlong you will see many things more clearly. Try to recover\r\nhealth\xe2\x80\x94health; that is what you need. Drink in the air of the Severn\r\nSea; it will be a cordial to you after this stifling London. Next\r\nsummer I shall\xe2\x80\x94I hope I shall be at Cheddar, and then I shall come\r\nover to Clevedon\xe2\x80\x94and we shall laugh and talk as if we had never known\r\na care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, if that time were come! But you have done me good. I shall try\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mustn\xe2\x80\x99t forget,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda, without looking at her, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I owe you\r\nthanks. You have done what you felt was right in spite of all it cost\r\nyou; and you have very greatly relieved my mind. Of course it is all a\r\nsecret between us. If I make it understood that a doubt is no longer\r\ntroubling me I shall never say how it was removed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow I wish I had come before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor your own sake, if I have really helped you, I wish you had. But as\r\nfor anything else\xe2\x80\x94it is much better as it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Rhoda stood with erect head, smiling her smile of liberty. Monica\r\ndid not dare to ask any question. She moved up to her friend, holding\r\nout both hands timidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill next summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey embraced, and kissed each other, Monica, when she had withdrawn\r\nher hot lips, again murmuring words of gratitude. Then in silence they\r\nwent together to the house-door, and in silence parted.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX\r\n\r\nRETREAT WITH HONOUR\r\n\r\n\r\nAlighting, on his return to London, at the Savoy Hotel, Barfoot\r\ninsensibly prolonged his stay there. For the present he had no need of\r\na more private dwelling; he could not see more than a few days ahead;\r\nhis next decisive step was as uncertain as it had been during the first\r\nfew months after his coming back from the East.\r\n\r\nMeantime, he led a sufficiently agreeable life. The Brissendens were\r\nnot in town, but his growing intimacy with that family had extended his\r\nsocial outlook, and in a direction correspondent with the change in his\r\nown circumstances. He was making friends in the world with which he had\r\na natural affinity; that of wealthy and cultured people who seek no\r\nprominence, who shrink from contact with the circles known as \xe2\x80\x9csmart,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwho possess their souls in quiet freedom. It is a small class,\r\nespecially distinguished by the charm of its women. Everard had not\r\nadapted himself without difficulty to this new atmosphere; from the\r\nfirst he recognized its soothing and bracing quality, but his\r\nexperiences had accustomed him to an air more rudely vigorous; it was\r\nonly after those weeks spent abroad in frequent intercourse with the\r\nBrissendens that he came to understand the full extent of his sympathy\r\nwith the social principles these men and women represented.\r\n\r\nIn the houses where his welcome was now assured he met some three or\r\nfour women among whom it would have been difficult to assign the\r\nprecedence for grace of manner and of mind. These persons were not in\r\ndeclared revolt against the order of things, religious, ethical, or\r\nsocial; that is to say, they did not think it worthwhile to identify\r\nthemselves with any \xe2\x80\x9cmovement\xe2\x80\x9d; they were content with the unopposed\r\nright of liberal criticism. They lived placidly; refraining from much\r\nthat the larger world enjoined, but never aggressive. Everard admired\r\nthem with increasing fervour. With one exception they were married, and\r\nsuitably married; that member of the charming group who kept her maiden\r\nfreedom was Agnes Brissenden, and it seemed to Barfoot that, if\r\npreference were at all justified, Agnes should receive the palm. His\r\nview of her had greatly changed since the early days of their\r\nacquaintance; in fact, he perceived that till of late he had not known\r\nher at all. His quick assumption that Agnes was at his disposal if he\r\nchose to woo her had been mere fatuity; he misread her perfect\r\nsimplicity of demeanour, the unconstraint of her intellectual\r\nsympathies. What might now be her personal attitude to him he felt\r\naltogether uncertain, and the result was a genuine humility such as he\r\nhad never known. Nor was it Agnes only that subdued his masculine\r\nself-assertiveness; her sisters in grace had scarcely less dominion\r\nover him; and at times, as he sat conversing in one of these\r\ndrawing-rooms, he broke off to marvel at himself, to appreciate the\r\nperfection of his own suavity, the vast advance he had been making in\r\npolished humanism.\r\n\r\nTowards the end of November he learnt that the Brissendens were at\r\ntheir town house, and a week later he received an invitation to dine\r\nwith them.\r\n\r\nOver his luncheon at the hotel Everard reflected with some gravity,\r\nfor, if he were not mistaken, the hour had come when he must make up\r\nhis mind on a point too long in suspense. What was Rhoda Nunn doing? He\r\nhad heard nothing whatever of her. His cousin Mary wrote to him, whilst\r\nhe was at Ostend, in a kind and friendly tone, informing him that his\r\nsimple assurance with regard to a certain disagreeable matter was all\r\nshe had desired, and hoping that he would come and see her as usual\r\nwhen he found himself in London. But he had kept away from the house in\r\nQueen\xe2\x80\x99s Road, and it was probable that Mary did not even know his\r\naddress. As the result of meditation he went to his sitting-room, and\r\nwith an air of reluctance sat down to write a letter. It was a request\r\nthat Mary would let him see her somewhere or other\xe2\x80\x94not at her house.\r\nCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they have a talk at the place in Great Portland Street, when\r\nno one else was there?\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot answered with brief assent. If he liked to come to Great\r\nPortland Street at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock on Saturday she would be awaiting him.\r\n\r\nOn arriving, he inspected the rooms with curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have often wished to come here, Mary. Show me over the premises,\r\nwill you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was your purpose\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not altogether. But you know how your work interests me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary complied, and freely answered his various questions. Then they sat\r\ndown on hard chairs by the fire, and Everard, leaning forward as if to\r\nwarm his hands, lost no more time in coming to the point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to hear about Miss Nunn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo hear about her? Pray, what do you wish to hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very glad of that. Does she ever speak of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me see\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t think she has referred to you lately.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard looked up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let us play a comedy, Mary. I want to talk very seriously. Shall\r\nI tell you what happened when I went to Seascale?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you went to Seascale, did you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you know that?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, unable to decide the question from\r\nhis cousin\xe2\x80\x99s face, which was quite friendly, but inscrutable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou went when Miss Nunn was there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course. You must have known I was going, when I asked you for her\r\nSeascale address.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did happen? I shall be glad to hear\xe2\x80\x94if you feel at liberty\r\nto tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a pause, Everard began the narrative. But he did not see fit to\r\ngive it with all the detail which Mary had learnt from her friend. He\r\nspoke of the excursion to Wastwater, and of the subsequent meeting on\r\nthe shore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe end of it was that Miss Nunn consented to marry me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe consented?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat comes as a surprise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we arranged everything. Rhoda was to stay till the fifteen days\r\nwere over, and the marriage would have been there. But then arrived\r\nyour letter, and we quarrelled about it. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t disposed to beg and\r\npray for justice. I told Rhoda that her wish for evidence was an\r\ninsult, that I would take no step to understand Mrs. Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbehaviour. Rhoda was illogical, I think. She did not refuse to take my\r\nword, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t marry me until the thing was cleared up. I told\r\nher that she must investigate it for herself, and so we parted in no\r\nvery good temper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Barfoot smiled and mused. Her duty, she now felt convinced, was to\r\nabstain from any sort of meddling. These two people must settle their\r\naffairs as they chose. To interfere was to incur an enormous\r\nresponsibility. For what she had already done in that way Mary reproved\r\nherself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I want to ask you a plain question,\xe2\x80\x9d Everard resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat letter\r\nyou wrote to me at Ostend\xe2\x80\x94did it represent Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s mind as well as\r\nyour own?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quite impossible for me to say. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, perhaps that is a satisfactory answer. It implies, no doubt,\r\nthat she was still resolved not to concede the point on which I\r\ninsisted. But since then? Has she come to a decision?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was necessary to prevaricate. Mary knew of the interview between\r\nMiss Nunn and Mrs. Widdowson, knew its result; but she would not hint\r\nat this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no means of judging how she regards you, Everard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is possible she even thinks me a liar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understood you to say that she never refused to believe you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made a movement of impatience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlainly\xe2\x80\x94you will tell me nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing to tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I suppose I must see Rhoda. Perhaps she will refuse to admit me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say. But if she does her meaning would be unmistakable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCousin Mary\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he looked at her and laughed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI think you will be very\r\nglad if she _does_ refuse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe seemed about to reply with some pleasantry, but checked herself,\r\nand spoke in a serious voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I have no such feeling. Whatever you both agree upon will satisfy\r\nme. So come by all means if you wish. I can have nothing to do with it.\r\nYou had better write and ask her if she will see you, I should think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarfoot rose from his seat, and Mary was glad to be released so quickly\r\nfrom a disagreeable situation. For her own part she had no need to put\r\nindiscreet questions; Everard\xe2\x80\x99s manner acquainted her quite\r\nsufficiently with what was going on in his thoughts. However, he had\r\nstill something to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think I have behaved rather badly\xe2\x80\x94let us say, harshly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not so foolish as to form any judgment in such a case, cousin\r\nEverard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeaking as a woman, should you say that Rhoda had reason on her\r\nside\xe2\x80\x94in the first instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary replied, with reluctance, but deliberately, \xe2\x80\x9cthat she\r\nwas not unreasonable in wishing to postpone her marriage until she knew\r\nwhat was to be the result of Mrs. Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s indiscreet behaviour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, perhaps she was not,\xe2\x80\x9d Everard admitted thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what _has_ been the result?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only know that Mrs. Widdowson has left London and gone to live at a\r\nhouse her husband has taken somewhere in the country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m relieved to hear that. By-the-bye, the little lady\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cindiscreet\r\nbehaviour\xe2\x80\x9d is as much a mystery to me as ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary replied with an air of indifference.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, let us take it for granted that I was rather harsh with\r\nRhoda. But suppose she still meets me with the remark that things are\r\njust as they were\xe2\x80\x94that nothing has been explained?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t discuss your relations with Miss Nunn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever, you defend her original action. Be so good as to admit that I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t go to Mrs. Widdowson and request her to publish a statement that\r\nI have never\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall admit nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Miss Barfoot rather tartly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave advised you to see Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x94if she is willing. And there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnothing more to be said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. I will write to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n* * *\r\n\r\nHe did so, in the fewest possible words, and received an answer of\r\nequal brevity. In accordance with permission granted, on the Monday\r\nevening he found himself once more in his cousin\xe2\x80\x99s drawing-room,\r\nsitting alone, waiting Miss Nunn\xe2\x80\x99s appearance. He wondered how she\r\nwould present herself, in what costume. Her garb proved to be a plain\r\ndress of blue serge, certainly not calculated for effect; but his eye\r\nat once distinguished the fact that she had arranged her hair as she\r\nwore it when he first knew her, a fashion subsequently abandoned for\r\none that he thought more becoming.\r\n\r\nThey shook hands. Externally Barfoot was the more agitated, and his\r\nembarrassment appeared in the awkward words with which he began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had made up my mind never to come until you let me know that I was\r\ntried and acquitted But after all it is better to have reason on one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch better,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Rhoda, with a smile which emphasized her\r\nambiguity.\r\n\r\nShe sat down, and he followed her example. Their relative positions\r\ncalled to mind many a conversation they had held in this room.\r\nBarfoot\xe2\x80\x94he wore evening dress\xe2\x80\x94settled in the comfortable chair as\r\nthough he were an ordinary guest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you would never have written to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered quietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you are too proud, or because the mystery is still a mystery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no longer any mystery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard made a movement of surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed? You have discovered what it all meant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know what it all meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you gratify my not unnatural curiosity?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can say nothing about it, except that I know how the\r\nmisunderstanding arose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda was betraying the effort it had cost her to seem so\r\nself-possessed when she entered. Her colour had deepened, and she spoke\r\nhurriedly, unevenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it didn\xe2\x80\x99t occur to you that it would be a kindness, not\r\ninconsistent with your dignity, to make me in some way acquainted with\r\nthis fact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel no uneasiness on your account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSplendidly frank, as of old. You really didn\xe2\x80\x99t care in the least how\r\nmuch I suffered?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou misunderstand me. I felt sure that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t suffer at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I see. You imagined me calm in the assurance that I should some\r\nday be justified.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had every reason for imagining it,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Rhoda. \xe2\x80\x9cOtherwise, you\r\nwould have given some sign.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course he had deeply offended her by his persistent silence. He had\r\nintended to do so first of all; and afterwards\xe2\x80\x94had thought it might be\r\nas well. Now that he had got over the difficulty of the meeting he\r\nenjoyed his sense of security. How the interview would end he know not;\r\nbut on his side there would be nothing hasty, unconsidered, merely\r\nemotional. Had Rhoda any new revelation of personality within her\r\nresources?\xe2\x80\x94that was the question. If so, he would be pleased to\r\nobserve it. If not\xe2\x80\x94why, it was only the end to which he had long ago\r\nlooked forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not for me to give any sign,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet you have said that it is well to have reason on one\xe2\x80\x99s side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPerhaps a softer note allowed itself to be detected in these words. In\r\nany case, they were not plainly ironical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmit, then, that an approach was due from me. I have made it. I am\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda said nothing. Yet she had not an air of expectancy. Her eye was\r\ngrave, rather sad, as though for the moment she had forgotten what was\r\nat issue, and had lost herself in remoter thought. Regarding her,\r\nEverard felt a nobility in her countenance which amply justified all he\r\nhad ever felt and said. But was there anything more\xe2\x80\x94any new power?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo we go back,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cto our day at Wastwater. The perfect\r\nday\xe2\x80\x94wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never wish to forget it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda reflectively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we stand as when we quitted each other that night\xe2\x80\x94do we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe glanced at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what is the difference?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe waited some seconds, and repeated the question before Rhoda answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are conscious of no difference?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonths have elapsed. We are different because we are older. But you\r\nspeak as if you were conscious of some greater change.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you are changed noticeably. I thought I knew you; perhaps I did.\r\nNow I should have to learn you all over again. It is difficult, you\r\nsee, for me to keep pace with you. Your opportunities are so much\r\nwider.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was puzzling. Did it signify mere jealousy, or a profounder view\r\nof things? Her voice had something even of pathos, as though she\r\nuttered a simple thought, without caustic intention.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI try not to waste my life,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cI have made new\r\nacquaintances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell me about them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me first about yourself. You say you would never have written to\r\nme. That means, I think, that you never loved me. When you found that I\r\nhad been wrongly suspected\xe2\x80\x94and you suspected me yourself, say what you\r\nwill\xe2\x80\x94if you had loved me, you would have asked forgiveness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a like reason for doubting _your_ love. If you had loved me you\r\ncould never have waited so long without trying to remove the obstacle\r\nthat was between us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was you who put the obstacle there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Everard, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. An unlucky chance did that. Or a lucky one. Who knows?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe began to think: If this woman had enjoyed the social advantages to\r\nwhich Agnes Brissenden and those others were doubtless indebted for so\r\nmuch of their charm, would she not have been their equal, or more? For\r\nthe first time he compassionated Rhoda. She was brave, and\r\ncircumstances had not been kind to her. At this moment, was she not\r\ncontending with herself? Was not her honesty, her dignity, struggling\r\nagainst the impulses of her heart? Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s love had been worth more\r\nthan his, and it would be her one love in life. A fatuous reflection,\r\nperhaps; yet every moment\xe2\x80\x99s observation seemed to confirm it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s the question which we must decide. If\r\nyou incline to think that the chance was fortunate\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe would not speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must know each other\xe2\x80\x99s mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that is so difficult!\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda murmured, just raising her hand and\r\nletting it fall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, unless we give each other help. Let us imagine ourselves back at\r\nSeascale, down by the waves. (How cold and grim it must be there\r\nto-night!) I repeat what I said then: Rhoda, will you marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked fixedly at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do the words matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was not what you said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe watched the agitation of her features, until his gaze seemed to\r\ncompel her to move. She stepped towards the fireplace, and moved a\r\nlittle screen that stood too near the fender.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you want me to repeat exactly what I said?\xe2\x80\x9d Everard asked,\r\nrising and following her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak of the \xe2\x80\x9cperfect day.\xe2\x80\x9d Didn\xe2\x80\x99t the day\xe2\x80\x99s perfection end before\r\nthere was any word of marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at her with surprise. She had spoken without turning her face\r\ntowards him; it was visible now only by the glow of the fire. Yes, what\r\nshe said was true, but a truth which he had neither expected nor\r\ndesired to hear. Had the new revelation prepared itself?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho first used the word, Rhoda?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was silence. Rhoda stood unmoving, the fire\xe2\x80\x99s glow upon her face,\r\nand Barfoot watched her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at length, \xe2\x80\x9cI was not quite serious when I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned sharply upon him, a flash of indignation in her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot quite serious? Yes, I have thought that. And were you quite\r\nserious in _anything_ you said?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI loved you,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered curtly, answering her steady look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet wanted to see whether\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could not finish the sentence; her throat quivered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI loved you, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. And I believe I still love you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRhoda turned to the fire again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, moving a step nearer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are \xe2\x80\x9cnot quite serious\xe2\x80\x9d.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have asked you twice. I ask for the third time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t marry you with the forms of marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d Rhoda answered in an\r\nabrupt, harsh tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it is you who play with a serious matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said we had both changed. I see now that our \xe2\x80\x9cperfect day\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nmarred by my weakness at the end. If you wish to go back in imagination\r\nto that summer night, restore everything, only let _me_ be what I now\r\nam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverard shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible. It must be then or now for both of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLegal marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, glancing at him, \xe2\x80\x9chas acquired some new\r\nsanction for you since then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the whole, perhaps it has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNaturally. But I shall never marry, so we will speak no more of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs if finally dismissing the subject she walked to the opposite side of\r\nthe hearth, and there turned towards her companion with a cold smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn other words, then, you have ceased to love me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I no longer love you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet, if I had been willing to revive that fantastic idealism\xe2\x80\x94as you\r\nthought it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe interrupted him sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _was_ it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a kind of idealism undoubtedly. I was so bent on making sure that\r\nyou loved me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all, the perfection of our day was half make-believe. You never\r\nloved me with entire sincerity. And you will never love any woman\xe2\x80\x94even\r\nas well as you loved me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my soul, I believe it, Rhoda. And even now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd even now it is just possible for us to say good-bye with something\r\nlike friendliness. But not if you talk longer. Don\xe2\x80\x99t let us spoil it;\r\nthings are so straight\xe2\x80\x94and clear\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA threatened sob made her break off, but she recovered herself and\r\noffered him her hand.\r\n\r\n* * *\r\n\r\nHe walked all the way back to his hotel, and the cold, clammy night\r\nrestored his equanimity. A fortnight later, sending a Christmas\r\npresent, with greetings, to Mr. and Mrs. Micklethwaite, he wrote thus\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am about to do my duty\xe2\x80\x94as you put it\xe2\x80\x94that is, to marry. The name\r\nof my future wife is Miss Agnes Brissenden. It will be in March, I\r\nthink. But I shall see you before then, and give you a fuller account\r\nof myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI\r\n\r\nA NEW BEGINNING\r\n\r\n\r\nWiddowson tried two or three lodgings; he settled at length in a small\r\nhouse at Hampstead; occupying two plain rooms. Here, at long intervals,\r\nhis friend Newdick came to see him, but no one else. He had brought\r\nwith him a selection of solid books from his library, and over these\r\nthe greater part of each day was spent. Not that he studied with any\r\nzeal; reading, and of a kind that demanded close attention, was his\r\nonly resource against melancholia; he knew not how else to occupy\r\nhimself. Adam Smith\xe2\x80\x99s classical work, perused with laborious\r\nthoroughness, gave him employment for a couple of months; subsequently\r\nhe plodded through all the volumes of Hallam.\r\n\r\nHis landlady, and the neighbours who were at leisure to observe him\r\nwhen he went out for his two hours\xe2\x80\x99 walk in the afternoon, took him for\r\nan old gentleman of sixty-five or so. He no longer held himself\r\nupright, and when out of doors seldom raised his eyes from the ground;\r\ngrey streaks had begun to brindle his hair; his face grew yellower and\r\nmore deeply furrowed. Of his personal appearance, even of cleanliness,\r\nhe became neglectful, and occasionally it happened that he lay in bed\r\nall through the morning, reading, dozing, or in a state of mental\r\nvacuity.\r\n\r\nIt was long since he had seen his relative, the sprightly widow; but he\r\nhad heard from her. On the point of leaving England for her summer\r\nholiday, Mrs. Luke sent him a few lines, urging him, in the language of\r\nthe world, to live more sensibly, and let his wife \xe2\x80\x9chave her head\xe2\x80\x9d now\r\nand then; it would be better for both of them. Then followed the time\r\nof woe, and for many weeks he gave no thought to Mrs. Luke. But close\r\nupon the end of the year he received one day a certain society journal,\r\naddressed in a hand he knew to the house at Herne Hill. In it was\r\ndiscoverable, marked with a red pencil, the following paragraph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmong the English who this year elected to take their repose and\r\nrecreation at Trouville there was no more brilliant figure than Mrs.\r\nLuke Widdowson. This lady is well known in the _monde_ where one never\r\n_s\xe2\x80\x99ennuie_; where smart people are gathered together, there is the\r\ncharming widow sure to be seen. We are able to announce that, before\r\nleaving Trouville, Mrs. Widdowson had consented to a private engagement\r\nwith Capt. William Horrocks\xe2\x80\x94no other, indeed, than \xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Bill,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nuniversal favourite, so beloved by hostesses as a sure dancing man. By\r\nthe lamented death of his father, this best of good fellows has now\r\nbecome Sir William, and we understand that his marriage will be\r\ncelebrated after the proper delays. Our congratulations!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSubsequently arrived a newspaper with an account of the marriage. Mrs.\r\nLuke was now Lady Horrocks: she had the title desired of her heart.\r\n\r\nAnother two months went by, and there came a letter\xe2\x80\x94re-addressed, like\r\nthe other communications, at the post office\xe2\x80\x94in which the baronet\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife declared herself anxious to hear of her friends. She found they\r\nhad left Herne Hill; if this letter reached him, would not Edmund come\r\nand see her at her house in Wimpole Street?\r\n\r\nMisery of solitude, desire for a woman\xe2\x80\x99s sympathy and counsel, impelled\r\nhim to use this opportunity, little as it seemed to promise. He went to\r\nWimpole Street and had a very long private talk with Lady Horrocks,\r\nwho, in some way he could not understand, had changed from her old\r\nself. She began frivolously, but in rather a dull, make-believe way;\r\nand when she heard that Widdowson had parted from his wife, when a few\r\nvague, miserable words had suggested the domestic drama so familiar to\r\nher observation, she at once grew quiet, sober, sympathetic, as if\r\nreally glad to have something serious to talk about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow look here, Edmund. Tell the whole story from the first. You\xe2\x80\x99re the\r\nsort of man to make awful blunders in such a case as this. Just tell me\r\nall about it. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a bad sort, you know, and I have troubles of my\r\nown\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind telling you so much. Women make fools of\r\nthemselves\xe2\x80\x94well, never mind. Just tell me about the little girl, and\r\nsee if we can\xe2\x80\x99t square things somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had a struggle with himself, but at length narrated everything,\r\noften interrupted by shrewd questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one writes to you?\xe2\x80\x9d the listener finally inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am expecting to hear from them,\xe2\x80\x9d was Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s answer, as he sat\r\nin the usual position, head hanging forward and hands clasped between\r\nhis knees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo hear what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I shall be sent for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSent for? To make it up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is going to give birth to a child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Horrocks nodded twice thoughtfully, and with a faint smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you find this out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have known it long enough. Her sister Virginia told me before they\r\nwent away. I had a suspicion all at once, and I forced her to tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if you are sent for shall you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWiddowson seemed to mutter an affirmative, and added,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall hear what she has to tell me, as she promised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it\xe2\x80\x94is it possible\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lady\xe2\x80\x99s question remained incomplete. Widdowson, though he\r\nunderstood it, vouchsafed no direct answer. Intense suffering was\r\nmanifest in his face, and at length he spoke vehemently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever she tells me\xe2\x80\x94how can I believe it? When once a woman has\r\nlied how can she ever again be believed? I can\xe2\x80\x99t be sure of anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll that fibbing,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Lady Horrocks, \xe2\x80\x9chas an unpleasant look. No\r\ndenying it. She got entangled somehow. But I think you had better\r\nbelieve that she pulled up just in time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no love for her left,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on in a despairing voice. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nall perished in those frightful days. I tried hard to think that I\r\nstill loved her. I kept writing letters\xe2\x80\x94but they meant nothing\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nthey only meant that I was driven half crazy by wretchedness. I had\r\nrather we lived on as we have been doing. It\xe2\x80\x99s miserable enough for me,\r\nGod knows; but it would be worse to try and behave to her as if I could\r\nforget everything. I know her explanation won\xe2\x80\x99t satisfy me. Whatever it\r\nis I shall still suspect her. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that the child is mine. It\r\nmay be. Perhaps as it grows up there will be a likeness to help me to\r\nmake sure. But what a life! Every paltry trifle will make me uneasy;\r\nand if I discovered any fresh deceit I should do something terrible.\r\nYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know how near I was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shuddered and hid his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Othello business won\xe2\x80\x99t do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Horrocks not unkindly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have gone on together, of course; you had to part for a time.\r\nWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s all over; take it as something that couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be helped.\r\nYou were behaving absurdly, you know; I told you plainly; I guessed\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d be trouble. You oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to have married at all, that\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nfact; it would be better for most of us if we kept out of it. Some\r\nmarry for a good reason, some for a bad, and mostly it all comes to the\r\nsame in the end. But there, never mind. Pull yourself together, dear\r\nboy. It\xe2\x80\x99s all nonsense about not caring for her. Of course you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\neating your heart out for want of her. And I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I think:\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s very likely Monica was pulled up just in time by discovering\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nunderstand?\xe2\x80\x94that she was more your wife than any one else\xe2\x80\x99s. Something\r\ntells me that\xe2\x80\x99s how it was. Just try to look at it in that way. If the\r\nchild lives she\xe2\x80\x99ll be different. She has sowed her wild oats\xe2\x80\x94why\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t a woman as well as a man? Go down to Clevedon and forgive\r\nher. You\xe2\x80\x99re an honest man, and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t every woman\xe2\x80\x94never mind. I\r\ncould tell you stories about people\xe2\x80\x94but you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care to hear\r\nthem. Just take things with a laugh\xe2\x80\x94we _all_ have to. Life\xe2\x80\x99s as you\r\ntake it: all gloom or moderately shiny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith much more to the same solacing effect. For the time Widdowson was\r\nperchance a trifle comforted; at all events, he went away with a sense\r\nof gratitude to Lady Horrocks. And when he had left the house he\r\nremembered that not even a civil formality with regard to Sir William\r\nhad fallen from his lips. But Sir William\xe2\x80\x99s wife, for whatever reason,\r\nhad also not once mentioned the baronet\xe2\x80\x99s name.\r\n\r\n* * *\r\n\r\nOnly a few days passed before Widdowson received the summons he was\r\nexpecting. It came in the form of a telegram, bidding him hasten to his\r\nwife; not a word of news added. At the time of its arrival he was\r\ntaking his afternoon walk; this delay made it doubtful whether he could\r\nget to Paddington by six-twenty, the last train which would enable him\r\nto reach Clevedon that night. He managed it, with only two or three\r\nminutes to spare.\r\n\r\nNot till he was seated in the railway carriage could he fix his\r\nthoughts on the end of the journey. An inexpressible repugnance then\r\naffected him; he would have welcomed any disaster to the train, any\r\ninjury which might prevent his going to Monica at such a time. Often,\r\nin anticipation, the event which was now come to pass had confused and\r\ndarkened his mind; he loathed the thought of it. If the child, perhaps\r\nalready born, were in truth his, it must be very long before he could\r\nregard it with a shadow of paternal interest; uncertainty, to which he\r\nwas condemned, would in all likelihood make it an object of aversion to\r\nhim as long as he lived.\r\n\r\nHe was at Bristol by a quarter past nine, and had to change for a slow\r\ntrain, which by ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock brought him to Yatton, the little junction\r\nfor Clevedon. It was a fine starry night, but extremely cold. For the\r\nfew minutes of detention he walked restlessly about the platform. His\r\nchief emotion was now a fear lest all might not go well with Monica.\r\nWhether he could believe what she had to tell him or not, it would be\r\nworse if she were to die before he could hear her exculpation. The\r\nanguish of remorse would seize upon him.\r\n\r\nAlone in his compartment, he did not sit down, but stamped backwards\r\nand forwards on the floor, and before the train stopped he jumped out.\r\nNo cab was procurable; he left his bag at the station, and hastened\r\nwith all speed in the direction that he remembered. But very soon the\r\ncrossways had confused him. As he met no one whom he could ask to\r\ndirect him, he had to knock at a door. Streaming with perspiration, he\r\ncame at length within sight of his own house. A church clock was\r\nstriking eleven.\r\n\r\nAlice and Virginia were both standing in the hall when the door was\r\nopened; they beckoned him into a room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it over?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, staring from one to the other with his dazzled\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt four this afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Alice, scarce able to articulate. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nlittle girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe had to have chloroform,\xe2\x80\x9d said Virginia, who looked a miserable,\r\nlifeless object, and shook like one in an ague.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all\xe2\x80\x99s well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe think so\xe2\x80\x94we hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d they stammered together.\r\n\r\nAlice added that the doctor was to make another call to-night. They had\r\na good nurse. The infant seemed healthy, but was a very, very little\r\nmite, and had only made its voice heard for a few minutes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knows you sent for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And we have something to give you. You were to have this as soon\r\nas you arrived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Madden handed him a sealed envelope; then both the sisters drew\r\naway, as if fearing the result of what they had done. Widdowson just\r\nglanced at the unaddressed missive and put it into his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must have something to eat,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, wiping his forehead. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the\r\ndoctor comes I\xe2\x80\x99ll see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis visit took place while he was engaged on his supper. On coming\r\ndown from the patient the doctor gave him an assurance that things were\r\nprogressing \xe2\x80\x9cfairly well\xe2\x80\x9d; the morning, probably, would enable him to\r\nspeak with yet more confidence. Widdowson had another brief\r\nconversation with the sisters, then bade them good-night, and went to\r\nthe room that had been prepared for him. As he closed the door he heard\r\na thin, faint wail, and stood listening until it ceased; it came from a\r\nroom on the floor below.\r\n\r\nHaving brought himself with an effort to open the envelope he had\r\nreceived, he found several sheets of notepaper, one of them, remarked\r\nimmediately, in a man\xe2\x80\x99s writing. At this he first glanced, and the\r\nbeginning showed him that it was a love-letter written to Monica. He\r\nthrew it aside and took up the other sheets, which contained a long\r\ncommunication from his wife; it was dated two months ago. In it Monica\r\nrecounted to him, with scrupulous truthfulness, the whole story of her\r\nrelations with Bevis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only make this confession\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94so she concluded\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cfor the sake of the\r\npoor child that will soon be born. The child is yours, and ought not to\r\nsuffer because of what I did. The enclosed letter will prove this to\r\nyou, if anything can. For myself I ask nothing. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I shall\r\nlive. If I do I will consent to anything you propose. I only ask you to\r\nbehave without any pretence; if you cannot forgive me, do not make a\r\nshow of it. Say what your will is, and that shall be enough\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nHe did not go to bed that night. There was a fire in the room, and he\r\nkept it alight until daybreak, when he descended softly to the hall and\r\nlet himself out of the house.\r\n\r\nIn a fierce wind that swept from the north-west down the foaming\r\nChannel, he walked for an hour or two, careless whither the roads\r\ndirected him. All he desired was to be at a distance from that house,\r\nwith its hideous silence and the faint cry that could scarcely be\r\ncalled a sound. The necessity of returning, of spending days there, was\r\nan oppression which held him like a nightmare.\r\n\r\nMonica\xe2\x80\x99s statement he neither believed nor disbelieved; he simply could\r\nnot make up his mind about it. She had lied to him so resolutely\r\nbefore; was she not capable of elaborate falsehood to save her\r\nreputation and protect her child? The letter from Bevis might have been\r\na result of conspiracy between them.\r\n\r\nThat Bevis was the man against whom his jealousy should have been\r\ndirected at first astounded him. By now he had come to a full\r\nperception of his stupidity in never entertaining such a thought. The\r\nrevelation was equivalent to a second offence just discovered; for he\r\nfound it impossible to ignore his long-cherished suspicion of Barfoot,\r\nand he even surmised the possibility of Monica\xe2\x80\x99s having listened to\r\nlove-making from that quarter previously to her intimacy with Bevis. He\r\nloathed the memory of his life since marriage; and as for pardoning his\r\nwife, he could as soon pardon and smile upon the author of that\r\naccursed letter from Bordeaux.\r\n\r\nBut go back to the house he must. By obeying his impulse, and\r\nstraightway returning to London, he might be the cause of a fatal turn\r\nin Monica\xe2\x80\x99s illness. Constraint of bare humanity would keep him here\r\nuntil his wife was out of danger. But he could not see her, and as soon\r\nas possible he must escape from such unendurable circumstances.\r\n\r\nRe-entering at half-past eight, he was met by Alice, who seemed to have\r\nslept as little as he himself had done. They went into the dining-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has been inquiring about you,\xe2\x80\x9d began Miss Madden timorously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot worse, I believe. But so very weak. She wishes me to ask you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis manner did not encourage the poor woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be obliged to tell her something. If I have nothing to say she\r\nwill fret herself into a dangerous state. She wants to know if you have\r\nread her letter, and if\xe2\x80\x94if you will see the child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWiddowson turned away and stood irresolute. He felt Miss Madden\xe2\x80\x99s hand\r\nupon his arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t refuse! Let me give her some comfort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the child she\xe2\x80\x99s anxious about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlice admitted it, looking into her brother-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s face with woeful\r\nappeal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay I will see it,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cand have it brought into some\r\nroom\xe2\x80\x94then say I _have_ seen it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMayn\xe2\x80\x99t I take her a word of forgiveness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, say I forgive her. She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t wish me to go to her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlice shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen say I forgive her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he directed so it was done; and in the course of the morning Miss\r\nMadden brought word to him that her sister had experienced great\r\nrelief. She was sleeping.\r\n\r\nBut the doctor thought it necessary to make two visits before\r\nnightfall, and late in the evening he came again. He explained to\r\nWiddowson that there were complications, not unlikely to be dangerous,\r\nand finally he suggested that, if the morrow brought no decided\r\nimprovement, a second medical man should be called in to consult. This\r\nconsultation was held. In the afternoon Virginia came weeping to her\r\nbrother-in-law, and told him that Monica was delirious. That night the\r\nwhole household watched. Another day was passed in the gravest anxiety,\r\nand at dusk the medical attendant no longer disguised his opinion that\r\nMrs. Widdowson was sinking. She became unconscious soon after, and in\r\nthe early morning breathed her last.\r\n\r\nWiddowson was in the room, and at the end sat by the bedside for an\r\nhour. But he did not look upon his wife\xe2\x80\x99s face. When it was told him\r\nthat she had ceased to breathe, he rose and went into his own chamber,\r\ndeath-pale, but tearless.\r\n\r\n* * *\r\n\r\nOn the day after the funeral\xe2\x80\x94Monica was buried in the cemetery, which\r\nis hard by the old church\xe2\x80\x94Widdowson and the elder sister had a long\r\nconversation in private. It related first of all to the motherless\r\nbaby. Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s desire was that Miss Madden should undertake the care\r\nof the child. She and Virginia might live wherever they preferred;\r\ntheir needs would be provided for. Alice had hardly dared to hope for\r\nsuch a proposal\xe2\x80\x94as it concerned the child, that is to say. Gladly she\r\naccepted it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there\xe2\x80\x99s something I must tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with embarrassed\r\nappeal in her wet eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Virginia wishes to go into an\r\ninstitution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWiddowson looked at her, not understanding; whereupon she broke into\r\ntears, and made known that her sister was such a slave to strong drink\r\nthat they both despaired of reformation unless by help of the measure\r\nshe had indicated. There were people, she had heard, who undertook the\r\ncare of inebriates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know that we are by no means penniless,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Alice. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\r\nvery well bear the expense. But will you assist us to find a suitable\r\nplace?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe promised to proceed at once in the matter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when she is cured,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Madden, \xe2\x80\x9cshe shall come and live\r\nwith me. And when baby is about two years old we will do what we have\r\nbeen purposing for a long time. We will open a school for young\r\nchildren, either here or at Weston. That will afford my poor sister\r\noccupation. Indeed, we shall both be better for the exertion of such an\r\nundertaking\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a wise thing, I have no doubt whatever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe large house was to be abandoned, and as much of the furniture as\r\nseemed needful transported to a smaller dwelling in another part of\r\nClevedon. For Alice resolved to stay here in spite of painful\r\nassociations. She loved the place, and looked forward with quiet joy to\r\nthe life that was prepared for her. Widdowson\xe2\x80\x99s books would go back to\r\nLondon; not to the Hampstead lodgings, however. Fearful of solitude, he\r\nproposed to his friend Newdick that they should live together, he, as a\r\nman of substance, bearing the larger share of the expense. And this\r\nplan also came into execution.\r\n\r\n* * *\r\n\r\nThree months went by, and on a day of summer, when the wooded hills and\r\ngreen lanes and rich meadows of Clevedon looked their best, when the\r\nChannel was still and blue, and the Welsh mountains loomed through a\r\nsunny haze, Rhoda Nunn came over from the Mendips to see Miss Madden.\r\nIt could not be a gladsome meeting, but Rhoda was bright and natural,\r\nand her talk as inspiriting as ever. She took the baby in her arms, and\r\nwalked about with it for a long time in the garden, often murmuring,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor little child! Dear little child!\xe2\x80\x9d There had been doubt whether it\r\nwould live, but the summer seemed to be fortifying its health. Alice,\r\nit was plain, had found her vocation; she looked better than at any\r\ntime since Rhoda had known her. Her complexion was losing its muddiness\r\nand spottiness; her step had become light and brisk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where is your sister?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Miss Nunn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStaying with friends at present. She will be back before long, I hope.\r\nAnd as soon as baby can walk we are going to think very seriously about\r\nthe school. You remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe school? You will really make the attempt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be so good for us both. Why, look,\xe2\x80\x9d she added laughingly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chere is one pupil growing for us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake a brave woman of her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rhoda kindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will try\xe2\x80\x94ah, we will try! And is your work as successful as ever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Rhoda. \xe2\x80\x9cWe flourish like the green bay-tree. We shall\r\nhave to take larger premises. By-the-bye, you must read the paper we\r\nare going to publish; the first number will be out in a month, though\r\nthe name isn\xe2\x80\x99t quite decided upon yet. Miss Barfoot was never in such\r\nhealth and spirit\xe2\x80\x94nor I myself. The world is moving!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhilst Miss Madden went into the house to prepare hospitalities, Rhoda,\r\nstill nursing, sat down on a garden bench. She gazed intently at those\r\ndiminutive features, which were quite placid and relaxing in soft\r\ndrowsiness. The dark, bright eye was Monica\xe2\x80\x99s. And as the baby sank\r\ninto sleep, Rhoda\xe2\x80\x99s vision grew dim; a sigh made her lips quiver, and\r\nonce more she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor little child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE ODD WOMEN ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 4313-0.txt or 4313-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/4/3/1/4313/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one\xe2\x80\x94the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
